@p11@p2These are the names of the B’nei Yisrael.@p3 Though [Scripture] has previously enumerated them during their lifetime by their names, it again enumerates them at their deaths. [This is] to show how dear they are [to God], because they are compared to the stars which He brings out and brings in by number and by their [individual] names; as it is said: “Who brings out their host by number, and calls each by its name.”<QC>
@p15@p2Yoseif [who] was [already] in Egypt.@p3 But, were not he and his sons included in the seventy? Then what does [Scripture] intend to teach us? Would we not know ourselves that he was in Egypt? But, [the purpose is] to let you know how righteous Yoseif was — the same Yoseif who tended his father’s sheep, is the same Yoseif who was in Egypt and he became king, [yet] he remained steadfast in his righteousness. @p17@p2And Prolific.@p3 They bore six children at one time. @p18@p2A new king came into power.@p3 Rav and Shmuel [disagreed as to the meaning of @hh<HEB>מֶלֶךְ חָדָשׁ@ee<ENG>]. One said, literally a new king while the other said, [he was actually the same king] but his edicts were new.<QC>
@d1Who did not know.@d2 He acted @ITas if@it he did not know [him]. @p110@p2Come let us deal wisely.@p3 Wherever @hh<HEB>הָבָה@ee<ENG> [appears in Scripture] it means “preparation and readiness” for a given matter; that is: “Prepare yourselves for this!” @d1Wisely with him.@d2 [@hh<HEB>לוֹ@ee<ENG> — @ITto him@it — refers] to the nation [of Israel]. “Let us wisely determine what to do to him.” Our Sages [however] explained [the singular @hh<HEB>לוֹ@ee<ENG>] as: “Let us deal shrewdly with the Savior of Israel (God) by bringing about their fate through water for He has already sworn that he will never again bring a flood upon the world.” (They [the Egyptians] did not realize that [His promise was] not to flood the entire world but He might flood one nation. [From an old Rashi text]). @d1Driving us from the land — @d2 against our will. @d1Shifrah.@d2 This is Yocheved, [and she was called @hh<HEB>שִׁפְרָה@ee<ENG>] because [@hh<HEB>שֶׁמְּשַׁפֶּרֶת@ee<ENG>] she would put the newborn into proper [physical] condition. @d1Puah.@d2 This is Miriam, [and she was called @hh<HEB>פּוּעָה@ee<ENG>] because she would make loud noises and speak and articulate to the newborn, as do women who pacify a child that is crying. @d1Let her live.@d2 [@hh<HEB>וָחָיָה@ee<ENG> being the same as] @hh<HEB>וְתִחְיֶה@ee<ENG> — “and she shall live.” @p117@p2And they kept the infant boys alive.@p3 [Meaning:] they provided water and food for them. Onkelos’ translation of the first [@hh<HEB>וַתְּחַיֶּיןָ@ee<ENG>] is @hh<HEB>וְקַיָּימָא@ee<ENG> — @ITand they [the women] preserved [the newborn];@it whereas the second time [that @hh<HEB>וַתְּחַיֶּיןָ@ee<ENG> appears — v. 18] [he translates it] @hh<HEB>וְקַיֵּימְתּוּן@ee<ENG> — @ITand you [women] have preserved.@it This is because, in Hebrew, this word and any form thereof is used to mean: “they” (fem.) did something as well as: “you” (fem. pl.) did something. E.g., “(@hh<HEB>וַתּאֹמַרְןָ@ee<ENG> — ) They said, “an Egyptian, etc.,” which is [fem. pl.] past tense as would be @hh<HEB>וַיֹּאמְרוּ@ee<ENG> — for masculine. [Another example:] “(@hh<HEB>וַתְּדַבֵּרְנָה@ee<ENG>) you [women] have spoken with your mouths” — the same as @hh<HEB>דִּבַּרְתֶּן@ee<ENG> corresponding to @hh<HEB>וַתְּדַבְּרוּ@ee<ENG> in the masculine. Similarly: (@hh<HEB>וַתְּחַלְּלֶנָה@ee<ENG>) “you [women] have maligned me to my nation” — which is in the past tense. The same as @hh<HEB>חִלַּלְתֶּן@ee<ENG> corresponding to @hh<HEB>וַתְּחַלְּלוּ@ee<ENG> in the masculine.<QC>
@p119@p2They know how to deliver.@p3 [Meaning:] they are as knowledgeable as midwives. Onkelos’ translation of @hh<HEB>מְיַלְּדוֹת@ee<ENG> (midwives) is @hh<HEB>חַיָיתָא @ee<ENG>. However, our Sages explained [@hh<HEB>חָיוֹת@ee<ENG> as “animals”] [i.e.] they may be compared to animals of the field who do not require midwives [when giving birth]. And where is it that they are compared to animals? “[Yehudah] is like a young lion,” “[Binyamin] is like a wolf that preys,” “[Yoseif] is like the first-born of his ox,” “[Naftali] is a gazelle-like messenger.” And [those of the Tribes] where no reference is made [to animals], Scripture includes them [in the comparison] [in the statement:] “And he blessed them.” It is also written: “What a lioness is your mother!” @p120@p2Elokim was good.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וַיֵּיטֶב@ee<ENG> means:] He did good to them. The following is the difference concerning a word whose root letters are two and it is prefixed by a @hh<HEB>וי"ו@ee<ENG> and a @hh<HEB>יו"ד@ee<ENG>: When it intends to convey the idea of causing someone else to do something, the @hh<HEB>יו"ד@ee<ENG> is vocalized with a @hh<HEB>צֵירֵי@ee<ENG> which is also called a @ITkometz koton.@it For example: “(@hh<HEB>וַיֵּיטֶב@ee<ENG>) God did good to the midwives.” “@hh<HEB>וַיֶּרֶב@ee<ENG> [the mourning] of the daughter of Yehudah” [meaning:] He caused great mourning. Similarly: “@hh<HEB>וַיֶּגֶל@ee<ENG> the remnant” [which is written] concerning Nevuzaraddan [meaning:] he caused the exile of the remnant. [Or:] “@hh<HEB>וַיֶּפֶן@ee<ENG> tail to tail” [meaning:] he caused the tails to turn to each other. All these examples have the meaning: he caused others to do something. But, when it speaks in terms of “he did” the @hh<HEB>יו"ד@ee<ENG> is vocalized with a @hh<HEB>חִירִק@ee<ENG>. For example: “@hh<HEB>וַיִּיטַב@ee<ENG> in his eyes” [meaning:] it was good [in his eyes]. Similarly: “@hh<HEB>וַיִּרֶב@ee<ENG> the nation” [meaning:] the nation increased; “@hh<HEB>וַיִּגֶל@ee<ENG> Yehudah” [meaning:] Yehudah was exiled; “(@hh<HEB>וַיִּפֶן@ee<ENG>) He looked all around” [meaning:] He turned here and there. Do not reject my [explanation] [because of the words] @hh<HEB>וַיֵלֶךְ, וַיֵשֶׁב, וַיֵרֶד וַיֵצֵא@ee<ENG>, because these [words] are not from the same type of verbs as those [above] for the @hh<HEB>יו"ד@ee<ENG> in these are part of the root — [in the words:] @hh<HEB>יָלֹךְ, יָשֹׁב, יָרֹד, יָצֹא@ee<ENG> the @hh<HEB>יו"ד@ee<ENG> is a third root letter. @d1Elokim was good to the midwives.@d2 What was this good [that was done to them]? @p121@p2[It was that] He gave them houses.@p3 Houses of the Priesthood and the Levites and Royalty which are referred to as “houses” (dynasties), [as is written:] “And he built the House of God and the house of the king;” the Priesthood and the Levites [descended] from Yocheved and Royalty from Miriam; as is cited in Maseches Sotah. @p122@p2All his people.@p3 Also upon them (the Egyptians) did he issue the decree. On the day that Moshe was born his astrologers told him, “Today, their savior was born, but we do not know whether he is from the Egyptians or the Jews, but we forsee that he is destined to be smitten through water.” Therefore, on that day, he (Pharaoh) issued a decree also regarding the Egyptians, as it is said: “@ITEvery@it son that will be born” and it does @ITnot@it say: “that will be born to the Hebrews.” They (the astrologers), however, did not know that he (Moshe) was destined to be smitten by the waters of Meriva. @p11@p2And married the daughter of Levi.@p3 He (Amram) had separated from her due to Pharaoh’s decree and he [now] took her back and married her a second time. She, too, became youthful again. [Actually] she was 130 years old, for she was born upon their arrival in Egypt between the walls (the entrance of the city). They then spent 210 years there (in Egypt). When they left, Moshe was 80 years old. Thus, when she became pregnant with him she was 130 [years old]. [Yet,] she is referred to as “the daughter of Levi.” @p12@p2She saw that he was good.@p3 When he was born the entire house was filled with light. @p13@p2When she could no longer hide him.@p3 Because the Egyptians calculated [the time] from the day that he (Amram) took her back, but she gave birth to him after six months and one day. [This is possible] because one who gives birth in the seventh month can give birth [even if the 7th month is] incomplete. [But] they (the Egyptians) inquired after her [only] at the end of the ninth month. @d1A Papyrus box.@d2 It is called @hh<HEB>גְמִי@ee<ENG> in Mishnaic Hebrew, and @ITjonc@it in Old French. It is a pliable substance which can withstand soft or hard [pressure]. @d1With clay and tar.@d2 Tar on the outside and clay on the inside, so that the righteous [child] should not have to smell the bad odor of tar. @d1And placed it in the reeds.@d2 @hh<HEB>סוּף@ee<ENG> means @ITmarshland@it [on the side of a river], @ITrosel@it (reeds) in Old French, similar to “reeds and marshes (@hh<HEB>סוּף@ee<ENG>) shall wilt.” @p15@p2To bathe by the river.@p3 You must transpose [words in] the verse and [then] explain it [as follows]: “Pharaoh’s daughter went down to the river in order to bathe in it.” @d1Along the river’s edge.@d2 [Meaning:] @ITnear@it the river, as in: “See Yoav’s property is next to mine (@hh<HEB>עַל יָדִי@ee<ENG>).” It has the meaning of the actual hand because a person’s hand is next to him. Our Sages explained that @hh<HEB>הוֹלְכוֹת@ee<ENG> — walking refers to dying, as in: “Behold, I am going (@hh<HEB>הוֹלֵךְ@ee<ENG>) to die.” They were going to die because they opposed her (Pharaoh’s daughter).” The verse supports them (the Sages’ explanation), because [otherwise] why do we need to write: “And her maidens were walking [going]?” @d1Her Maid.@d2 [Meaning:] her handmaiden. But it is my opinion that it (@hh<HEB>מְשִׁיתִיהוּ@ee<ENG>) is not derived from @hh<HEB>מָשׁ @ee<ENG> or @hh<HEB>יָמוּשׁ@ee<ENG> but from the root @hh<HEB>מָשֹׁה@ee<ENG> which has the meaning of “extraction.” Similarly: “He extracts me (@hh<HEB>יַמְשֵׁנִי@ee<ENG>) from many waters.” For if it (@hh<HEB>מְשִׁיתִיהוּ@ee<ENG>) would have been from the root @hh<HEB> מָש@ee<ENG>, it would be incorrect to say @hh<HEB>מְשִׁיתִיהוּ@ee<ENG>, but rather @hh<HEB>הֲמִישׁוֹתִיהוּ@ee<ENG>, just as from the root @hh<HEB>קָם@ee<ENG> — (@ITarise@it) @hh<HEB>הֲקִימוֹתִי@ee<ENG> — (@ITI established@it) and from @hh<HEB>שָב@ee<ENG> — @hh<HEB>הֲשִׁיבוֹתִי@ee<ENG> — (@ITI caused to return@it) and from @hh<HEB>בָא@ee<ENG> — @hh<HEB>הֲבִיאוֹתִי@ee<ENG> (@ITI brought@it); or [another @hh<HEB>הפעיל@ee<ENG> form:] @hh<HEB>מַשְׁתִּיהוּ@ee<ENG> [should be used] as in: “I will remove the iniquity of the land,” whereas the form @hh<HEB>מָשִׁיתִי@ee<ENG> can only be derived from a word whose verb has a @hh<HEB>ה"א@ee<ENG> at the end of the [root] word. For example: @hh<HEB>כְּגוֹן מָשָׁה, בָּנָה, עָשָׂה, צִוָּה, פָּנָה@ee<ENG> when the intent is first person singular, the letter @hh<HEB>יו"ד@ee<ENG> is inserted in place of a @hh<HEB>ה"א @ee<ENG> as in @hh<HEB>בָנִיתִי@ee<ENG> — (@ITI built@it); @hh<HEB>עָשִׂיתִי@ee<ENG> — (@ITI did@it); @hh<HEB>צִוִּיתִי@ee<ENG> — (@ITI commanded@it). @p111@p2Moshe was grown.@p3 Was it not already perviously written “And the lad had grown up”? @p114@p2Who made you a man — @p3 “and you are still a boy.” @d1Do you intend to kill me.@d2 From here we infer that he killed [the Egyptian] with the Divine Name. @d1Moshe was frightened.@d2 As its plain meaning would indicate. @p119@p2The king of Egypt will not allow you to go —@p3 unless I show him My strong hand; meaning: “As long as I do not make him aware [of] My strong hand he will not allow you to leave. @d1Will not allow.@d2 [As Onkelos translates it:] He will not permit, as in: “Therefore I did not (@hh<HEB>נְתַתִּיךָ@ee<ENG>) allow you” — [or:] “God did not allow him to harm me.” These are all terms denoting @ITgiving.@it There are others who explain @hh<HEB>וְלֹא בְּיָד חֲזָקָה@ee<ENG>: “And not because his hand is strong, for ‘once I will send forth My Hand and strike Egypt, etc.’” Onkelos translates it [accordingly]: “And not because his power is strong.” In the name of R. Yaakov b. R. Menachem this was told to me. @p122@p2From the woman living in her house.@p3 [Meaning:] from the one that resides with her in the house. @d1And you will divest [Egypt from its wealth].@d2 As Onkelos translates it: @ITYou will empty.@it Similarly: “(@hh<HEB>וַיְנַצְּלוּ@ee<ENG>) And they emptied Egypt,” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>וַיִּתְנַצְּלוּ@ee<ENG>) The Israelites divested themselves of their ornaments.” The letter @hh<HEB>נ'@ee<ENG> in it is part of the root. However, Menachem places it in the Machberes [with the words beginning with a] @hh<HEB>צד"י@ee<ENG> together with: “(@hh<HEB>וַיַּצֵּל@ee<ENG>) God took away your father’s cattle” [or:] “That (@hh<HEB>הִצִּיל@ee<ENG>) God took away from our father.” His (Menachem’s) words cannot be given credence for if the @hh<HEB>נו"ן@ee<ENG> were not part of the root and [since] it is vocalized with a @ITchirik@it it cannot be used to express “And you shall do something” but, rather, a term of: “Something shall be done to you,” as in: “(@hh<HEB>וְנִסַחְתֶּם@ee<ENG>) You will be uprooted from the land,” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>וְנִתַּתֶּם@ee<ENG>) You will be given over to an enemy,” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>וְנִגַפְתֶּם@ee<ENG>) You will be stricken before your enemies,” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>וְנִתַּכְתֶּם@ee<ENG>) You will be disintegrated in her midst,” [or:] “You shall say, (@hh<HEB>נִצַּלְנוּ@ee<ENG>) ‘We have been saved.’ ” meaning: “Something was done to us.” Whereas, every letter @hh<HEB>נו"ן@ee<ENG> which sometimes appears in a word and is sometimes omitted such as the @hh<HEB>נו"ן@ee<ENG> of: @hh<HEB>נוֹגֵף, נוֹשֵֹא, נוֹתֵן, נוֹשֵׁךְ@ee<ENG> when it speaks in terms meaning: “And you shall do something” is vocalized with a @ITshva,@it e.g. “(@hh<HEB>וּנְשָׂאתֶם@ee<ENG>) You shall carry your father,” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>וּנְתַתֶּם@ee<ENG>) And you shall give them the land of Gilead,” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>וּנְמַלְתֶּם@ee<ENG>) And you shall circumcise the flesh of your foreskin.” I, therefore, say that this [letter @hh<HEB>נו"ן@ee<ENG>] that is vocalized with a @IT chirik@it is part of the root and has the same root as @hh<HEB>נִצּוּל@ee<ENG> which is one of the “strong” conjugations such as @hh<HEB>דִּבּוּר, כִּפּוּר, לִמּוּד@ee<ENG>. When [these words] express the term “And you shall do something will be vocalized with a @ITchirik,@it as in: “(@hh<HEB>וְדִבַּרְתֶּם@ee<ENG>) You shall speak to the stone,” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>וְכִּפַּרְתֶּם@ee<ENG>) And you will atone for the house,” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>וְלִמַּדְתֶּם@ee<ENG>) You shall teach it to your sons.”<QC>
@p12@p2“What is that in your hand?”@p3 [@hh<HEB>מַה זֶה@ee<ENG>] is written as one word so that it can be explained: “From that which is in your hand you deserve to be punished, for you were suspicious of righteous people.” The plain meaning of it is as a person who says to his friend, “Do you agree that the item before you is a stone?” “Yes,” he responds. “Well, watch as I turn it into wood.” @p13@p2And it turned into a snake.@p3 He indicated to him that he spoke slanderous words against Israel, when he said, “They will not believe me,” and so he assumed for himself the occupation of the snake. @p14@p2And took hold of it.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וַיַחֲזֶק@ee<ENG>] has the meaning of @ITgrasping.@it There are a number of [such uses] in Scripture: “[@hh<HEB>וַיַּחֲזִיקוּ@ee<ENG>] The men took hold of his hand,” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>וְהֶחֱזִיקָה@ee<ENG>) She took hold of his private parts,” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>וְהֶחֱזַקְתִּי@ee<ENG>) I took hold of his beard.” Wherever the verb @hh<HEB>חִזוּק@ee<ENG> is followed by the prefix @hh<HEB>ב' @ee<ENG> it has the meaning of @ITgrasping.@it<QC>
@p16@p2A snow-like @ITtzora’as.@it@p3 It is usual for @ITtzora’as@it to be white [as we find:] “If it be a white blemish.” With this sign, too, He hinted to him that he spoke slanderously when he said, “They will not believe me.” It is for this reason that He struck him with @ITtzora’as,@it just as Miriam was struck for speaking slanderously. @p17@p2And when he withdrew from his bosom, etc.@p3 We can derive from this that [when God reveals] His attribute of goodness it is much faster in coming than [when He reveals] His attribute for punishment, for in the first instance [when he was struck with @ITtzora’as@it] it does not state: “from his bosom.” @p18@p2Then they will believe the voice [of proof] of the latter sign.@p3 “Once you tell them, ‘because of you I was struck [with @ITtzora’as@it] for relating slanderous things about you, they will believe you, because they have already become experienced in this, that those who conspire to harm them are struck with @ITtzora’as,@it such as Pharaoh and Avimelech [who were struck with @ITtzora’as@it] on account of Sarah. @p19@p2You shall take from the water of the river.@p3 He alluded to them that with the first plague He will exact retribution from their deity (Explanation: When God exacts retribution from the nations He first exacts retribution from their deity, for they had worshipped the Nile which gave them sustenance and He turned them (its waters) to blood. @p114@p2The officers of the B’nei Yisrael were beaten.@p3 The officers were Israelites and they took pity on their fellow-men and did not pressure them and when they tallied the bricks for the taskmasters, who were Egyptians and they fell short of the quota, they [the Egyptians] would beat them (the officers) for not pressuring the workers. It is for this reason that these officers merited to become [members of] the Sanhedrin and some of the holy spirit which was on Moshe was set apart and put on them. As it is said: “Gather to me seventy men of the Elders of Israel” i.e., “of those about whom you know of the good that they did in Egypt, for @ITthey@it are the elders of the people and its officers.” @d1The officers of the B’nei Yisrael were beaten.@d2 [Those officers] that Pharaoh’s taskmasters appointed to be officers over them (the B’nei Yisrael). @d1And were told, “Why, etc.?”@d2 Why were they beaten? @p116@p2And yet we are told to make bricks.@p3 [I.e.] the taskmasters tell us, “Make bricks to equal the earlier amount.” @d1And it is a sin for your people.@d2 If @hh<HEB>וחטאת@ee<ENG> had been vocalized with a @ITpatach@it (@hh<HEB> וחטַאת@ee<ENG>), I would have said that it is in the construct state [and it would have meant:] “And this thing is the sin of your people.” But now that it is a kametz (@hh<HEB>וחטָאת@ee<ENG>), it is an [independent] noun and @ITthis@it is its meaning: “and this thing brings sin to your people,” as if it were written: “@hh<HEB>וְחַטָּאת לְעַמֶּךָ@ee<ENG>” “It will be a sin @ITto@it your people” as in @hh<HEB>כְּבוֹאָנָה בֵּית לֶחֶם@ee<ENG> — “when they came to Beis Lechem” which is the same as @hh<HEB>לְבֵית לֶחֶם@ee<ENG> — @ITto@it Beis Lechem, and there are many such instances. @p118@p2Quota of bricks.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וְתֹכֶן לְבֵנִים@ee<ENG> means:] the number of bricks. @d1And by a strong hand he will drive them from his land.@d2 He will drive them out against their will so that they will not have a chance to prepare provisions [for the journey]. And so it is said: “The Egyptians pressed the people” — to hurry and send them away.<QC>
@p12@p2Elokim spoke to Moshe.@p3 He (Hashem) spoke to him with severity for he (Moshe) had spoken harshly, saying, “Why have you brought harm to this nation?” @d1And said to him, “I am Adonoy.”@d2 (I.e.) “[I] can be trusted to properly reward those who walk before Me. And it is not in vain that I sent you but to keep My promise which I had made to the early Fathers.” And it is in this sense that we find in many places: @hh<HEB>,,אֲנִי ה'"@ee<ENG> [denoting] that He may be relied upon to exact retribution, when it (@hh<HEB>אני ה'@ee<ENG>) appears in the context of punishment, as in: “You shall be profaning your God’s Name (@hh<HEB>אֲנִי ה'@ee<ENG>) I am God. For I made promises but have not [as yet] fulfilled them.”<QC>
@p14@p2I also established My covenant, etc.@p3 [Meaning:] Also when I revealed Myself to them as Almighty Shaddai, I established and set up My covenant between Myself and them. @d1To give them the Land of Canaan.@d2 To Avraham, in the chapter concerning circumcision, it is said: “I am Almighty Shaddai, etc., and I will give to you and to your descendants who will follow you the land of your temporary residence.” To Yitzchok: “For to you and to your descendants I will give all these lands, I will [thus] keep the oath that I swore to Avraham,” and that oath that I swore to Avraham was with [My Name] Almighty Shaddai. To Yaakov: “I am Almighty Shaddai be fruitful and multiply, etc. and the land that, etc. [I gave to Avraham and to Yitzchok I will give to you].” Thus, [you see] that I have made vows to them but have not [as yet] fulfilled [them]. @p15@p2I have also.@p3 “Just as I established and set up the covenant so, too, must I fulfill it. @d1that the Egyptians are enslaving them and I remember@d2 that covenant, for at the covenant of ‘Between the Pieces’ I said to him (Avraham): ‘And also the nation whom they will serve I will judge.’” @p16@p2Therefore.@p3 [Meaning:] in accordance with that oath — @BOsay to the B’nai Yisrael — “I am Adonoy —@bo Who can be trusted to fulfill My promise.” @d1I will bring you out.@d2 For I so promised: ‘And afterwards they will leave with great wealth.’<QC>
@d1The burdens of Egypt.@d2 [Meaning:] the trouble of the burden of Egypt. @p18@p2I raised My hand.@p3 [Meaning:] “I lifted it to swear by My Throne.” @p19@p2But they would not listen to Moshe.@p3 [Meaning:] they did not accept [his] words of comfort. @d1Because of [their] shortness of wind.@d2 If someone is in a distressed state he is short of breath and he cannot draw long breaths. I heard [an explanation] similar to this one in this @ITParsha@it from Rabbi Baruch b. Rabbi Eliezer and he brought me proof from the following verse: “This time I will make known to them My hand and My might so that they will know that My Name is Adonoy.” We learn [from this] that when Hashem fulfills His words even where punishment is involved, He lets it be known that His Name is Adonoy. This is all the more so when it is a fulfillment of reward [promised]. The Sages, however, explained it as referring to what is discussed earlier — when Moshe said, “Why have You brought harm?” Hashem said to him, “Woe for those who are gone and cannot be replaced!” I have reason to mourn the deaths of the Fathers [for] many times I revealed Myself to them with [the Name] El Shaddai and they [never] said, “What is Your Name?” But, you said, “[‘Should they ask] what is His Name, what shall I tell them?’”... @d1...I also established, etc. (v. 4)...@d2 And when Avraham sought to bury Sarah, he could not find land until he had to buy it at great expense. Similarly, concerning Yitzchok, there was opposition to him on account of the wells that he dug. Similarly, concerning Yaakov, [it is written:] “He bought the part of the field” in order to pitch his tent, yet they did not question My attributes, but, you said, “Why did You bring harm?” However, this Midrash does not flow with the text for many reasons. First, because it does not state: “And they did @ITnot ask@it about My Name, Adonoy.” Should you say [that the intent of the verse is] He did not make known that that is His name, [this cannot be,] because in the beginning when He appeared to Avraham [at the Covenant] ‘Between the Pieces’ it is said: “I am Adonoy Who brought you out of Ur Kasdim.” And, furthermore, how is there continuity of context with the statements which [the Torah] places immediately afterwards, [viz.,] “I have also heard, etc.” (v. 5) [and:] “Therefore say to the B’nei Yisrael” (v. 6). Therefore, I say, let the verse be explained simply, each statement fitting its context, and let the exposition [by the Sages] be expounded, as it is said: “Is not My Word as fire, says Hashem, and like a hammer that shatters stone,” [so, too, does Hashem’s word] divide into many sparks. @p112@p2How then will Pharaoh listen to me.@p3 This is one of the ten @ITa fortiori@it inferences in the Torah. @d1[Whose] lips are covered.@d2 [Literally,] obstructed regarding the lips. Similarly, wherever a term [derived from] @hh<HEB>עָרְלָה@ee<ENG> [appears] I say that it means @ITobstructed@it [as in:] “Their ear is @hh<HEB>עֲרֵלָה@ee<ENG>,” [meaning:] obstructed from hearing. [or:] “@hh<HEB>עַרְלֵי@ee<ENG> of heart” [meaning:] obstructed of understanding, [or:] “You drink and @hh<HEB>וְהֵעָרֵל@ee<ENG>” [meaning:] and become obstructed [of understanding] from intoxication caused by the cup of curse. The foreskin of the flesh [is so called] because the member is obstructed and covered by it. @hh<HEB>וַעֲרַלְתֶּם עָרְלָתוֹ@ee<ENG> [means:] make for it (the fruit) an obstruction and cover — a prohibition that will prevent its being eaten. “For three years they shall be @hh<HEB>עֲרֵלִים@ee<ENG> to you” [@hh<HEB>עֲרֵלִים@ee<ENG> meaning:] obstructed and covered and barred from eating.<QC>
@p113@p2Adonoy [then] spoke to Moshe and Aharon.@p3 Since Moshe had said, “And I have a speech defect,” (v. 12) Hashem had Aharon join him so that he would be his spokesman and interpreter. @d1Commanding them regarding the B’nei Yisrael.@d2 He commanded them regarding them (the B’nei Yisrael) to lead them with gentleness and to be patient with them.<QC>
@d1And Pharaoh, king of Egypt.@d2 He commanded them regarding him (Pharaoh) to treat him with respect when speaking [to him]. This is its midrashic meaning. But, its simple meaning (of @hh<HEB>אֶל בְּנֵי יִשְּׂרָאֵל וְאֶל פַּרְעֹה@ee<ENG>) is that He commanded them regarding the matter of Yisrael and regarding his mission to Pharaoh. And as far as what the substance of the command was, it is explained in the next chapter after the order of the genealogy, but, having mentioned Moshe and Aharon, it (the Torah) interrupts the narrative with, “These are the heads of their paternal households” to teach us how they were born and to whom they trace their lineage. @p114@p2These are the heads of their paternal households.@p3 Since it was necessary to state the genealogy of the tribe of Leivi down to Moshe and Aharon because of Moshe and Aharon, it (the Torah) begins to give their genealogy in the order of their births, [beginning] from Reuvein. But, in @ITPesikta Rabbasi@it (7, 12) I saw: Because our Father Yaakov criticized these three tribes at the time of his death, Scripture, here again traces only their genealogy thereby indicating that they were [nevertheless] worthy. @p116@p2The years of Leivi’s life were, etc.@p3 Why are the years of Leivi’s life specified [here]? To tell the length of time that the enslavement lasted, for as long as one of the Tribes (Yaakov’s sons) remained alive there was no enslavement, as it is said “Yoseif and his brothers died,” and afterwards: “A new king arose,” and Leivi outlived all of them.<QC>
@p118@p2The years of Kehos’s life, the years of Amram’s life, etc. (v. 20).@p3 From these calculations we learn that the 400 year sojourn of the B’nei Yisrael of which Scripture speaks, was not [spent] in Egypt alone, but [was calculated] from the day Yitzchok was born. [This can be calculated thus:] For Kehos was among those who went down to Egypt. [Now] total up all of his years and the years of Amram [his son], and the eighty years of Moshe, you will find that they do not total [even] 400 years, [especially since] many of the [sons’] years are included in the fathers’ years. @p120@p2His aunt Yocheved.@p3 [Translated by Onkelos:] “His father’s sister,” for she was Leivi’s daughter, Kehos’s sister.<QC>
@p123@p2Sister of Nachshon.@p3 From here we learn that one [who contemplates] marrying a woman should [first] investigate her brothers.<QC>
@p125@p2One of the daughters of Putiel.@p3 [He was] of the descendants of Yisro who [at one time] fattened (@hh<HEB>פִּטֵּם@ee<ENG>) calves [for use as] idolatrous [sacrifices], and of the descendants of Yoseif, who showed contempt (@hh<HEB>פִּטְפֵּט@ee<ENG>) for his evil inclination.<QC>
@p126@p2This is [the lineage of] Aharon and Moshe.@p3 The same ones about whom it is mentioned above (v. 20) that Yocheved bore to Amram. @d1This is [the lineage of] Aharon and Moshe.@d2 There are places where [the Torah] sets Aharon ahead of Moshe, and there are places where it sets Moshe ahead of Aharon, to tell you that they were equal. @d1With their multitudes.@d2 [@hh<HEB>עַל צִבְאֹתָם@ee<ENG> meaning:] @ITwith@it their multitudes —  [i.e.] all their multitudes according to their tribes. This is the method [of a narrator], just as a person who says, “Let us return to the first [subject].” @p11@p2I have made you a god to Pharaoh.@p3 [@hh<HEB>אלקים@ee<ENG> means here] a judge and castigator, to castigate him with plagues and sufferings. @d1Will be your spokesman.@d2 [@hh<HEB>נְבִיאֶךָ@ee<ENG> is] as Onkelos translates it: “your interpreter.” Similarly, wherever [you find] the term @hh<HEB>נְבוּאָה@ee<ENG> (prophecy) [it refers to] a person who proclaims and lets the nation hear words of admonition, and comes from the same root as @hh<HEB>נִיב שְׂפָתָיִם@ee<ENG> [or] @hh<HEB>יָנוּב חָכְמָה@ee<ENG> — “It utters wisdom,” [or] @hh<HEB>וַיְכַל מֵהִתְנַבּוֹת@ee<ENG> — “He ended proclaiming,” of the Book of Shmuel. In Old French he is called @ITpreideor@it — preacher. @p12@p2You will speak —@p3 once, each and every message just as you heard it from Me and your brother Aharon will eloquently express it and make it palatable to the ears of Pharaoh. @p13@p2I will harden.@p3 Since he acted wickedly and in opposition towards Me, and it is revealed to Me that the nations take no pleasure in wholeheartedly repenting, it is better for Me that his heart be hardened so that My signs be increased against him and you will thereby recognize My power. This is the way of Hashem: He brings punishment upon the wicked nations so that Israel may hear about it and fear [Him], as it is said: “I have cut off nations making their corners desolate... @p19@p2A marvel.@p3 A sign to make it known that there is power in the One Who sends you. @d1A serpent.@d2 [@hh<HEB>לְתַנִּין@ee<ENG> means] a serpent.<QC>
@p111@p2With their magic art.@p3 [Onkelos translates it as] @hh<HEB>בְּלַחֲשֵׁיהוֹן@ee<ENG> — “with their magic.” There is no comparable word [to @hh<HEB>בְּלַהֲטֵיהֶם@ee<ENG>] in Scripture. However, one may compare to it: “the @hh<HEB>לַהַט@ee<ENG> of the rotating sword,” for it appears to rotate [as if] by magic. @p112@p2Aharon’s rod swallowed.@p3 After it became a rod again, did it swallow all the rest. @p114@p2Is heavy.@p3 The correct version of Onkelos is @hh<HEB>יַקִּיר@ee<ENG> — is @IT hard@it and not @hh<HEB>אִתְיַקַּר@ee<ENG> — @IThardened,@it because it is an adjective as in: “the thing is too hard for you.”<QC>
@p115@p2When he goes out to the water —@p3 to relieve himself, for he made claims to be a god and said that he did not need to relieve himself. He would arise early and go out to the Nile and there relieve himself. @p116@p2Thus Far.@p3 [The plain meaning of @hh<HEB>עַד כֹּה@ee<ENG> is:] thus far. But its midrashic explanation is: “until you will hear [the word @hh<HEB>כֹּה@ee<ENG>] from Me at the killing of the first-born, which I will begin with the word @hh<HEB>כֹה@ee<ENG>: [viz.] “@hh<HEB>כֹּה@ee<ENG> — Thus did Hashem say at midnight.” @p117@p2It will turn into blood.@p3 Since rain does not fall in Egypt and the Nile rises and irrigates the land, and the Egyptians worship the Nile, He therefore struck their god [first] and afterwards struck them. @p118@p2The Egyptians will weary themselves —@p3 [attempting] to find a cure so that the waters of their river should be fit to drink. @p119@p2Say to Aharon.@p3 Since the river protected Moshe when he was thrown into it, therefore it was not struck by him, neither at the plague of blood nor at the plague of frogs, but was struck by Aharon. @d1The rivers.@d2 [@hh<HEB>נְהָרוֹת@ee<ENG>] are the flowing rivers, just like our rivers. @d1Their canals.@d2 [@hh<HEB>יְאוֹרִים@ee<ENG>] are man-made irrigation canals that extend from the river’s edge to the fields. The waters of the Nile rise [periodically] and travel by way of the canals to irrigate the fields. @d1Their lakes.@d2 [@hh<HEB>אֲגַם@ee<ENG> is] a gathering of waters that do not spring up or flow but are stationary in one place, and called [in Old French] @ITestanc.@it @d1Throughout all the land of Egypt.@d2 Also in their bathhouses and the baths in their homes. @d1In the wood and stones.@d2 [Meaning] the water in the wooden vessels and the stone vessels.<QC>
@p122@p2With their magic art.@p3 [@hh<HEB>בְּלָטֵיהֶם@ee<ENG> refers to a] magical incantation which is said (@hh<HEB>בַּלָט@ee<ENG>) secretly and in a (@hh<HEB>לַחַשׁ@ee<ENG>) whisper. Our Sages say: @hh<HEB>בְּלָטֵיהֶם@ee<ENG> refers to works of demons, whereas @hh<HEB>בְּלַהֲטֵיהֶם@ee<ENG> (v. 11) refers to witchcraft. @d1Pharaoh’s heart remained hardened — @d2 by claiming, “You are doing this through witchcraft, you are importing straw to Efarayim — a city that is full of straw, you, too, are bringing witchcraft to Egypt which is full of witchcraft.” @p123@p2Even to this.@p3 [I.e., neither] to the wonder of the rod that turned into a serpent, neither to this [wonder] of the blood. @p125@p2[Seven] full [days passed].@p3 [@hh<HEB>וַיִמָּלֵא@ee<ENG> refers to] @ITthe number@it of seven days that the river did not revert to its original condition. For each plague functioned for a quarter of a month, and for three quarters [of a month] he would caution and warn them. @p127@p2If you refuse.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וְאִם מָאֵן אַתָּה@ee<ENG> means:] If you are a refuser @hh<HEB>מָאֵן@ee<ENG> meaning the same as @hh<HEB>מְמָאֵן@ee<ENG> “one who refuses”) or @hh<HEB>מְסָרֵב@ee<ENG> — “one who refuses.” [The difference being] only that it is describing a person based on an ongoing action, similar to @hh<HEB>שָׁלֵו@ee<ENG> — “a person at ease,” [or] @hh<HEB>שָׁקֵט@ee<ENG> — “a person serene” [or] @hh<HEB>סַר וְזָעֵף@ee<ENG> — “a person with anger.” @d1I will plague all of your borders.@d2 [@hh<HEB>נֹגֵף@ee<ENG> means] striking a blow. Similarly, every form of the word @hh<HEB>מַגֵּפָה@ee<ENG> does not [necessarily] refer to killing, but, rather, refers to striking. Similarly, “They will strike (@hh<HEB>וְנָגְפוּ@ee<ENG>) a pregnant woman” does not refer to killing. Similarly, “Before your feet (@hh<HEB>יִתְנַגְּפוּ@ee<ENG>) strike against something,” [or] “Lest your feet (@hh<HEB>תִּגּוֹף@ee<ENG>) strike a stone,” [or] “And as a stumbling stone.”<QC>
@p128@p2They will emerge@p3 — from the river. @d1Into your house@d2 — and afterwards in your servants’ houses. [Since] he initiated the plan at the beginning: “He said to his nation” (1, 9), therefore the punishment began with him. @p129@p2Upon you and upon your people.@p3 They would enter their intestines and croak. @p12@p2And the frog emerged.@p3 There was [only] one frog and [when] they struck at it it would split apart into various teeming swarms. That is its midrashic explanation. But as to its plain meaning, it may be said [that] the swarming of the frogs is referred to in the singular. Similarly @hh<HEB>וַתְּהִי הַכִּנָּם@ee<ENG> — [lit.] “There was lice” [referring to] the swarming. @ITPedoliere,@it in Old French — swarming lice, so, too @hh<HEB>וַתַּעַל הַצְּפַרְדֵּעַ@ee<ENG> [is translated as] @ITgrenoilliere@it in Old French — a swarm of frogs. @p15@p2Glorify yourself at my expense.@p3 As in: (@hh<HEB>הֲיִתְפָּאֵר@ee<ENG>) “Should the axe boast to the one who chops with it?” [@hh<HEB>הֲיִתְפָּאֵר@ee<ENG> meaning:] boasting by saying, “I am greater than you.” @ITWanter@it in Old French — to boast. This, too, is the meaning of @hh<HEB>הִתְפָּאֵר עָלַי@ee<ENG>: “You boast of your cleverness by asking [me to perform] something difficult and then saying that I will be unable to do it.” @d1[Exactly for] when shall I pray for you.@d2 I will pray for you today for the destruction of the frogs — by when do you wish them destroyed so that you may see whether I will fulfill my promise at the appointed time you will set for me.” Had it said: @hh<HEB>מָתַי אַעְתִּיר@ee<ENG> [without the @hh<HEB>ל'@ee<ENG> prefix], it would have meant: “When shall I pray?” But, now that it says @hh<HEB>לְמָתַי@ee<ENG> [with the @hh<HEB>ל'@ee<ENG> prefix], it means: “I shall pray for you today that the frogs be destroyed by whatever time you may set for me. Just say by which day you want them destroyed.” The words @hh<HEB>אַעְתִּיר@ee<ENG> (this verse), @hh<HEB>הַעְתִּירוּ@ee<ENG> (v. 4) and @hh<HEB>וְהַעְתַּרְתִּי@ee<ENG> (v. 25) [are in the @hh<HEB>הִפְעִיל@ee<ENG> form] and not: @hh<HEB>אֶעְתַּר, עִתְרוּ, וְעָתַרְתִּי@ee<ENG> [in the @hh<HEB>קַל@ee<ENG> form], because wherever a form of @hh<HEB>עָתֹר@ee<ENG> appears it denotes a great deal of praying. And just as it says: @hh<HEB>אַרְבֶּה, הַרְבּוּ, וְהִרְבֵּיתִי@ee<ENG> terms denoting @ITto cause to do,@it so, too, does it say @hh<HEB>אַעְתִּיר, הַעְתִּירוּ, וְהַעְתַּרְתִּי@ee<ENG> [referring to an increase of] words. The main proof of all this is [the verse:] “@hh<HEB>וְהַעְתַּרְתֶּם@ee<ENG> your words about me,” [@hh<HEB>וְהַעְתַּרְתֶּם@ee<ENG> meaning] @ITyou have increased.@it @p16@p2He [Pharaoh] said by tomorrow.@p3 [I.e.] “Pray today that they be destroyed by tomorrow.” @p18@p2They left — [and Moshe] cried out.@p3 [He cried out] immediately that they be destroyed by tomorrow.<QC>
@p110@p2Many heaps.@p3 [@hh<HEB>חֳמָרִים חֳמָרִים@ee<ENG> means] heaps [or mounds], as Onkelos translates it @hh<HEB>דְגוֹרִין@ee<ENG> which means @ITheaps.@it @p111@p2He hardened his heart.@p3 [The word @hh<HEB>הַכְבֵּד@ee<ENG>] is in the infinitive form the same as @hh<HEB>הָלוֹךְ וְנָסוֹעַ@ee<ENG> “going and travelling,” and similarly “@hh<HEB>וְהַכּוֹת@ee<ENG> (striking) the Moabites,” [or:] “@hh<HEB>וְשָׁאוֹל@ee<ENG> (and asking) Hashem for him” [or:] “@hh<HEB>הַכֵּה וּפָצֹעַ@ee<ENG> striking and wounding.” @d1Just as Adonoy had spoken.@d2 And where had He said this? When He said, “Pharaoh will not listen to you.”<QC>
@p112@p2Say to Aharon.@p3 It was not fitting that the dust be struck by Moshe because it protected him when he killed the Egyptian and hid him in the sand and was [instead] struck by Aharon. @p113@p2And there was lice (lit. louse).@p3 [@hh<HEB>הַכִּנָם@ee<ENG> refers to] the swarm of lice. @ITPedoliere@it in Old French — swarming lice. @p114@p2To bring forth lice.@p3 [Meaning] to create them from elsewhere. @d1But they could not — @d2 because a demon has no power over a creature that is smaller than barley. @p115@p2“This is the finger of Elohim.”@p3 [I.e.,] this plague was not brought about by witchcraft — it is from Hashem. @d1As Adonoy had spoken.@d2 “Pharaoh will not listen to you.” @p117@p2I will send against you.@d2 [Meaning] incite against you. Similarly, it is said, (Devorim 32:24) “And the teeth of the animals will I send [@hh<HEB>אֲשַׁלַּח@ee<ENG>] against them,” [@hh<HEB>אֲשַׁלַּח@ee<ENG>] being a term of @ITincitement.@it @ITEnticier@it in Old French — to incite.<QC>
@d1Swarms of wild animals.@d2 All kinds of wild beasts, snakes and scorpions, en masse (@hh<HEB>בְעִרְבוּבִיָא@ee<ENG> from @hh<HEB>עָרֹב@ee<ENG> — @ITa mixture@it), who caused devastation among them. There is a reason in aggadic writings for each and every plague why this particular one and why that particular one. With similar strategies of war as employed by kings did He (Hashem) come against them — as does a kingdom that besieges a city. First it ruins its springs (water supply) and afterwards they sound trumpets against them in order to frighten and terrify them: the frogs thus croaked and made loud noises, etc., as is recorded in Midrash Rabbi Tanchuma. @p118@p2I will set apart.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וְהִפְלֵיתִי@ee<ENG> means] I will set apart. Similarly: @hh<HEB>וְהִפְלָה ה'@ee<ENG> — “Hashem will set apart.” Similarly: “It is not @hh<HEB>נִפְלֵאת@ee<ENG> from You,” [meaning] it is not set apart nor separated from You.<QC>
@d1So that you will know that I am Adonoy in the midst of the earth.@d2 Though My Divine Presence is in the heavens, My decree is fulfilled in the lower worlds. @p119@p2I will set a [sign of] deliverance —@p3 which will set apart My nation from your nation. @p120@p2The land was devastated.@p3 [@hh<HEB>תִשָּׁחֵת הָאָרֶץ@ee<ENG> means:] the land was devastated; [as Onkelos translates it] @hh<HEB>אִתְחַבָּלַת אַרְעָא@ee<ENG> (past tense). @p121@p2“Sacrifice to your God [here] in the land.”@p3 [I.e.] “where you are, and do not go into the wilderness.”<QC>
@p122@p2Abominable to Egypt.@p3 [Actually] that which Egypt reveres (i.e., its idols) as in “and for Milkom the (@hh<HEB>תוֹעֲבַת@ee<ENG>) abomination of B’nei Ammon” but only when speaking to Israel is it called abominable. @hh<HEB>תּוֹעֲבַת מִצְרַיִם@ee<ENG> can also be explained another way: It would be despicable to Egypt [to witness] the sacrifice which we are sacrificing for it is their god that we sacrifice. @d1And not have them stone us?@d2 [Meant as] a wondrous question.<QC>
@p125@p2Deceive us.@p3 [@hh<HEB>הָתֵל@ee<ENG> is] the same as @hh<HEB>לְהָתֵל@ee<ENG>. @p126@p2And prayed to Adonoy.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וַיֶּעְתַּר@ee<ENG> means] he exerted himself in prayer. Similarly, had he wished to say @hh<HEB>וַיַּעְתִּיר@ee<ENG> he could have said so and it would mean he used many [words] of prayer. Now that he says it in terms of @hh<HEB>וַיִּפְעַל@ee<ENG> it means he increased [the intensity] of the prayer. @p127@p2He caused the wild animals to depart.@p3 They did not die as did the frogs, for had they died they would have benefitted from the skins. @p128@p2Again this time.@p3 [Once more,] though he said, “I will send you out,” he did not keep his promise. @p12@p2Holding them.@p3 [@hh<HEB>מַחֲזִיק בָּם@ee<ENG> means] @ITholding them,@it as in “(@hh<HEB>וְהֶחֱזִיקָה@ee<ENG>) and she took hold of his private parts.” @p13@p2Behold, the hand of Adonoy is directed.@p3 [@hh<HEB>הוֹיָה@ee<ENG>] is in the present tense, for this is how [the verb @hh<HEB>הָיָה@ee<ENG>] is expressed in the feminine gender: in the past tense: @hh<HEB>הָיְתָה@ee<ENG> — @ITshe was,@it in the future tense: @hh<HEB>תִּהְיֶה@ee<ENG> — @ITshe shall be,@it and in the present tense: @hh<HEB>הוֹיָה@ee<ENG> — @ITshe is;@it the same form as @hh<HEB>רוֹעָה@ee<ENG>, @hh<HEB>רוֹצָה@ee<ENG>, @hh<HEB>עוֹשָׂה @ee<ENG>. @p14@p2[Adonoy] will distinguish.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וְהִפְלָה@ee<ENG> means] He will set apart.<QC>
@p18@p2Full handfuls.@p3 @ITJelones@it in Old French — handfuls. @d1Furnace soot.@d2 [@hh<HEB>פִּיחַ@ee<ENG> is] that which is blown from dying embers that have been burnt in a furnace, in Old French @ITolves@it — furnace ashes. @hh<HEB>פִּיחַ@ee<ENG> having the same root as @hh<HEB>הַפָּחָה@ee<ENG> — @IT blowing,@it for the wind blows and disperses them. @d1And let Moshe throw it.@d2 And since anything thrown with force is thrown only with one hand, [we may conclude that] there were a number of miracles: One, that Moshe’s one fistful held both his and Aharon’s handfuls. And another, that the soot spread over the entire land of Egypt. @p19@p2A rash which will break out into boils.@p3 As Onkelos translates it: @hh<HEB>לְשִׁיחֲנָא סַגִּי אַבַעְבּוּעִין@ee<ENG>, (a rash producing boil) [meaning] that through it (@hh<HEB>שְׁחִין@ee<ENG>) boils sprouted all over them. @d1A rash.@d2 @hh<HEB>שְׁחִין@ee<ENG> has the meaning of @ITheat.@it There are several instances of this in the Mishnah: [e.g.] @hh<HEB>שָׁנָה שְׁחוּנָה@ee<ENG> — “a hot year.” @p110@p2In man and beast.@p3 Should you ask: from where did they have these animals, has it not already been stated: “and the livestock in Egypt died”? [I would answer] that the decree was only on those [that remained] in the fields exclusively, as it is said: “at your livestock that are in the field,” and whoever feared the word of Hashem made his livestock flee into the house. It is likewise taught in the Mechilta concerning: “He took 600 selected chariots.”<QC>
@p114@p2All My plagues.@p3 We learn from here that @hh<HEB>מכת בכורות@ee<ENG> is equivalent to all the plagues. @p115@p2For now.@p3 I [could] have extended my hand, etc.@p3 I.e., “for had I wished, [then] when My hand was [striking] at your livestock when I struck them with pestilence, I would have sent it and struck you and your people together with the animals — and you would have been obliterated from the earth. but it is for this reason that I have let you survive, etc. @p117@p2But you are still oppressing my people.@p3 [@hh<HEB>מִסְתּוֹלֵל בְּעַמִי@ee<ENG> is] as Onkelos translates it: “You are oppressing My people.” It (@hh<HEB>מִסְתּוֹלֵל@ee<ENG>) has the same root as @hh<HEB>מְסִלָה@ee<ENG>, which is translated by Onkelos as: “a well-worn path.” @ITChalchier@it in Old French — a trodden path. I have already explained at the end of Parshas Miketz that any word whose first root letter is @hh<HEB>סָמֶ"ךְ@ee<ENG> and intends to convey the @hh<HEB>הִתְפָּעֵל@ee<ENG> conjugation one places the prefix letter @hh<HEB>ת'@ee<ENG> in the middle of the root letters as in this case (@hh<HEB>מִסְתּוֹלֵל@ee<ENG>) and for example @hh<HEB>וְיִסְתַּבֵּל הֶחָגָב@ee<ENG> — “the grasshopper will drag itself,” @hh<HEB>יִסְתַּבֵּל@ee<ENG> having the root @hh<HEB>סָבֹל@ee<ENG> — carry a load, [or] @hh<HEB>כִּי תִשְׂתָּרֵר עָלֵינוּ@ee<ENG> — “that you would make yourself our ruler” @hh<HEB>תִשְׂתָּרֵר@ee<ENG> having the root of @hh<HEB>שַׂר וְנָגִיד@ee<ENG> — “a prince and a nobleman,” [or] @hh<HEB>מִשְׂתַּכַּל הֲוֵית@ee<ENG> — “I was reflecting.” @p118@p2Tomorrow at this time.@p3 [@hh<HEB>כָּעֵת מָחָר@ee<ENG> means] at the same time tomorrow. He scratched a mark in the wall [and said,] “Tomorrow when the sun reaches here the hail will come down. @d1It was founded.@d2 [@hh<HEB>הִוָּסְדָה@ee<ENG> means] that it was founded. Every word whose first root-letter is a @hh<HEB>י'@ee<ENG>, e.g., @hh<HEB>יָסֹד@ee<ENG> (founded), @hh<HEB>יָלֹד@ee<ENG> (gave birth) @hh<HEB>יָדֹעַ@ee<ENG> (knew), @hh<HEB>יָסֹר@ee<ENG> (removed), when it is put in the @hh<HEB>הִתְפָּעֵל@ee<ENG> form the letter @hh<HEB>ו'@ee<ENG> replaces the letter @hh<HEB>י'@ee<ENG>, as in @hh<HEB>הִוָּסְדָה@ee<ENG> or @hh<HEB>הִוָּלְדָה@ee<ENG> @hh<HEB>וַיִּוָּדַע@ee<ENG> — “it became known,” [or] @hh<HEB>וַיִּוָּלֵד לְיוֹסֵף@ee<ENG> — “There was born to Yoseif,” [or] “With words will a servant @hh<HEB>לֹא יִוָּסֶר@ee<ENG> — not be rebuked.” @p119@p2Send [word] and gather.@p3 @hh<HEB>שְׁלַח הָעֵז@ee<ENG> is as Onkelos translates it: “send [word] and gather.” Similarly: “The inhabitants of Geibim (@hh<HEB>הֵעִיזוּ@ee<ENG>) gathered,” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>הֵעִיזוּ@ee<ENG>) gather! children of Binyamin.” @d1And will not be brought into the house.@d2 @hh<HEB>יֵאָסֵף@ee<ENG> is a term meaning @ITgathering in.@it @p120@p2Made flee.@p3 @hh<HEB>הֵנִיס@ee<ENG> means @ITcaused to flee@it from the word “@hh<HEB> וַיָּנָס@ee<ENG>.” @p122@p2Toward the Heavens.@p3 [@hh<HEB>עַל הַשָּׁמַיִם@ee<ENG> means] toward the heavens. But the midrashic explanation is: Hashem lifted Moshe over the heavens.<QC>
@p124@p2Flashing Among the Hailstones.@p3 [This was] a miracle within a miracle; the fire and the hail intermingled, yet the hail is water! And to do the bidding of their Creator they made peace between themselves.<QC>
@p128@p2Enough.@p3 [I.e.] let it be sufficient for Him with what He has already brought down. @p129@p2When I leave the city.@p3 [@hh<HEB>אֶת הָעִיר@ee<ENG> has the same meaning as] @hh<HEB>מִן הָעִיר@ee<ENG> — from the city. But within the city he would not pray because it was full of idols. @p130@p2You are not yet in fear.@p3 [@hh<HEB>טֶרֶם תִּירְאוּן@ee<ENG> is translated:] “You are @ITnot yet@it in fear.” Similarly, wherever @hh<HEB>טֶרֶם@ee<ENG> appears in Scripture it has the meaning: @ITnot yet,@it and does not mean: @ITbefore;@it as in @hh<HEB>טֶרֶם יִשְׁכָּבוּ@ee<ENG> — [which Onkelos translates] “They did @ITnot yet@it lie down,” [or: @hh<HEB>טֶרֶם יִצְמָּח@ee<ENG> — [which Onkelos translates:] “did @ITnot yet@it sprout. This one (i.e., @hh<HEB>טֶרֶם תִּירְאוּן@ee<ENG>), too, has a similar meaning: “I know that you [and your servants] do not yet fear, and that the moment there will be relief you will continue with your corruption. @p131@p2The flax and barley have been broken.@p3 [@hh<HEB>נֻכָּתָה@ee<ENG> meaning] has been broken, from the same term as @hh<HEB>פַּרְעֹה נְכֹה@ee<ENG> — @ITthe lame Pharaoh;@it [or] @hh<HEB>נְכָאִים@ee<ENG> — @ITbroken.@it Similarly @hh<HEB>לֹא נֻכּוּ@ee<ENG> [means “were not broken”]. It would not be correct to explain it as a term meaning “striking,” for the letter @hh<HEB>נ'@ee<ENG> cannot replace the letter @hh<HEB>ה' @ee<ENG> so that @hh<HEB>נוּכָּתָה@ee<ENG> can be explained as @hh<HEB>הוּכָּתָה@ee<ENG>, or @hh<HEB>נֻכּוּ@ee<ENG> to be the same as @hh<HEB>הוּכּוּ@ee<ENG>. But rather, the @hh<HEB>נ'@ee<ENG> is part of the word-root and is [grammatically] the same form as @hh<HEB>וְשֻׁפוּ עַצְמוֹתָיו@ee<ENG> — “his bones were bared.”<QC>
@d1Since the barley was ripe.@d2 [Meaning] it has already ripened and is standing in its stalks and they [therefore] broke and fell. So, too, the flax has already become full-grown and has hardened so as to stand on its stems. @d1The barley was ripe.@d2 [@hh<HEB>אָבִיב@ee<ENG> meaning] stood in its stalks, from the same term as: @hh<HEB>בְּאִבֵּי הַנָּחַל@ee<ENG> — “the stalks of the stream.” @p132@p2Since they are late in sprouting.@p3 [@hh<HEB>אֲפִיGֹת@ee<ENG> meaning] late, and were still soft [and pliable] and were able to stand [even] in the face of [a] hard [blow]. Although it has been stated: “All the vegetation in the fields was struck by the hail” one can explain the plain meaning of the verse as referring to the vegetation that stood in their stalks (i.e., were ripe), and were vulnerable to be broken by the hail. [However], in Midrash Rabbi Tanchuma, some Sages dispute this and explain @hh<HEB>כִּי אֲפִיGֹת@ee<ENG> [as meaning] a wonder of wonders (@hh<HEB>פֶּלֶא@ee<ENG> =wonder) occurred that they were not struck.<QC>
@p133@p2Did not reach.@p3 [@hh<HEB>לֹא נִתַּךְ@ee<ENG> meaning] did not reach. Even those that were already in the air [never] reached the ground. Similar to this is: “@hh<HEB>וַתִּתַּךְ עָלֵינוּ@ee<ENG> the curse and the oath” in the Book of Daniel [@hh<HEB>וְתַּגִּיעַ עָלֵינוּ@ee<ENG> meaning:] it reached us. Menachem ben Saruk classified it in the category of @hh<HEB>כְּהִתּוּךְ כֶּסֶף@ee<ENG> meaning: the pouring (melting) of metal. I agree with his opinion, as Onkelos translates “@hh<HEB>וַיִּצֹק@ee<ENG> as @hh<HEB>וְאַתִּיךְ@ee<ENG> [and] “@hh<HEB>לָצֶקֶת@ee<ENG>” @hh<HEB>לְאַתָּכָא@ee<ENG> — to cast. So, too, @hh<HEB>לֹא נִתַּךְ לָאָרֶץ@ee<ENG> [has the meaning:] [The hail] was not poured to the ground.<QC>

@p12@p2I made a mockery.@p3 [@hh<HEB>הִתְעַלַּלְתִּי@ee<ENG> means] I have mocked, as in: “For (@hh<HEB>הִתְעַלַּלְתְּ@ee<ENG>) you have mocked me;” [or:] “Is it not so, when (@hh<HEB>הִתְעַלֵּל@ee<ENG>) he mocked them?” which is said regarding the Egyptians. @d1Beware, for evil confronts you.@d2 This should be translated as Onkelos does. An aggadic explanation that I heard [states:] There is a star by the name of “Evil”; Pharaoh said to them: “I see by my astrological calculations that that star is rising towards you in the wilderness and it is a sign of blood and killing.” When [eventually] the Israelites sinned by [worshipping] the calf and God wished to kill them, Moshe said in his prayer, “Why should the Egyptians speak, saying: ‘He took them out with (@hh<HEB>רָעָה@ee<ENG>) the star “Evil,’” for this is meant by what he said, ‘Beware, for “Evil” confronts you’.” Immediately “God reconsidered the ‘Evil’” and turned the blood to blood of circumcision for Yehoshua circumcised them. @p119@p2Westerly wind.@p3 [@hh<HEB>רוּחַ יָם@ee<ENG> means] a westerly wind. @d1Into the Reed sea@d2 It is my opinion that the Reed Sea was partially to the west [of Eretz Yisrael] opposite the entire southern side, and was also on the eastern side of Eretz Yisrael. @p121@p2 The darkness will be tangible.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וְיָמֵשׁ חֹשֶׁךְ@ee<ENG> means:] There will be upon them a darkness greater then the darkness of night, [i.e.,] the darkness of night will become even more black and dark. @d1@hh<HEB>וְיָמֵשׁ@ee<ENG> (will be tangible) —@d2 is the same as @hh<HEB>וְיַאֲמֵשׁ@ee<ENG> (@ITHe will darken@it). We have many words missing the letter @hh<HEB>א'@ee<ENG>. Since the enunciation of the @hh<HEB>א'@ee<ENG> is not very noticeable Scripture is not particular if it is omitted. For example: @hh<HEB>וְלֹא יַהֵל שָׁם עֲרָבִי@ee<ENG> [@hh<HEB>וְלֹא יַהֵל@ee<ENG>] being the same as @hh<HEB>לֹא יַאֲהִיל@ee<ENG> ( @ITwith@it an @hh<HEB>א'@ee<ENG>) [meaning:] “[An Arab] will not pitch his tent [there].” Similarly, @hh<HEB>וַתַּזְרֵנִי חָיִל@ee<ENG> (“you have girded me with strength”) the same as @hh<HEB>וַתְּאַזְרֵנִי@ee<ENG> — (@ITwith@it an @hh<HEB>א' @ee<ENG>). Onkelos translates [@hh<HEB>וְיָמֵש@ee<ENG>] as having the meaning of @ITremoving@it as in @hh<HEB>לֹא יָמוּשׁ@ee<ENG> — “It will not depart.” [Onkelos’ understanding of @hh<HEB>וְיָמֵשׁ חְשֶׁךְ@ee<ENG> is:] “After the darkness of night has departed” [i.e.,] when the light of day will approach. However, this statement does not fit the letter @hh<HEB>ו'@ee<ENG> of the word @hh<HEB>וְיָמֵשׁ@ee<ENG> because it is written after @hh<HEB>וִיהִי חֹשֶׁך@ee<ENG>. A Midrash Aggada explains it (@hh<HEB>וְיָמֵשׁ@ee<ENG>) from the term (@hh<HEB>מְמַשֵּׁשׁ@ee<ENG>) feeling his way in the afternoon,” for [the darkness] was doubled and redoubled and so thick that it was tangible.<QC>
@p122@p2There was total darkness. For three days, etc.,@p3 [@hh<HEB>חֹשֶׁךְ אֲפֵילָה@ee<ENG> means:] darkness of gloom so that one person did not see another during those three days, and for another three days afterwards [there was] a darkness twice as thick where no one was able to rise from his place. If he was sitting he could not stand and if he was standing he could not sit. Why did He bring darkness on them? Because there were among the Israelites of that generation evil people who did not wish to leave, and they died out during the three days of darkness so that the Egyptians not see their demise thereby saying, “They are being struck as we are.” Furthermore, [during the darkness] the Israelites searched and saw their [the Egyptians’ valuable] vessels and when they left [Egypt] and asked them [for the valuable vessels] and they (the Egyptians) responded, “We have nothing [to give you],” he (the Israelite) would say to him, “But I did see it in your house and it is in that particular place.” @d1Three days.@d2 [@hh<HEB>שְׁלֹשֶׁת יָמִים@ee<ENG> means:] @ITa threesome of days.@it @ITTerzaine@it in Old French — a threesome. @d1About the time of midnight.@d2 [@hh<HEB>כַּחֲצֹת לַיְלָה@ee<ENG> lit. means:] @ITwhen the night is divided. @it @hh<HEB>כַּחֲצוֹת@ee<ENG> being the same form as @hh<HEB>בַּעֲלוֹת@ee<ENG> (@IT When [it] went up@it) or (@hh<HEB>בַּחֲרוֹת@ee<ENG>) @ITwhen they became angry at us.@it This is its (@hh<HEB>כַּחֲצֹת לַיְלָה@ee<ENG>) plain meaning so that it fits within its context, for @hh<HEB>חֲצוֹת@ee<ENG> is not the noun of @hh<HEB>חֵצִי@ee<ENG>. @p15@p2To the first-born of the captive.@p3 Why were the captives struck down? @p19)@p2Thus I will multiply My wonders.@p3 The word @hh<HEB>מוֹפְתַי@ee<ENG> [in the plural] represents two [wonders]. @hh<HEB>רְבוֹת@ee<ENG> — @ITincrease@it represents a third [wonder]: 1. the killing of the first-born, 2. the splitting of the Reed Sea, and 3. the turbulent stirring of the Egyptians. @p110@p2Moshe and Aharon had done, etc.@p3 This has already been written for us regarding all the wonders, and is repeated here only as a lead-in to the section that follows it. @p11@p2Adonoy said to Moshe and Aharon.@p3 Since Aharon performed, and troubled himself, in regard to the wonders, just as much as Moshe, He (God) does him this honor at the first @ITmitzvah@it, by including him with Moshe in this message. @d1In the land of Egypt —@d2 [but] outside the city. or was that not so, but [it was] rather within the city? The Torah, therefore, states [concerning Moshe’s prayer]: “When I leave the city, etc.” Now, if [regarding] prayer, which is [relatively] less significant, he would not pray within the city for it was full of idols, then, [regarding] a message [from God], which is so much more significant, how much more so [that he would leave the city.] @p12@p2This month.@p3 He (God) showed him the moon at its renewal and said to him, “When the moon renews itself, let that be for you the beginning of a new month.” However, Scripture does not depart from its plain meaning: He (God) was telling him about the month of @ITNissan@it; [viz.] this (Nissan) shall be the start of the order of the counting of the months, so that @ITIyar@it will be called the second [month] and @ITSivan@it, the third. @d1This.@d2 Moshe had difficulty regarding the renewal of the moon how much of it must be visible so that it be fit to be consecrated. He (God) then showed him with His “finger” the moon in the sky, and said to him, “When you see it like this, sanctify it.” But how could He show it to him — did He not speak with him only by day, as it is said: “And on @ITthe day@it that God spoke,” [or:] “on @ITthe day@it that He commanded him,” [or:] “from @ITthe day@it that God commanded and beyond?” But [the answer is] it was near sunset [when] this section was told to him (Moshe) and He pointed it out to him at nightfall. @p13@p2Speak to the entire community.@p3 But did Aharon speak? Has it not already been stated “You (Moshe) speak?” But [the answer is] they bestowed honor on each other and said to one another: “Teach me [what to say].” The message would then emanate from both, as if both were speaking. @d1To the entire community of Israel saying, “On the tenth [day] of this month.”@d2 [I.e.,] “Speak today, on the first of the month that they should take it (the lamb) on the tenth of the month.<QC>
@d1This.@d2 The @ITkorbon Pesach@it in Egypt was acquired on the tenth, but not the @ITkorbon Pesach@it of future generations. @d1A lamb for [his] family.@d2 [Meaning one lamb] per family. Thus, if there were many [members in the family], one might think that one lamb [suffices] for all. The Torah, therefore, states: “A lamb for each household.” @p14@p2If the household [members] are too few for the lamb.@p3 [I.e.,] If they are too few to be sufficient for one lamb, so that they are unable to eat it [all] and will thus come to [the transgression of] @hh<HEB>נוֹתָר@ee<ENG>, then “Let him and his neighbor take, etc.,” That is the implication here according to its plain meaning. However, there is also an exposition [of the Sages]: [This verse intends] to teach that [even] after they have been counted in it they may [still] be excluded [from the group] and withdraw their association from it and be counted as [partners] of another lamb. However, if they do decide to withdraw their association and be counted [as partners] of another lamb, then this should be done “@hh<HEB>מִהְיוֹת מִשֶּׂה@ee<ENG>” — [i.e.,] they should be excluded while the lamb still exists, [that is to say,] while it is still alive and not once it has been slaughtered.<QC>
@d1According to the number.@d2 [@hh<HEB>בְּמִכְסַת@ee<ENG> means: according to] @ITthe number.@it Similarly: @hh<HEB>מִכְסַת הָעֶרְכְּךָ@ee<ENG> [means @ITthe number of the evaluation@it]. @d1According to what the person eats.@d2 Each according to his fitness to eat, to exclude the sick and the old who cannot eat a @ITkezayis@it (a piece the size of an olive). @d1Shall you make your count.@d2 [As Onkelos translates it:] @hh<HEB>תִּתְמְנוּן@ee<ENG> — @ITyou will be counted.@it @p15@p2Flawless.@p3 Without a blemish @d1A yearling@d2 Its entire [first] year it is called a @hh<HEB>בֶּן שָׁנָה@ee<ENG>, as if to say: it was born this year. @d1From the sheep or goats.@d2 Either one or the other, for a goat is also called @hh<HEB>שֶׂה@ee<ENG> as it is said: “And a @hh<HEB>שֶׂה@ee<ENG> of the goats.” @p16@p2You shall hold it in safekeeping.@p3 This term (@hh<HEB>לְמִשְׁמֶרֶת@ee<ENG>) refers to “examining,” for it requires examination against any blemish for four days before its slaughter. Why was it necessary to acquire it four days before its slaughter — something not commanded regarding the Pesach of later generations? [In response] R. Masia b. Cheresh would say: Scripture says: “And I passed over you and saw you and behold your time was a time of love” — “the time has come [to fulfill] the oath which I swore to Avraham that I will redeem his children.” But they possessed no @ITmitzvos@it with which to be occupied in order to merit redemption as it is said “And you are naked and bare.” So He gave them two @ITmitzvos@it [regarding] the blood of the [@ITkorbon@it] @ITpesach@it and the blood of circumcision, for that night they circumcized themselves; as it is said: [“when I passed over you I saw] you wallowing in your bloods (pl.),” referring to two bloods (Pesach and circumcision). The Torah says: “Seven days.” This is one of the (thirteen) methods [through which] the Torah [is expounded]: Something that was included in a general rule and was singled out to teach [something new] was not singled out to teach only about itself but rather to teach [something new] concerning the rule as a whole [In this case it applies thus] just as [eating matzoh] the seventh day is voluntary so, too, are the [other] six days voluntary. @p140@p2Living in Egypt.@p3 After the other settlements where they settled as strangers in a land that was not theirs. @d1Four hundred and thirty years.@d2 Altogether, from when Yitzchok was born until now, there were four hundred years. [Our reckoning is from then] because [only] from the time that Avraham had a child could there be a fulfillment [of the prophecy]: “For your descendant will be a stranger,” and thirty years passed from when the decree of “between the pieces” was decreed until the birth of Yitzchok. Similarly: “For Adonoy is fighting @ITfor@it them.” Similarly: “Will you argue @ITfor@it God?” Similarly: “And the one who spoke @ITfor@it me” Similarly: “Will you argue @ITfor@it the Ba’al” @p115@p2 Why do you cry to Me?@p3 [From this] we learn that Moshe was standing and praying [when] God said to him, “Now is not the time to pray @ITat length,@it when Yisrael is in trouble.” An alternate explanation: “Why do you cry out? @hh<HEB>אֵלָי@ee<ENG> — This thing depends on Me, not on you.” [This is ] similar to what is written elsewhere: “Concerning My sons and concerning My handiwork do you need to command Me?!” @d1Speak to the B’nei Yisrael and let them move on.@d2 There is nothing for them to do but move on because the sea will not stand in their way. The merit of their ancestors as well as the trust they had in me, when they went out [of Egypt], is effective to split the sea for them.<QC>
@p119@p2And went behind them.@p3 To separate the Egyptian camp from the Israelite camp and to receive the arrows and the projectiles of the Egyptians. Everywhere else it says: “The angel of Adonoy” but here [it states] “The angel of Elokim.” “Elokim” wherever it occurs denotes only “judgment.” This teaches that Israel was placed in judgment at that moment, whether [they were worthy] to be saved or whether to be destroyed with the Egyptians. @d1The pillar of cloud moved.@d2 After it became dark and the pillar of cloud completed its tour over the camp [and gave way] to the pillar of fire, the cloud was not removed as it was usually removed in the evening  — completely, but, rather, it continued to move behind them to make it dark for the Egyptians. @p120@p2 It came between the Egyptian camp.@p3 This can be compared to a person on a journey, with his son walking ahead of him. If highway men would come to kidnap him (the son), he would take him from in front and place him behind. Then, if a wolf would come up from behind he would place him (the son) in front. If highway men would approach from the front and wolves from behind, he would place him on his arm and fight them. So, too, [did God do for the Israelites]: “I led Ephraim (Israel) — He took them upon His arms.” @d1There was the cloud and darkness@d2 — for the Egyptians. @d1And lit up.@d2 [I.e.,] the pillar of fire [lit up] the night for the Israelites and moved in front of them, as was usual for it to travel all night, while the darkness of the cloud was turned toward the Egyptians. @d1They did not approach each other@d2 [I.e.,] one camp to another. @p121@p2With a strong east wind,@p3 @hh<HEB>בְּרוּחַ קָדִים עַזָּה@ee<ENG> — with an east wind, which is the strongest of all the winds. It is this wind with which God punishes the wicked as it is said: “I will disperse them with the east wind.” [or:] “And east wind will come — the wind of God.” [or:] “The east wind has shattered you in the heart of the seas.” [or:] “He removed with His strong wind on the day of the east wind.” @d1And the waters were divided@d2  — all the waters of the world. @p123@p2All of Pharaoh’s @hb<HEB>סוס@hb@p1<ENG> horse(s).@p3 Was there only one horse?! This teaches us that all of them are considered before God as but one horse. @p124@p2The morning watch.@p3 The three parts of the night are called @hh<HEB>אַשְׁמוּרוֹת@ee<ENG>, and the one before the morning is called @hh<HEB>אַשְׁמֹרֶת הַבֹּקֶר@ee<ENG> — “The morning @hh<HEB>אַשְׁמֹרֶת@ee<ENG>.” It is my opinion that because the night is divided into watches for the song of the ministering angels, one group [of angels] after another, in three parts, therefore, it is called @hh<HEB>אַשְׁמוֹרֶת@ee<ENG>. It is for this reason that Onkelos translates it: @hh<HEB>מַטְּרַת@ee<ENG> — [meaning: @ITa watch@it]. @d1Gazed down.@d2 [@hh<HEB>וַיַּשְׁקֵף@ee<ENG> means literally] he gazed; as if to say: He turned His attention to them to destroy them. Onkelos’ translation of it as: @hh<HEB>וְאִסְתְּכֵי@ee<ENG>, is also a term referring to @ITgazing@it as in: “@hh<HEB>שְֹדֵה צֹפִים@ee<ENG> [which Onkelos translates as:] the field of the gazers. @d1Through the pillar of fire and cloud.@d2 The pillar of cloud descended and made it (the sea floor) like clay and the pillar of fire heated it up so that the horses’ hooves fell off. @d1And He caused panic.@d2 [@hh<HEB>וַיָּהָם@ee<ENG> is derived from] the term @hh<HEB>מְהוּמָה@ee<ENG> — confusion, @ITestordison,@it in Old French (tumult, confusion). He put them into a state of confusion; [i.e.] He took away their senses. We learned in the Chapters of R. Eliezer the son of R. Yose haGlili: wherever the term @hh<HEB>מְהוּמָה@ee<ENG> is used, it indicates a thunderous noise and the following is the forerunner of them all: “And God thundered with a great sound, etc. upon the Plishtim (@hh<HEB>וַיְהֻמֵּם@ee<ENG>) and threw them into confusion.”<QC>
@p125@p2He removed the wheels of their chariots.@p3 As a result of the fire the wheels burnt up causing the chariots to be dragged and those who were sitting in them were thrown about causing their limbs to break apart. @d1And made them move heavily.@d2 [He moved them] with a movement that was heavy and hard for them — in the same measure that they (Egypt) meted out. [Egypt acted thus:] “He @IThardened@it his heart, he and his servants;” here, too, “He treated them @IThard@it (harshly).” @d1Is fighting for them against Egypt.@d2 [@hh<HEB>בְּמִצְרַיִם@ee<ENG> means:] against the Egyptians. An alternate explanation of @hh<HEB>בְּמִצְרַיִם@ee<ENG>:  — @ITin the land of Egypt@it [viz.] that just as these are being struck on the sea, so, too, are those left @ITin Egypt@it being struck. @p126@p2 And the water will come back.@p3 [The waters] that were standing erect as a wall will return to their place and cover the Egyptians.<QC>
@p127@p2 Toward morning.@p3 At the time that the morning @ITturns@it to come. @d1To its strength.@d2  — to its @IToriginal@it strength. @d1Were fleeing toward it.@d2 Because they were confused and in panic, they ran @ITtoward@it the waters. @d1Adonoy overturned.@d2 As a person who stirs a pot and overturns that which is on top to the bottom and that which is at the bottom to the top, so, too, did they (the Egyptians) go up and down and were broken apart in the sea; and God kept them alive so that they receive the pain [longer]. @d1Stirred.@d2 [Onkelos translates it:] @hh<HEB>וְשַׁנֵּיק@ee<ENG> which is a term meaning @ITto stir@it in Aramaic. There are many such usages in Midrash Aggadah. @p128@p2They covered the chariots, etc.@bo @BOincluding Pharaoh’s entire army.@p3 This is usual for [Biblical] verses to write a redundant @hh<HEB>למ"ד@ee<ENG> as in: “(@hh<HEB>לְכָל@ee<ENG> all its vessels you shall make of copper.” Similarly: “(@hh<HEB>לְכֹל@ee<ENG>) all the vessels of the Mishkon in all its service.” [or:] “and their pegs and their ropes (@hh<HEB>לְכָל@ee<ENG>) and all their vessels.” This is done only for the purpose of style. @p130@p2 Yisrael saw the Egyptians dead.@p3  — because the sea expelled them (the Egyptians) onto its shore, so that Yisrael would not say: “Just as we are coming up from this side so, too, are they coming up from the other side, at a distance from us and they will pursue us.” @p131@p2 The great hand.@p3 [Meaning:] the great power which was effected by the hand of God. There are many meanings that fit the term @hh<HEB>יָד@ee<ENG>, and they all signify the actual @IThand@it and the one explaining it should fit the meaning according to the context. @p11@p2Then Moshe sang.@p3 [I.e.,] @ITThen,@it when Moshe saw the miracle, it entered his mind (@hh<HEB>שֶׁיָּשִׁיר@ee<ENG>) to sing a song. Similarly: “Then @hh<HEB>יְדַבֵּר@ee<ENG> Yehoshua.” Similarly: “And a house @hh<HEB>יַעֲשֶׂה@ee<ENG> for Pharaoh’s daughter,” [meaning:] he thought in his heart to make it for her. Here, too, @hh<HEB>יָשִׁיר@ee<ENG> [means] his heart told him that he should sing, and he did so [as is immediately added:] “@hh<HEB>וַיֹּאמְרוּ לֵאמֹר@ee<ENG> and they said, ‘I will sing to Adonoy’ ” Similarly in the case of Yehoshua — when he saw the miracle, his heart told him to speak, and he did so [as it says:] “And he said before the eyes of Israel.” This also applies to the Song of the Well, which begins with: @hh<HEB>אָז יָשִׁיר יִשְׂרָאֵל@ee<ENG> it explicitly states afterwards: “Come up, you Well — sing to it” [similarly:] @hh<HEB>אָז יִבְנֶה שְׁלֹמֹה בָּמָה@ee<ENG>, which the Sages of Israel explained that he @ITwanted@it to build [an altar for an idol] but he did not [actually] build one. We learn from this that the prefix letter @hh<HEB>י'@ee<ENG> is used to express intention. All of the above is to satisfy [the verse’s] plain meaning. However, its midrashic explanation: Our Sages, of blessed memory, said: From here we derive an allusion that the resurrection of the dead [has its source] in Scripture. This applies to all [the examples cited] except for the one regarding Shlomo which they explained “he intended to build but did not build.” One cannot say and explain this usage the same way as other things written in the future tense and, yet, referring to the present; for example: “Thus (@hh<HEB>יַעֲשֶׂה@ee<ENG>) is Iyov doing” [or:] “At the command of God (@hh<HEB>יַחֲנוּ@ee<ENG>) they encamp” [or:] “Sometimes the cloud is [on the Mishkon].” This is because they are things that were continuously happening and they can accept both future and past. But this (@hh<HEB>יָשִׁיר@ee<ENG> and the other examples) which was only a one-time occurrence cannot be explained in these terms. @d1For He is most high.@d2 @hh<HEB>גָּאֹה גָּאָה@ee<ENG> is as Onkelos translates it. (An alternate explanation: The repetition [of @hh<HEB>גָאֹה גָּאָה@ee<ENG>] intends to convey that He did something that is impossible for a human to do. When he (the human) fights against his fellow man and overpowers him, he will throw him from his horse, whereas, here: “The horse @ITand@it its rider he threw into the sea.” And regarding anything that cannot be done by anyone else [except Him], the correct expression is @hh<HEB>גֵאוּת@ee<ENG> (@hh<HEB>גָאָה@ee<ENG>) as in: “For (@hh<HEB>גֵאוּת@ee<ENG>) gloriously has He done.” Similarly, everywhere in the @ITShira@it you will find repetitive expressions, [e.g.,] “the @ITstrength@it and @ITretribution@it of God was the cause of my deliverance.” [or:] “@ITAdonoy@it is the master of war @ITAdonoy@it is His name.” And so on [in the rest of the verses].  — From an old Rashi manuscript) Another explanation: For He is most high, He transcends all songs. and as much as I may praise him, there is still more in Him [to be praised] and not like a king of flesh and blood who is praised and does not possess [those attributes]. @d1Horse and its rider —@d2 both tied to each other and the water brought them up high and then threw them to the depths and, yet, they did not come apart.<QC>
@d1He threw.@d2 [@hh<HEB>רָמָה@ee<ENG> means] He threw. Similarly: “(@hh<HEB>וּרְמִיו@ee<ENG>) They were thrown into a burning furnace.” There is a Midrash Aggada: One verse states @hh<HEB>רָמָה@ee<ENG> — @ITthrown upwards,@it whereas another verse states @hh<HEB>יָרָה@ee<ENG> — @ITthrown downwards.@it This teaches that they went up to the heights and went down to the abyss; as in: “who (@hh<HEB>יָרָה@ee<ENG>) threw down the cornerstone?”  — from above to below. @p12@p2The strength and retribution of God.@p3 Onkelos translates [@hh<HEB>עָזִי וְזִמְרָת@ee<ENG> as:] “my might and my praise,” @hh<HEB>עָזִי@ee<ENG> being the same as @hh<HEB>עֻזִי@ee<ENG>, vocalized with a @ITshuruk@it and @hh<HEB>וְזִמְרָת@ee<ENG> being the same as @hh<HEB>וְזִמְרָתִי@ee<ENG>. But I wonder at the wording of the verse for there is none vocalized like it in Scripture, except in three places where it is placed next to “@hh<HEB>וְזִמְרָת@ee<ENG>,” whereas, everywhere else it is vocalized with a @ITshuruk,@it [e.g.] @hh<HEB>ה' עֻזִּי וּמָעֻזִּי@ee<ENG> [or:] @hh<HEB>עֻזּוֹ אֵלֶיךָ אֶשְׁמֹרָה@ee<ENG>. Similarly, every two-letter word that is vocalized with a @ITmalopum@it (@ITcholam@it), when it is extended by a third [suffix] letter, and the second one is not a @ITsheva@it or @ITchatef@it, then the first [letter] is vocalized with a @ITshuruk@it: For example: [The word] @hh<HEB>עֹז@ee<ENG> [becomes] @hh<HEB>עֻזִּי,@ee<ENG> [the word] @hh<HEB>רֹק@ee<ENG> [becomes] @hh<HEB>רֻקִּי@ee<ENG> @hh<HEB>חֹק@ee<ENG> [becomes] @hh<HEB>חֻקִּי@ee<ENG>, @hh<HEB>עֹל@ee<ENG> [becomes] @hh<HEB>עֻלּוֹ@ee<ENG> [e.g.] “@hh<HEB>יָסוּר עוּלּוֹ@ee<ENG>,” @hh<HEB>כֹּל@ee<ENG> [becomes] @hh<HEB>כֻּלֹוֹ@ee<ENG> [e.g.] @hh<HEB>וְשָׁלִישִׁים עַל כֻּלּוֹ@ee<ENG>. But [in] these three — @hh<HEB>עָזִי וְזִמְרָת@ee<ENG> which appears here, and the one in Yeshaiyahu (12, 2) and the one in Tehillim (118, 14) — [the letter @hh<HEB>ע'@ee<ENG>] is vocalized with a short @ITkamatz. @it And furthermore in none of them is it written @hh<HEB>וְזִמְרָתִי@ee<ENG>, but @hh<HEB>וְזִמְרָת@ee<ENG> and they are all followed by @hh<HEB>וַיְהִי לִי לִישׁוּעָה@ee<ENG> Therefore, I say, so that the wording of the text fit, that @hh<HEB>עָזִּי@ee<ENG> does not mean the same as @hh<HEB>עֻזִּי@ee<ENG> nor does @hh<HEB>וְזִמְרָת@ee<ENG> mean the same as @hh<HEB>וְזִמְרָתִי@ee<ENG>, but, rather, @hh<HEB>עָזִי@ee<ENG> is a noun as in: “@hh<HEB>הַיּשְׁבִי@ee<ENG> (the One Who dwells) in the heavens,” [or:] “@hh<HEB>שֹׁכְנִי@ee<ENG> (the one who dwells) in the cracks of the rock,” [or:] “@hh<HEB>שֹׁכְנִי@ee<ENG> (the one who dwells) in the bush.” And the following is the praise [conveyed here]: “The @hh<HEB>עֹז@ee<ENG> and the @hh<HEB>זִמְרָה@ee<ENG> of God were a deliverance for me,” and @hh<HEB>וְזִמְרָת@ee<ENG> is in the construct to the word @hh<HEB>ה' @ee<ENG>, as in: @hh<HEB>לְעֶזְרַת ה'@ee<ENG> — “to the help @ITof@it God,” [or:] @hh<HEB>בְּעֶבְרַת ה'@ee<ENG> — “by the anger @ITof@it God,” [or:] @hh<HEB>עַל דִּבְּרַת בְּנֵי הָאָדָם@ee<ENG> — “because of the speech @ITof@it men.” The word @hh<HEB>וְזִמְרָת@ee<ENG> is from the same root as “@hh<HEB>לֹא תִזְמוֹר@ee<ENG>” [or:] “@hh<HEB>זְמִיר עָרִיצִים@ee<ENG>” — “The cutting down of the evil ones” [@hh<HEB>זְמִיר@ee<ENG>] being a term for @ITmowing down@it and @IT cutting off.@it [Thus the meaning here is] “The strength and retribution of our God was a deliverance for us.” Do not wonder about the use of @hh<HEB>וַיְהִי@ee<ENG> (lit. “it will be”) that it does not say @hh<HEB>הָיָה@ee<ENG> (“it was”), for there @ITare@it verses that express themselves in this way, and the following is an example: “around the walls of the house, for the Temple and the Holy of Holies, (@hh<HEB>וַיַּעַשׂ@ee<ENG>) he made side-structures all around,” where it should have said @hh<HEB>עָשָׂה@ee<ENG> [instead of @hh<HEB>וַיַּעַשׂ @ee<ENG>].
