Here, too: “If you do mistreat him” conveys a threat, [i.e.] “In the end you will get yours.” Why? “when he cries out to Me, etc.” @p123@p2Your wives will become widows.@p3 From that which is implied by its saying: “and I will kill you,” would I not know myself that “your wives will be widows and your children will be orphans”? But the answer is that this is an additional curse: that the women will be bound as living widows — that there will be no witnesses who can testify to the death of their husbands and they will thus be prohibited from remarrying. And the children will be orphans, [i.e.] the Beis Din will not allow them to take possession of the fathers’ estates, for they do not know whether they died or were taken captive.<QC>
@p124@p2When you lend money.@p3 Rabbi Yishmael said: Each and every time @hh<HEB>אִם@ee<ENG> appears in the Torah [it refers to] something optional except in three instances of which this is one. @d1My people.@d2 [I.e. if you are faced with a choice of lending money to] one of My people or to a gentile, then “one of My people” has priority. [If the choice is between] a poor person and a rich one, then the poor person has priority. [If the choice is between] the poor of your city and the poor of another city, then the poor of your city have priority. And this is the way it is implied [in the text]: “When you lend money, [then,] lend it “to one of My people” and not to the gentile. And to which particular one of My people? “The poor one.” And to which particular poor one? To the one that is “with you.” (An alternate explanation of @hh<HEB>אֶת עַמִּי@ee<ENG>: “Do not act disrespectfully towards him when lending him money, for he is one of My people” — @d1the poor that is with you —@d2 [saying, in effect:] “Look at yourself as if you were the poor person.”) @d1Do not act toward him as a creditor.@d2 [I.e.] do not forcibly demand payment from him. If you know that he has no money do not appear towards him as if you have lent him money but, rather, as if you have not lent him, in other words: Do not embarrass him! @d1Interest.@d2 [@hh<HEB>נֶשֶׁךְ@ee<ENG> has the meaning of:] “interest” because it is (@hh<HEB>כִנְשִׁיכַת@ee<ENG>) like the bite of a snake which bites [inflicting] a small wound in his foot which he does not feel and, suddenly, it burgeons and swells [his entire body] till his head. So it is with interest: He (the borrower) feels nothing and it is not noticeable [at first] until the interest accumulates and causes him a great loss of money. @p125@p2If you take security.@p3 Wherever the term @hh<HEB>חֲבָלָה@ee<ENG> appears it does not refer to security [taken] at the time the loan is made but rather the security that is taken from the borrower when the loan comes due and he does not pay. (@hh<HEB>חָבוֹל תַּחְבֹּל@ee<ENG>: [The Torah asks] that you repeat taking the security even many times over. God [in effect] says: “How much do you owe Me? and see for yourself that your soul ascends to Me each and every night and gives an accounting of itself and thereby becomes beholden to Me, and yet I return it to you [every morning]. So shall you, too, take the security and return it, take it [again] and return it.) @d1You must return it till sunset.@d2 Return it to him [for] the entire day [so that he may have it] till sunset. And when the sun sets take it again until the morning of the following day. This verse deals with a garment worn by day which he (the borrower) does not need at night.<QC>
@p126@p2For this is his covering.@p3 This refers to the outer-garment. @d1His garment.@d2 This refers to the shirt [worn on the flesh]. @d1With what shall he lie down?@d2 This includes the bed-covering. @p127@p2Do not curse judges (or God).@p3 Here is the prohibition against cursing God as well as the prohibition against cursing judges.<QC>
@p128@p2Your fullness.@p3 [Meaning:] the obligation that was placed upon you when your grains reach their full ripeness. This refers to @hh<HEB>בִּכּוּרִים@ee<ENG> — “the first fruits.” @d1And your @ITterumah@it-offering.@d2 [@hh<HEB>וְדִמְעֲךָ@ee<ENG> refers to] the @ITterumah@it-offering, but, I do not know why it is called @hh<HEB>דֶמַע@ee<ENG>. @d1You must not delay.@d2 [Meaning:] Do not alter their order of separating them [from their fruits], by separating later what should be separated earlier, i.e., he may not [separate] the @ITterumah@it before [separating] the @ITbikkurim,@it or the tithe offering before the @ITterumah.@it @d1The first-born of your sons, you shall give to Me —@d2 by redeeming him for five @ITsela’im@it from the @ITkohein.@it But has [the Torah] not given this command elsewhere? But [the reason it is repeated here is] so as to juxtapose it to: “You must do likewise with your oxen,” [thereby teaching that] just as the first-born of man is redeemed after thirty days [from birth], as it is said: “And those that need to be redeemed, you shall redeem from a month old.” So, too, regarding the first-born of the smaller cattle: he (the owner) occupies himself with it for 30 days and, afterwards, gives it to the @ITkohein.@it @p129@p2Seven days it shall remain with its mother.@p3 This is a warning to the @ITkohein@it that if he wishes to hurry his sacrifice [of the @hh<HEB>בְּכוֹר@ee<ENG>] he may not rush to do so before the eighth day because it is “lacking in time” (i.e., premature). @d1On the eighth day you must give it to Me.@d2 One might think that it is obligatory on @ITthat@it day! [This is not so for] it states here: “the eighth day”, and it states further on: “and from the eighth day onward it shall be accepted.” Just as “the eighth day” mentioned later intends to declare [the animal] fit [for sacrifice] from the eighth day and on, so, too, “the eighth day” mentioned here intends to declare [the animal] fit from the eighth day and on. Hence, this is the intended meaning of it: “And on the eighth day you are permitted to give it to Me” (i.e., to sacrifice it). @p130@p2You shall be men of holiness to Me.@p3 [This is meant as a promise:] “If you will be holy and abstain from the abhorrence of [eating] @ITneveilah@it and @ITtereifah,@it then you are (@hh<HEB>לִי@ee<ENG>) Mine. But if not, then you are not Mine.<QC>
@d1Flesh that was torn off in the field.@d2 Actually the same applies within the house, but [the reason “the field” is mentioned is] the Torah speaks of what is common — the place where it is usual for animals to be torn apart [by wild beasts]. Similarly: “For he found her in the field,” Similarly: “One who is unclean as a result of a nocturnal emission,” where the same rule would apply to an emission during the day. The Torah just speaks of what is most common. However, Onkelos translates [@hh<HEB>וּבָשָׂר בַּשָֹדֶה טְרֵיפָה@ee<ENG>] as: “Flesh torn from a living animal,” i.e., flesh that was torn off as a result of an attack by a wolf or lion from a permitted beast, or from permitted cattle, while it was alive [“you must not eat”]. @d1Throw it to the dog.@d2 [You may sell it] also to a gentile. Or perhaps it refers only to a dog? The Torah therefore says regarding @ITneveilah:@it “or sell it to a gentile.” [Then we may assume] @ITa fortiori@it regarding @ITtereifah:@it that all benefits are permitted [including selling to a gentile]. If so, why does it state: “to the dog”?! To teach you that the dog gets more respect than he. And the verse [also] teaches that God does not withhold the reward that is due to any creature. [And the dog has a reward coming] for its said: “But against any of the B’nei Yisrael no dog wagged its tongue (i.e., barked),” so God said, “Give it its due reward.” @p11@p2Do not accept a false report.@p3 As Onkelos translates it: “Do not accept a false report.” This is an admonition directed to one who accepts (i.e., believes) slanderous talk, and to a judge, that he not listen to the claims of one party until the other party arrives. @d1Do not join hands with a wicked man.@d2 One who makes a demand on his fellow man by means of a false claim, and you promise him that you will be a corrupt witness. @p12@p2Do not follow the majority to do evil.@p3 Regarding this verse, there are various expositions by the Sages of Yisrael but they do not fit the arrangement of the verse: From here they derived that a guilty verdict should not be arrived at by a majority of one judge. And they explain the end of the verse as follows: “It must be decided by a majority,” [meaning] that if there are two more for conviction than there are for acquital, then decide the case according to them (the majority) for a guilty verdict. [According to this explanation] this verse is dealing with capital crimes. And they explained the middle of the verse as follows: @hh<HEB>וְלֹא תַעֲנֶה עַל רִיב@ee<ENG> [should be understood] [as if it were written] @hh<HEB>עַל רַב@ee<ENG> (against the elder), i.e., that one must not contradict a more eminent member of the court. It is for this reason that in capital cases we begin [hearing the opinions of the judges] from the side [benches], i.e., the youngest among them are the first ones asked to state their opinion. According to [the above] words of our Sages, the interpretation of the verse is as follows: @d1Do not follow the majority to do evil —@d2 to sentence [a defendant] to death on account of the judge [whose vote] causes those favoring conviction to outnumber those who vote for acquital.<QC>
@d1Do not dispute an elder —@d2 by veering from his opinion. It is because [the word @hh<HEB>רִיב@ee<ENG>] is missing the letter @ITyod@it that they explained it thus. @d1It must be decided by the majority.@d2 There is a majority to whose view you may lean. When is this so? When the majority for conviction outnumber by two those who vote to acquit. From that which is implied by what is said: “Do not follow the majority [of one] to do evil” I may derive: But, you may be with them (i.e., rule like them) if it is for good (i.e., to acquit the defendant). It is from here that they (the Sages) said: In case of capital offenses, the decision to acquit may be based on a majority of one but to convict there must be a majority of two. Onkelos translates [@hh<HEB>לֹא תַעֲנֶה עַל רִיב לִנְטוֹת@ee<ENG>]: “Do not refrain from conveying your opinion when asked about anything pertaining to a court case.” The Hebrew, according to Onkelos, should be explained as follows: @d1Do not respond in a dispute to lean.@d2 [I.e.] if your opinion is asked in a court case do not respond by leaning to one side in order to remove yourself from the dispute, but decide the matter based on the truth. [The above are the explanations of the Sages and Onkelos...] But I offer an explanation to fit the verse’s arrangement according to its plain meaning. And this is its interpretation: @d1Do not follow the majority to do evil.@d2 If you see wicked men distorting justice, do not say: “since they are the majority I may as well lean towards them.” @d1Do not respond, in dispute, to lean, etc.@d2 And if the defendant should ask you [your opinion] regarding that judgment, do not respond to him, about the dispute, with a statement that leans towards the majority opinion, thereby distorting the judgment from the truth. But rather render the decision as it really is and let the collar (i.e., the responsibility) hang from the necks of the majority.<QC>
@p13@p2Do not show favor.@p3 Do not show special regard for him by finding in his favor in a lawsuit, and say, “He is poor. I will find in his favor and show him regard.” @p15@p2If you see the donkey of your enemy, etc.@p3 Here you have the word @hh<HEB>כִּי@ee<ENG> being used to mean: “perhaps” (or “possibly”), which is one of the four meanings that the word @hh<HEB>כִּי@ee<ENG> is used to express. This is, then, how [the verse] is to be interpreted: It is possible that you might see his donkey lying under its burden — @d1and you might not want to help him?@d2 Incredulously, with amazement. @d1Make every effort to help him.@d2 This word @hh<HEB>עֲזִיבָה@ee<ENG> has the meaning of “helping.” Similarly: “aided and (@hh<HEB>עָזוּב@ee<ENG>) helped” Similarly: “(@hh<HEB>וַיַּעֲזְבוּ@ee<ENG>) They helped Jerusalem up to the wall,” [meaning:] they filled it with earth to help and support the strength of the wall. A similar [use of the word @hh<HEB>כִּי@ee<ENG> as “possibly”]: “@hh<HEB>כִּי@ee<ENG> you will say in your heart, ‘These nations are many more than I, etc.’” [meaning:] “Could you possibly say this?! incredulously, with amazement... [therefore] “Do not be afraid of them.” As for its midrashic explanation, this is the way our Sages expounded it: “If you will see ... you may refrain.” [This indicates that] there are times that you may refrain [from helping] and there are times that you must help. When does this apply? If he is an elderly man and it is beneath his dignity [then] @hh<HEB>וְחָדַלְתָּ@ee<ENG> — “you may refrain” [applies]. Or if it is the animal of a gentile and the load of a Jew [then] @hh<HEB>וְחָדַלְתָּ@ee<ENG> [applies]. @d1You should make every effort to help him —@d2 to unload the burden, [as Onkelos translates the words @hh<HEB>מֵעֲזוֹב לוֹ@ee<ENG>]: “from removing a load from it.” @p16@p2Your needy.@p3 [@hh<HEB>אֶבְיֹנְךָ@ee<ENG> is] from the root @hh<HEB>אבה@ee<ENG> — “longing,” for he is destitute and longs for anything good. @p17@p2Do not kill an innocent righteous man.@p3 From where do we derive [that in a case] where one has left the court after having been convicted and a person says: “I have evidence to suggest his innocence!” that we bring him (the defendant) back? Because the Torah states (addressing the court): “Do not kill an innocent man.” And though he may not be a righteous man for he has not yet been acquitted in court, nevertheless he is (@hh<HEB>נָקִי@ee<ENG>) innocent from a death verdict for you must try to vindicate him. And from where do we derive [that in a case] where one has left the court after having been found innocent, and a person says: “I have evidence to suggest his guilt,” that we do not bring him back to court? Because the Torah states: “Do not kill a righteous person!” And this [defendant] is considered righteous for he has been acquitted by the court — @d1for I will not acquit a wicked person.@d2 There is no need for you to bring him back for I will not vindicate him when I judge him,” [because even] if he has left your hands acquitted, I have many agents to put him to death, with that death to which he is actually liable.<QC>
@p18@p2Do not accept bribery.@p3 Even if you intend to judge truthfully. And it is certainly [prohibited] [when you take the bribe] to pervert justice. @p110@p2And gather its crops.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וְאָסַפְתָּ@ee<ENG> is] a term for “bringing into the house,” “You shall gather it into your house.” @p111@p2You must let it rest —@p3 from working [the fields] — @d1and abandon it —@d2 from eating [its crops] after “the time of removal.” An alternate explanation [of @hh<HEB>תִּשְׁמְטֶנָּה וּנְטַשְׁתָּהּ@ee<ENG>]: “You must let it rest” from real work, such as plowing and sowing; “and abandon it” from manuring and hoeing. @d1What they leave over, beasts of the field can eat.@d2 This is to compare the food of the poor to the food of the wild beast. @p113@p2Preserve everything that I have said to you.@p3 This intends to make every positive precept [as stringent] as a negative precept. @p127@p2I will bring panic.@p3 @hh<HEB>וְהַמֹּתִי@ee<ENG> is the same as @hh<HEB>וְהָמַמְתִּי@ee<ENG>. Onkelos translates it @hh<HEB>וֶאֱשַׁגֵּשׁ@ee<ENG> — “I will confuse.” This is true of any word whose verb-root has its last root-letter doubled, when it is conjugated in the @hh<HEB>פָּעַלְתִּי@ee<ENG> form. There are instances where the doubled letter is taken and given a @ITdagesh@it and vocalized with a @ITmelopum@it (@ITcholam@it), for example: @hh<HEB>וְהַמֹּתִי@ee<ENG> from the root: “(@hh<HEB>וְהָמַם@ee<ENG>) A confused sound was made by the wheel of his cart,” [or:] @hh<HEB>וְסַבּוֹתִי@ee<ENG> — “I circled” from the same root as: “(@hh<HEB>וְסָבַב@ee<ENG>) He circled Beth El,” @p121@p2Place the Testimony in the Ark.@p3 I do not know why it is repeated for it has already said: “You shall place in the ark, the Testimony!” It may be said that it comes to teach that when the ark is alone, without the cover, he should beforehand place the Testimony in it and only afterwards place the cover on it. We find [that this actually transpired] when he (Moshe) erected the @ITmishkon@it it states: “He placed the Testimony in the ark,” and afterward: “He placed the cover on the ark on top.” @p122@p2I will set My meetings.@p3 When I set a meeting for you to speak with you, it is at that place that I will set the meeting, so that I come there to speak to you. @d1And I will speak with you from above the cover.@d2 But elsewhere it says: “God spoke to him from the Tent of Meeting saying,” which refers to the part of the @ITmishkon@it that is outside of the partition. Consequently there are two verses contradicting each other. The third verse now comes and reconciles them: “When Moshe would come into the Tent of Meeting, etc. he would hear the Voice speaking to him from above the cover, etc.” [We thereby learn that the following actually took place:] Moshe would enter the @ITmishkon@it and as soon as he would come inside the entranceway the Voice would descend from the heavens to between the cherubim [on top of the cover] and it would emanate from there and be heard by Moshe in the Tent of Meeting. @d1All that which I will command you concerning the B’nei Yisrael.@d2 The letter @hh<HEB>ו'@ee<ENG> here is superfluous and of minor importance. There are many such instances in Scripture. This is how it should be interpreted: “@ITAnd@it that which I will speak to you there: all that I will command you will be concerning the B’nei Yisrael.” @p123@p2Its height.@p3 I.e., the height of its legs together with the thickness of the table. @p124@p2A gold crown-like rim.@p3 This is a symbol of the royal crown for “the table” represents wealth and greatness, as people express it: “a royal table.” @p125@p2A ledge.@p3 As Onkelos translates it @hh<HEB>גְדַנְפָא@ee<ENG> — “a protruding ledge.” Regarding this there is a dispute among the Sages of Israel. There are those who say [that the @ITmisgeres@it] was at the top around the @ITshulchan@it like the vertical rims that [are to be found]@n1A@n2 at the edges of tables of the aristocracy (@ITsee illustration 2 on page 140@it). There are others who say that [the @ITmisgeres@it] was fastened at the bottom from one leg [of the @ITshulchan@it] to another leg@n1B@n2 on all four sides of the @ITshulchan,@it and the flat board of the @ITshulchan@it@n1C@n2 rested on that @ITmisgeres.@it @d1And make a gold crown-like rim around the ledge.@d2 It is the same crown-like rim mentioned above. Here it clarifies for you that it was put onto the ledge. @p127@p2The rings shall be against the ledge.@p3 They were fastened to the legs opposite the edges of the ledge.@n1D@n2 @d1Receptacles for the poles.@d2 I.e., these rings shall be receptacles into which the poles will be inserted. @d1Houses —@d2 @ITfor the purpose@it of being receptacles. @d1Receptacles.@d2 As Onkelos translates it: “a place for the poles.” @p128@p2It will be carried by them.@p3 [@hh<HEB>נִשָֹּׂא@ee<ENG> is in] the @hh<HEB>נִפְעַל@ee<ENG> conjugation, i.e., the @ITshulchan@it will be carried by them (the poles).<QC>
@p129@p2Make its dishes and its spoons.@p3 “Its dishes” refers to its forms which were made like the shape of the bread. The bread was made in the shape of a [topless] box that was open on two of its sides@n1A@n2 (@ITsee illustration 3 on page 140@it). It had a bottom underneath. It was bent upwards at both ends,@n1B@n2 [forming] something like walls.@n1C@n2 It is for this reason that it is called @hh<HEB>לֶחֶם הַפָּנִים@ee<ENG> — “the bread of the faces,” because it has a “face”@n1D@n2 “looking” in two directions towards the sides of the House (i.e., mishkon) in one direction as well as in the other direction. It was placed lengthwise across the width of the shulchan with its walls standing vertically lined up with the edge of the shulchan. A golden form and a metal form were made for it. It was baked in the metal one and upon removing it from the oven it was placed in the golden one until the following day, which was Shabbos, when it was set up on the shulchan. That form is called @hh<HEB>קְעָרָה@ee<ENG> — “dish.” “Its spoons” are [known] as @hh<HEB>בָּזִיכִין@ee<ENG> (ladles) into which the frankincense was put. There were two for the two fistfuls of frankincense that were placed on the two piles, as is stated: “You shall put upon the pile pure frankincense.” @d1Its half-hollow tubes.@d2 They are similar to hollow, half tubes which are split along their length.@n1E@n2 There were similar to those made of gold and three were placed in a row atop each bread so that the next bread [above] should sit on top of these tubes and they would separate one bread from another so that air would enter between them and prevent them from spoiling. In Arabic anything hollow is called @hh<HEB>קסוא@ee<ENG>. @d1And its supports.@d2 Onkelos translates it @hh<HEB>וּמְכִילָתֵיהּ@ee<ENG>, which are supports.@n1F@n2 They were like golden pegs standing on the ground, rising up very high above the shulchan to the same height as the bread pile. They were notched in six places one above the other@n1G@n2 and the ends of the tubes that were between the breads would be supported by these notches so that the weight of the upper breads not weigh down on the lower ones which would cause them to break. The term @hh<HEB>מְכִילָתֵיהּ@ee<ENG> [quoted above from Onkelos] [means] its supports, as in: “I am exhausted from (@hh<HEB>הָכִיל@ee<ENG>) carrying it.” But I do not know how the term @hh<HEB>מְנַקִיוֹת@ee<ENG> applies to supports. There are among the Sages of Yisrael who say that @hh<HEB>קְשׂוֹתָיו@ee<ENG> is a reference to supports because they keep it (the bread) hard and keep it firm so that it does not break. Whereas the term @hh<HEB>מְנַקִּיוֹתָיו@ee<ENG> refers to the tubes, so called because they (@hh<HEB>מְנַקִּין@ee<ENG>) clean it (the bread) so that it does not spoil. But, Onkelos who translates it @hh<HEB>מְכִילָתֵיהּ@ee<ENG> agrees with the [Sage] who says that @hh<HEB>מְנַקִּיוֹת@ee<ENG> are supports. @d1By which they shall be covered.@d2 [Meaning:] by which they shall be covered. It is regarding its tubes that @hh<HEB>אֲשֶׁר יֻסַּךְ@ee<ENG> is said, for they (the tubes) were as a covering over it (the bread). Similarly, elsewhere it is said: @hh<HEB>וְאֵת קְשׂוֹת הַנָּסֶךְ@ee<ENG> — “and the tubes for the @hh<HEB>נָסֶךְ @ee<ENG>” — and both @hh<HEB>יֻסַּךְ@ee<ENG> and @hh<HEB>נָסֶךְ@ee<ENG> have the meaning of “covering.” @p130@p2Showbread.@p3 So called because it has @hh<HEB>פָּנִים@ee<ENG> (faces) as I have explained. The amount of bread and the order of piling it up are explained in the @ITparsha@it @hh<HEB>אֱמֹר אֶל הַכֹּהֲנִים@ee<ENG>. @p131@p2The Menorah must be made by hammering.@p3 It must @ITnot@it be made in sections. [That is] its branches or lamps must not be made as individual parts and afterwards attached as is done by smiths [through a process] called @ITsolder@it in Old French (to solder), but, rather, it should all originate from one piece, hammered with a hammer and cut with special tools, thereby separating the branches in two directions. @d1Hammering.@d2 Onkelos translates it @hh<HEB>נְגִיד@ee<ENG>, a term meaning: “drawn out,” for he draws out the parts from the mass [of gold] in two directions by hammering with the hammer. The term @hh<HEB>מִקְשָׁה@ee<ENG> means “hammering with a hammer,” @ITbatedic@it (hammered) in Old French, as in: “(@hh<HEB>נָקְשָׁן@ee<ENG>) they banged against each other.” @d1The menorah must be made.@d2 [It shall be made] by itself. Because Moshe had difficulties with it, God said to him: “Throw the mass [of gold] into the fire and it will form itself.” Therefore it does not say @hh<HEB>תַּעֲשֶׂה@ee<ENG> — “you must make.” @d1Its base.@d2 This refers to the “leg” at the bottom which was made in a shape similar to a box from which the three legs protruded downwards. @d1Its shaft.@d2 [This refers to] its middle branch which rose from the middle of the base vertically upwards. At the top of it was the middle lamp, made similar to a cup into which oil was poured and a wick was placed. @d1Its cups.@d2 They were like goblets which are made of glass long and narrow called @ITmadernes@it [in Old French]. These were made of gold and they protruded and stood out from each branch according to the number given in Scripture. They were there only as ornaments. @d1Its knobs.@d2 They were like round apples around [the branch], projecting outward around the middle branch, in the manner that candelabras are made for the aristocracy, called @ITpomels@it [in Old French]. Their number is given in this @ITparsha,@it as to how many knobs protrude from it and how much space between one knob and another. @d1And its flowers.@d2 Engravings made in the shape of flowers. @d1Shall be of it.@d2 [I.e.] all hammered out from the piece of mass and not made separately and then attached. @p132@p2Shall extend from its sides —@p3 in each direction diagonally going upwards until [they reach] the height of the menorah, which is the middle branch. They extend outward from the middle branch, one above the other. The bottom one is the longest one and the one above it is shorter and the top one is even shorter. [This was so] because the height at their top was the same as the height of the seventh middle branch, from which extended the six branches.<QC>
@p133@p2Engraved.@p3 [@hh<HEB>מְשֻׁקָּדִים@ee<ENG> is] as Onkelos translates it [@hh<HEB>מְצַיְירִין@ee<ENG>]: They were engraved in the manner that silver and gold vessels are made, called @ITnieler@it (engraving) [in Old French]. @d1And three cups —@d2 protruding from each of the branches. @d1A knob and a flower —@d2 was on each branch. @p134@p2The [shaft of the] menorah shall have four cups.@p3 In the body (center) of the menorah there were four cups, one protruding under the branches and three above from where the branches vented out, those that extended outward from its sides. @d1Engraved in almond shape patterns with their knob and flowers.@d2 This is one of the five verses [in the Torah] in which [an adjective] is left undecided. [Here] it is not known whether [it should read]: @hh<HEB>גְּבִיעִים מְשֻׁקָּדִים@ee<ENG> or @hh<HEB>מְשֻׁקָּדִים כַּפְתֹּרֶיהָ וּפְרָחֶיהָ@ee<ENG>. @p135@p2A knob under the two branches.@p3 From the knob, the branches extended from the two sides in opposite directions. In the @ITBeraisa d’Meleches Hamishkon@it we learned as follows: The menorah was altogether 18 @ITtefachim@it high. [We arrive at this as follows:] 3 @ITtefachim@it for the legs and the [lowest] flower@n1A@n2 (@ITsee illustrations on page 141@it). This is the flower that is mentioned together with the base, about which it says: “down to its base and its flower.” 2 @ITtefachim@it [above that were] unadorned@n1B@n2 1 @ITtefach@it [above that was] a cup,@n1C@n2 [i.e., one] of the four cups [mentioned in v. 34] as well as a knob and a flower [each of which was one] of the two knobs and two flowers that are mentioned with the menorah proper (i.e., the center branch), about which it is said (in v. 34): “the knobs and flowers were engraved,” from which we learn that the [center] branch had two knobs and two flowers besides the three knobs from which the [six] branches extended. [Regarding the latter three knobs] it says [here]: “and a knob under the two branches, etc.” 2@ITtefachim@it [above that were] unadorned.@n1D@n2 1@ITtefach@it [above that was] a knob,@n1E@n2 from which two branches extended in two directions.@n1F@n2 They extended upwards to a height equal to the [center] of the menorah. 1 @ITtefach@it [above that] was unadorned@n1G@n2 1 @ITtefach@it [above that was] a knob@n1H@n2 from which two branches extended.@n1I@n2 1 @ITtefach@it [above that] was unadorned.@n1J@n2 1 @ITtefach@it [above that] was a knob@n1K@n2 from which two branches extended upward to a height equal to [the center of] the menorah.@n1L@n2 2 @ITtefachim@it [above that] were unadorned.@n1M@n2 3 @ITtefachim@it now remained@n1N@n2, in which there were three cups (= 1 @ITtefach@it), and one knob (=1 @ITtefach@it) and one flower (=1 @ITtefach@it). Consequently the total number of cups was 22: 18 for the six branches, [since there were] three for each one; and 4 in the menorah proper (i.e., the center branch), giving you a total of 22. And the total number of knobs was 11: 6 for the six branches (one for each) and 3 in the menorah proper (i.e., the center branch) from which the branches extended. and an additional 2 in the menorah proper, about which it is said (v. 34): “its knobs were engraved” [which indicates 2] [since] the minimum of anything plural is 2. One of these [knobs] was at the bottom near the base and the other one was in the three upper @ITtefachim,@it together with the three cups. It (the menorah) had nine flowers: 6 for the six branches (one for each), as it is said (v. 33): “in each branch there was a knob and a flower,” and 3 for the menorah proper (i.e., the center branch). [Two of these are mentioned] when it is said “its knobs and @ITflowers@it were engraved” and the minimum of “flowers” is 2, and [an additional one] mentioned in the @ITparsha@it @hh<HEB>בְּהַעֲלֹתְךָ@ee<ENG>: “down to its base and its flower.” If you study the above @ITberaisa@it carefully you will find them as they were enumerated, each in its proper place.<QC>
@p137@p2Its lamps.@p3 They were a kind of cups into which the oil and the wicks were put. @d1So that they shine toward it.@d2 I.e., make the mouths of the six lamps, which were at the top of the branches which extended from its (the menorah’s) sides, turned toward the middle one, so that when the lamps were lit they would shine @hh<HEB>אֶל עֵבֶר פָּנֶיהָ@ee<ENG>. [That is,] their shine would be turned towards the front side of the middle branch. which is the menorah proper.@n1O@n2 @p138@p2Its tongs.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וּמַלְקָחֶיהָ@ee<ENG>] are the tongs which were made to pluck the wicks from the oil to set them up, and to pull them into the mouths of the lamps. Since they are used for “taking” (i.e., plucking) they are called @hh<HEB>מַלְקָחַיִם@ee<ENG> (from @hh<HEB>לְקָח@ee<ENG> — “take”). The word @hh<HEB>צִבְתָהָא@ee<ENG> with which Onkelos translates is from the word @hh<HEB>צְבָת@ee<ENG> — @ITtenálies@it in Old French. @d1And its scoops.@d2 They were like small receptacles which were used to rake out the ashes from the lamp every single morning, when he would clean the lamps from the ashes of the wicks which had burned during the night and were extinguished. The word @hh<HEB>מַחְתָּה@ee<ENG> is @ITpuisedoire@it in Old French, as in: “(@hh<HEB>לַחְתּוֹת@ee<ENG>) to rake fire from the oven.” @p139@p2A @ITkikar@it of pure gold.@p3 Its weight, including all its vessels, should be only a @ITkikar,@it no more and no less. The @ITkikar@it [used to measure] mundane things was the equivalent of sixty @ITmanehs,@it whereas the one [used to measure] holy things was double that — 120 @ITmaneh.@it A @ITmaneh@it is the equivalent of a @ITlitra@it which is used to weigh silver according to the weight of Cologne. This is equal to one hundred @ITzehuvim@it (dinars), equal to twenty five @ITsela’im,@it a @ITsela@it being four @ITzehuvim@it (dinars). @p140@p2Observe and make them.@p3 See here on the mountain the pattern which I show you. This tells us that Moshe had difficulties with the constructing of the menorah until God showed him a menorah [model] made of fire.<QC>
@d1That will be shown to you.@d2 As Onkelos translates it: “that you will be shown in the mountain.” If it were vocalized @hh<HEB>מַרְאֶה@ee<ENG> with a @ITpatach,@it its meaning would be: “you will show to others.” But, now that it is vowelized with a @ITkametz@it (@hh<HEB>מָרְאֶה@ee<ENG>), its meaning is: “that you will be shown” — that others show you. @p11@p2You shall make the @BImishkon@BO out of ten drapes —@p3 to serve as a roof and partitions on the outside of the planks, for the drapes were hung behind them to cover them. @d1Fine twined linen, greenish-blue, dark red and crimson.@d2 Thus you have four kinds [of material] together in each and every thread, one of linen and three of wool. Each and every thread was made six-fold. Hence the four materials when woven together made each strand consist of 24 threads@n1A@n2 (@ITsee illustration on page 142@it). @d1Figures of cherubim, the work of a craftsman.@d2 Cherubim were formed in them (the drapes) through weaving, and not through embroidery which is done through needlework. But, rather, by weaving on two sides, one image on one side and another image on the other side, a lion on this side and an eagle on the other side, just as silken belts are woven [nowadays], which are called @ITfeises@it (woven images) in Old French. @p13@p2Shall be joined together.@p3 They were sewn together with a needle one beside the other — each of five [drapes] separately. @d1Together.@d2 This is the way Scripture speaks (i.e., @hh<HEB>אִשָּׁה אֶל אֲחוֹתָהּ@ee<ENG>) of an object in the feminine gender. And when the object is in the masculine gender it says: @hh<HEB>אִישׁ אֶל אָחִיו@ee<ENG>, as it states regarding the cherubim: “they shall face one another (lit. one man to another).” @p14@p2Loops.@p3 @ITLaçols@it (loops) in Old French. Onkelos, too, translates it thus: @hh<HEB>עֲנוּבִין@ee<ENG> from the word @hh<HEB>עֲנִיבָה@ee<ENG> (loop or bow).@n1B@n2 (@ITSee illustration on page 142@it)<QC>
@d1At the end of the group.@d2 [I.e.] on that drape which is at the end of the group. The group of the five [joined] drapes is referred to as @hh<HEB>חוֹבָרֶת@ee<ENG>. @d1Do the same on the edge of the drape which is at the end of the second group.@d2 [I.e.] on that drape which is at the end. @hh<HEB>קִיצוֹנָה@ee<ENG> is from @hh<HEB>קָצֶה@ee<ENG> (end), that is, at @ITthe end@it of the group. @p15@p2The loops shall be opposite one another.@p3 Take care that you make the loops exactly equidistant from one another. And the measurements used for one drape shall be the same as in the other one, so that when you spread out one group next to the other the loops of one drape [should] line up exactly opposite the loops of the other. And that is the meaning of @hh<HEB>מַקְבִּילוֹת@ee<ENG> — “one opposite the other” [as indicated by] Onkelos’ translation of @hh<HEB>נֶגֶד@ee<ENG> (@ITopposite@it) is @hh<HEB> לָקֳבֵל@ee<ENG>. [Each of] the drapes was 28 [@ITamohs@it] long and four [@ITamohs@it] wide. And when the five drapes were joined together their width totaled 20 [@ITamohs@it]. The same was true for the second group [of drapes]. The [interior] length of the @ITmishkon@it was 30 @ITamohs@it@n1A@n2 (@ITsee illustration on page 143@it) from east to west, as it is said: “twenty planks for the southern side” and similarly for the northern side, each plank being 1½ @ITamohs@it wide.@n1B@n2 So you have 30 [@ITamohs@it] east to west. The [interior] width of the @ITmishkon,@it from north to south, was ten @ITamohs@it,@n1C@n2 as it is said: “For the western side of the @ITmishkon@it, etc., and make two planks for the corners.” Hence [the eight planks] equaled ten [@ITamohs@it of interior. [For two of the @ITamohs@it covered the thickness of the plank on each side]. I will explain these verses in their places. The drapes were placed [so that] their length was across the width of the @ITmishkon@it, ten of their middle @ITamohs@it covering the interior of the width of the @ITmishkon@it. An additional @ITamoh@it on each side [covered] the thickness of the top of the planks@n1D@n2 whose width was one @ITamoh@it. This left 16 amohs: eight [@ITamohs@it] on the northern side and eight [@ITamohs@it] on the southern side covered the height of the planks which were ten @ITamohs@it high. Consequently, the bottom two [@ITamohs@it of the planks] remained uncovered. The width of the drapes [totaled] 40 @ITamohs@it when they were all joined — 20 @ITamohs@it for each group. Thirty of the total [@ITamohs@it] covered the interior of the length of the @ITmishkon@it; one @ITamoh@it corresponded to the thickness of the tops of the planks on the western side and an additional @ITamoh@it [was needed] to cover the thickness of the pillars on the eastern side, — for there were no planks on the eastern side, just five pillars@n1E@n2 from which the screen was spread and hung by their (the pillars’) hooks as a sort of a curtain. Consequently there remained eight @ITamohs@it which hung in back of the @ITmishkon@it on the western side, leaving the bottom 2 @ITamohs@it uncovered. The above I found in the @ITBeraisa of 49 midos@it. However, in @ITMaseches@it Shabbos (98b) [we find]: The drapes did not cover the eastern beams, and nine @ITamohs@it hung in back of the @ITmishkon.@it What is written in this section supports this, [for it states:] “Place the @ITparoches@it under the clasps,” and if, in fact, it were as is stated in the @ITBeraisa@it it would result in the @ITparoches@it being a distance of one @ITamoh@it westward from the clasps.<QC>
@p16@p2Golden Clasps.@p3 @ITFermeilz@it (hooks or clasps) in Old French. One of their ends is inserted into [one of the] loops of one of the groups [of drapes] and their other end into the loops of the other group [of drapes,] thereby joining them through them. @p17@p2Drapes of goats’ [hair].@p3 [@hh<HEB>עִזִּים@ee<ENG> here means:] of goats’ hair. @d1For a covering over the @BImishkon.@BO@d2 I.e., to be spread over the lower drapes. @p18@p2Thirty @BIamohs — @BO@p3 so that when they are placed lengthwise across the width of the @ITmishkon,@it in the same way he placed the first @hh<HEB>יְרִיעוֹת@ee<ENG>], it follows that they will overhang an [additional] @ITamoh@it on this side and an @ITamoh@it on the other side, so as to cover one of the two @ITamohs@it of the planks that remained uncovered. @p19@p2Let the sixth drape overhang.@p3 [I.e.] the additional one of these upper [drapes], over and above the lower [drapes]. — @d1@bo[...shall overhang] @BObefore the entrance of the tent.@d2 Half of its width hung and folded over the curtain on the eastern side, before the entrance, similar to a modest bride whose face is covered by a veil. @p112@p2The overlapping excess of the drapes of the tent — @p3 over and above the [lower] curtains of the @ITmishkon.@it The “drapes of the Tent” refer to the upper ones made of goats’ hairs which are referred to [in the Torah] as @hh<HEB>אֹהֶל@ee<ENG>, as it says, regarding them: “to be an @hh<HEB>אֹהֶל@ee<ENG> over the @ITmishkon.@it” Wherever @hh<HEB>אֹהֶל@ee<ENG> is used regarding them (the upper drapes) it means a cover [and not the literal: “tent”] for they cover and shield over the lower ones. They were longer than the lower [drapes] by [the width of] half a drape on the western side for half of the eleventh drape, which was the one in excess [of the ten lower drapes], folded [downward] toward the entrance of the Tent. Consequently there remained two @ITamohs@it — half its width — in excess of the width of the lower [curtains]. @d1@bo[These two @ITamohs@it] @BOShall overlap the back of the @BImishkon@BO — @it@d2 to cover the two @ITamohs@it of the planks that remained uncovered [by the lower drapes]. @d1The back of the @BImishkon.@it@d2 This refers to the western side, [called the “back” of the @ITmishkon@it] because the entrance was on the eastern side which is its (the @ITmishkon@it’s) “front.” The northern and southern sides are called “the right and left sides.” @p113@p2The [extra] @BIamoh@BO on one side and the [extra] @BIamoh@BO on the other side —@p3 on the northern and southern sides. @d1That were in excess of the length of the drapes of the tent —@d2 that they were in excess over the length of the “drapes of the @ITmishkon@it” (the lower ones) — by two @ITamohs@it — @d1shall overlap the sides of the @BImishkon@BO.@d2 [I.e.] the northern and southern sides as I explained above (v. 12, @hh<HEB>ד"ה אחורי@ee<ENG>). The Torah thereby teaches a lesson in propriety: that a person should be protective of that which is beautiful. @p114@p2A covering for the tent.@p3 For the roof made of the drapes of goats’ hairs, make an additional covering out of red-dyed ram’s skins. And above that still another covering made out of @ITtachash@it skins. These coverings covered only the roof. Their length was 30 @ITamohs@it and their width ten. This is the opinion of Rabbi Nechemiah. However, according to the opinion of Rabbi Yehudah there was only one covering, half of it [made] of red-dyed rams’ skins and the other half of @ITtachash@it skins. @p115@p2Make the planks.@p3 It should have said: “Make planks,” as it says regarding each and every thing. Why, then, “@ITthe@it planks”? From those [particular ones] that were prepared and designated for this purpose. [For] our father Yaakov planted cedar trees in Egypt and when he died he commanded his sons to carry them up with them: when they would leave Egypt, and said to them: “God will, at a time in the future, command you to construct a @ITmishkon@it in the desert, made of acacia wood; see to it that you have them ready.” This is what the liturgist [meant when he] composed in his liturgical composition: “Rapidly did grow the seedlings of the diligent to become the cedars used for the beams of our House” for they were diligent [that the cedars] be ready in their possession in advance. @d1Out of acacia wood, standing.@d2 @ITEstantives@it (upright) in Old French. [I.e.,] the length of the planks should be placed upright in the walls of the @ITmishkon.@it Do not make the walls with the planks horizontal, [by placing] the width of the planks along the height of the walls one plank on top of another. @p116@p2Each plank shall be ten @BIamohs@BO long.@p3 From this we learn that the height of the @ITmishkon@it was ten @ITamohs.@it @d1And one and one half @BIamohs@BO shall be the width. From this we learn that the length of the @ITmishkon,@it [which corresponds] to the twenty planks that were on the northern and southern sides, running from east to west, was thirty @ITamohs.@it @p117@p2Each plank shall have two pegs.@p3 He cut out the bottom of the plank at its middle to the height of an @ITamoh.@it He left one fourth of its width on one side and another fourth of its width on the other side and these [protrusions are called] the pegs. The cut out part [was equal to] half the width of the plank in the center. These pegs were inserted into the sockets which were hollow. The sockets were one @ITamoh@it high@n1B@n2 and forty of them were placed in a line one next to another. The pegs of the plank that were inserted into the opening of the sockets were cut out on three of their sides. The width of the part that was cut away correspond to the thickness of the edge of the socket so that the plank would cover the entire top of the socket. For if this were not so it would have resulted in a gap between one plank and another equal to the thickness of the edges of two sockets which would have separated between them (the planks)@n1A@n2 (@ITsee illustration 2 on page 144@it). This is what is meant when it is said: “They must be coupled together [to fit exactly];” the sides of the pegs should be cut out so that the planks be flush next to one another.@n1B@n2 @d1Parallel.@d2 [@hh<HEB>מְשֻׁלָּבוֹת@ee<ENG> means:] made like the (@hh<HEB>שְׁלִיבוֹת@ee<ENG>) rungs of a ladder, separated from each other and planed at their ends so as to be inserted into the opening of the socket, like a (@hh<HEB>שְׁלִיבָה@ee<ENG>) rung which is inserted in the hole of the upright beams of a ladder. @d1To each other.@d2 [I.e., the pegs] were lined up [exactly] one opposite the other; [meaning] that their cut away sections should be exactly alike. The measurement of one should be the same as the other so that no two pegs should be [misaligned], one pulled more towards the inside while the other is pulled more to the outside in relation to the thickness of the plank which was one @ITamoh.@it Onkelos’ translation of @hh<HEB>יָדוֹת@ee<ENG> is “hinges” because they are similar to the hinges of a door which are inserted in the holes of the threshold.<QC>
@p118@p2For the southern side.@p3 The form of the word @hh<HEB>פֵּאָה@ee<ENG> here (@hh<HEB>לִפְאַת@ee<ENG>) does not have the [usual] meaning of “corner” [or “end”], but, rather, the entire side is called @hh<HEB>פֵּאָה@ee<ENG>, as Onkelos translates it: “for the southern side.” @p122@p2For the [west] end.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וּלְיַרְכְּתֵי@ee<ENG>] has the meaning of “end,” as Onkelos translates it: “And for its end.” Since the entrance is on the eastern side therefore the eastern side is called “the front” and the western side, “the back.” This is why it is referred to as “the end” since the front is “the beginning.” @d1Make six planks.@d2 Hence nine @ITamohs@it is its width. @p123@p2Make two planks for the corners.@p3 One for the northwestern corner and one for southwestern [corner]. All the eight planks were in one row, but these two [in the corners] did not enclose the interior of the @ITmishkon@it except that one half @ITamoh@it of this one (plank) and one half @ITamoh@it of the other were visible in the interior [of the @ITmishkon@it] so as to make the total width ten [@ITamohs@it]. The remaining @ITamoh@it of each [plank] was lined up against the @ITamoh@it thickness of the northern and southern @ITmishkon@it planks so that the ends on the outside would be even. @p124@p2They must be coupled together.@p3 All the planks must be flush with each other @d1at the bottom,@d2 so that no gap be formed by the thickness of the edge of any two [adjacent] sockets between them (the planks) to separate them from one another. This is what I meant when I explained that the “hinges” of the pegs must be cut out around their sides so that the width of the plank will protrude at its side beyond the pegs of the plank so as to cover the edge of the socket. The same [applies] to its adjacent plank resulting in their being coupled together. Each corner plank of the western row was cut away in its width in its thickness [lined up] opposite the cut-away part of the planks of the northern and southern sides so that the sockets will not cause a separation between them. @d1And they must also be coupled together evenly.@d2 [@hh<HEB>תַמִּים@ee<ENG> has] the same meaning as @hh<HEB>תוֹאֲמִים@ee<ENG> (coupled). @d1On the top.@d2 Of each plank.<QC>
@d1With one ring.@d2 Each and every plank was cut out at the top in its width@n1A@n2 (@ITsee illustration 3 on page 144@it). Two cut-outs at the two ends [of the width] were sufficient [to hold] the thickness of a ring. He inserts them (the two planks) into one ring resulting in its being flush with the adjacent planks.@n1B@n2 But I do not know about these rings whether they were fixed [to the planks] or whether they were removable. As for the plank at the corner, the rings were along the thickness of the southernmost and northernmost planks. Then the top of the corner plank along the western row would be inserted into it (the ring), resulting in the two walls being joined. @d1So must it be done with the two.@d2 [I.e.,] the two planks at the corners; the plank at the [western] end of the northern side with the western plank [adjacent to it]. The same applied to both corners.<QC>
@p125@p2Thus there will be eight planks.@p3 These are the same ones mentioned above: “make six planks... And make two planks for the corners,” thereby resulting in eight planks for the row on the western side. The following is stated in the part dealing with the order of the planks in the [@ITBeraisa@it] @ITd’Meleches Hamishkon:@it The sockets were made hollow. The planks were cut out at the bottom [leaving] a quarter [of the width] on one side and a quarter on the other side. Half the width was cut out in the middle thereby forming two pegs that were similar to two @hh<HEB>חֲמוּקִין@ee<ENG> (legs — (S.H.)) (To me it seems that it should read: “similar to two @hh<HEB>חֲוָוקִין@ee<ENG>”) [meaning:] similar to two rungs of a ladder that are set apart from each other, which were planed down so as to enable them to be inserted into the opening of the socket, as a rung which is inserted into the holes of the upright boards of a ladder. This is what is meant by @hh<HEB>מְשׁוּלָּבוֹת@ee<ENG> — made similar to a (@hh<HEB>שְׁלִיבָה@ee<ENG>) rung. They are inserted into the two sockets, as it is said: “two sockets [under one plank] and two sockets [under the other plank.” Then the top of the plank is cut out, the width of a finger on one side and the width of a finger on the other side and they are inserted into one golden ring so that they not separate from each other, as it is said: “They must be coupled together, etc.” The above is the Mishnah (i.e., Beraisa), and I have set forth its explanation above according to the order of the verses.<QC>
@p126@p2Bars.@p3 As Onkelos translates it @hh<HEB>עַבְּרִין@ee<ENG> and in Old French @ITesbars@it (bolts). @d1Five for the planks of one wall.@d2 These five are actually three, but [they are called five] only because the upper and lower bars are [each] made of two pieces. The one extends till the middle of the wall and the other extends till the middle of the wall. One is inserted into the rings on one side and the other is inserted into the rings on the other side, until they reach each other. Consequently the upper and lower ones are two which are made of four. But regarding the middle [bar], its length was equal to the whole wall from one end of the wall till the other end, as it is said: “The middle bar, etc. extended from one end to the other.” For the upper and lower [bars] had rings on the planks through which they were inserted, two rings for each board@n1A@n2 (@ITsee illustration 1 on page 145@it) placed at three equidistant places within the ten @ITamohs@it of the height of the plank. One section [of the plank] was above the upper ring@n1@n2 and one section was beneath the lowest one,@n1C@n2 each section being one fourth of the length of the plank. Two [more] sections were between the [upper and lower] rings.@n1D@n2 This was in order that all the rings would be lined up one across from the other. However, the middle bar had no rings, but the planks were bored through their thickness, and it (the bar) was inserted through the holes@n1E@n2 which were lined up one exactly opposite the other. This is what is meant when it is said: “[The middle bar shall go] through the planks.” The upper and lower bars that were on the northern and southern sides, the length of each was 15 @ITamohs@it whereas the middle one was 30 @ITamohs.@it That is what is meant by: “[The middle bar ... will extend] from one end to the other,” from east to west. As for the five bars on the western side: the length of the upper and lower ones was six @ITamohs,@it whereas the middle one’s length was 12 [@ITamohs@it], corresponding to the width of the eight planks. This is how it is explained in the [@ITBeraisa@it] @ITd’Meleches Hamishkon.@it @p129@p2To hold the bars.@p3 The rings which you will make for them will be housings [for the purpose] of inserting the bars into them. @d1You shall overlay the bars with gold.@d2 This does not mean to imply that the gold was attached onto the bars for they were not coated with anything. But, rather, he would attach to the plank something akin to two gold tubes, similar to two sections of a hollow-cane. He fastened them near the rings in both directions. Their length took up the entire width of the plank, from the ring to one side and from it (the ring) to the other side. The bar was inserted into it (the tube) and from it into the ring and from the ring to the other opening. Consequently the bars [appeared to be] coated with gold when they were attached to the planks. These bars projected to the outside [Hence] the rings and the tubes were not visible inside the @ITmishkon,@it but the whole wall was smooth on the inside. @p130@p2You shall erect the @ITmishkon.@it@p3 “After it is completed, erect it!” @d1Shown to you on the mountain —@d2 before this (i.e., before setting it up), for later on I shall teach you and show you the proper order for setting it up.<QC>
@p131@p2Paroches.@p3 [@hh<HEB>פָּרוֹכֶת@ee<ENG>] has the meaning of partition. The @ITparoches@it was ten @ITamohs@it long along the width of the @ITmishkon@it and ten @ITamohs@it wide corresponding to the height of the planks. [This is indicated] by what is said: “Place the @ITparoches@it under the clasps,” which joined the two groups of the curtains of the @ITmishkon.@it The width of the group was 20 @ITamohs@it and when it was spread over the top of the @ITmishkon@it from the entrance westward, it ended two thirds of the way into the @ITmishkon@it and the second group covered the remaining third of the @ITmishkon@it with the remainder [of the drapes] overhanging the back to cover the planks. @p135@p2Put the table.@p3 The @ITshulchan@it was in the north, drawn back from the northern wall 2½ @ITamohs.@it The menorah was in the south, drawn back from the southern wall 2½ @ITamohs.@it The golden altar was placed opposite the space between the @ITshulchan@it and the menorah, slightly drawn towards the east. The length of the @ITmishkon@it from the entrance to the @ITparoches@it was twenty @ITamohs.@it The altar, the @ITshulchan@it and the menorah were drawn back from the entrance westward ten @ITamohs.@it<QC>
@p136@p2You shall make a screen.@p3 A curtain which was a screen opposite the entrance, [@hh<HEB>מָסָךְ@ee<ENG>] as in: “(@hh<HEB>שַׂכְתָּ@ee<ENG>) Have you @IT protected@it him?” [both @hh<HEB>מָסָךְ@ee<ENG> and @hh<HEB>שַׂכְתָּ@ee<ENG>] expressing protection. @d1The work of an embroiderer.@d2 Its images were produced by needlework, the form of one side being the same as the form on the other side. @d1Embroiderer.@d2 [@hh<HEB>רֹקֵם@ee<ENG> is] the name of the craftsman and not the name of the craft. Then, how am I to understand: “and its height shall be three @ITamohs@it”? [The answer is that the “three @ITamohs@it are] from the top edge of the @ITsoveiv@it upwards. (See illustration on page 416.) @p12@p2Its projections shall be one piece with it.@p3 They are not to be made separately and then joined with it. @d1And you shall overlay it with copper.@d2 [Copper was used] to atone for insolence [called @hh<HEB>עֲזוּת מֵצַח@ee<ENG>]. [Insolence relates to copper:] for it says: “[For I know that you are stubborn], and your forehead is of copper.” @d1Make its pots.@d2 [@hh<HEB>סִירוֹת@ee<ENG> are] kinds of pots. @p13@p2To remove its ashes.@p3 To remove its ashes in them. It is as Onkelos translates it: “@hh<HEB>לְמִסְפֵּי קִטְמֵהּ@ee<ENG> — “to remove its ashes in them.” For [though @hh<HEB>לְדַשְּׁנוֹ@ee<ENG> — “to ash it” would imply the adding of ashes, the opposite is true here] there are words in the Hebrew language where the same word varies in meaning to be used to denote both “construction” and “destruction,” as in: @hh<HEB>וַתַּשְׁרֵשׁ שָׁרָשֶׁיהָ@ee<ENG> — “and it took root” [and] @hh<HEB>אֱוִיל מַשְׁרִישׁ@ee<ENG> — “the fools take root,” and in its opposite sense: @hh<HEB>וּבְכָל תְּבוּאָתִי תְּשָׁרֵשׁ@ee<ENG> — “and you uproot all my grain.” Similar to this: @hh<HEB>בִּסְעִפֶיהָ פֹּרִיָּה@ee<ENG> — “in its fruitful branches,” and in its opposite sense: @hh<HEB>מְסָעֵף פֻּארָה@ee<ENG> — “chops off its branches.” Similar to this: @hh<HEB>וְזֶה הָאַחֲרוֹן עִצְּמוֹ@ee<ENG> — “and this last one (@hh<HEB>עִצְּמוֹ@ee<ENG>) has broken his bones.” Similar to this: @hh<HEB>וַיִסְקְלֻהוּ בָאֲבָנִים@ee<ENG> — “They stoned him with stones,” and in its opposite sense: @hh<HEB>סַקְּלוּ מֵאֶבֶן@ee<ENG> — “remove its stones.” Similarly @hh<HEB>וַיְעַזְּקֵהוּ@ee<ENG> — ‘‘He fenced it in,’’ and @hh<HEB>וַיְסַקְּלֵהוּ@ee<ENG> he “cleaned out its stones.” Here, too, the word @hh<HEB>לְדַשְּׁנוֹ@ee<ENG> means “to @ITremove@it its ashes.” And in Old French @ITescendrer@it — “to remove ash.” @d1Its scoops.@d2@n1A@n2 (@ITsee illustration 1 on page 146@it) [@hh<HEB>וְיָעָיו@ee<ENG> is] as Onkelos translates it [@hh<HEB>וּמַגְרוֹפְיָתֵיה @ee<ENG>] — shovels in which he takes the ashes. They were similar to a thin metal pot-cover, but it also had a handle. And in Old French it is @ITwadil@it — “shovel.” @d1Its basins —@d2@n1B@n2 in which to receive the blood of the @ITkorbonos.@it @d1Its flesh-hooks.@d2@n1C@n2 These were a kind of bent hooks with which they struck the flesh so that they became imbedded in it and with them he would turn it (the flesh) over the coals of the pyre, so that they would burn more quickly. In Old French they are called @ITcrocins@it (hooks), and in the language of the Sages: @hh<HEB>צִנּוּרִיּוֹת@ee<ENG>. @d1And its fire-pans.@d2@n1D@n2 They had a scoop in which to take the coals from the altar, in order to carry them onto the inner altar for the incense. It is on account of their raking (@hh<HEB>חֲתִיָיתָן@ee<ENG>) the coals that they were called @hh<HEB>מַחְתּוֹת@ee<ENG>, as in: “@hh<HEB>לַחְתּוֹת@ee<ENG> fire from the pyre,” which means: drawing away fire from its place. Similarly: “(@hh<HEB>הֲיַחְתֶּה@ee<ENG>) can man rake fire into his lap?” @d1All of its vessels.@d2 [@hh<HEB>לְכָל כֵּלָיו@ee<ENG> here is] the same as @hh<HEB>כָּל כֵּלָיו@ee<ENG>. @p14@p2A grate.@p3 [@hh<HEB>מִכְבָּר@ee<ENG> is] from the word @hh<HEB>כְּבָרָה@ee<ENG> — “sieve,” called @ITcrible@it [in Old French]. It served as a kind of garment for the altar made with intermittent holes like a net@n1A@n2 (@ITsee illustration 2 on page 146@it). This verse has its word transposed and should be interpreted [as if it were written]: “Make for it a copper grate in the form of a net.”<QC>
@p15@p2The rim of the altar.@p3 @hh<HEB>כַּרְכֹּב@ee<ENG> has the meaning of @hh<HEB>סוֹבֵב@ee<ENG> — “that which surrounds.” Anything that surrounds completely is called @hh<HEB>כַּרְכֹּב@ee<ENG>, as we learned in Chapter @ITHakol Shochtin@it: “The following are unfinished wooden vessels: any [vessel] that will still be smoothed (@hh<HEB>וּלְכַרְכֵּב@ee<ENG>) and be rounded off.” This is similar to the practice of making grooves in the planks of the walls of chests and wooden benches. The altar, too, was engraved all around.@n1B@n2 Its (the engraving’s) width was one @ITamoh@it in its (the altar’s) wall for ornamentation. It was at the end of three (other versions: six) @ITamohs@it of its height [measured from the ground], according to the opinion that its height was twice its length [and that if you ask] how do I explain: “and its height shall be three @ITamohs@it”? [It means:] from the edge of the @ITsoveiv@it upward. But the @ITsoveiv,@it as a walkway for the @ITkohanim@it for the copper altar, was only at the top@n1C@n2 within its raised projections.@n1D@n2 Thus we learn in @ITMaseches Zevachim:@it What is the @hh<HEB>כַרְכּוֹב@ee<ENG>? [The space] between the raised projections which was one @ITamoh@it wide. Inward [toward the center of the altar] from them (i.e., the @hh<HEB>קְרָנוֹת@ee<ENG> and @hh<HEB>כַּרְכּוֹב@ee<ENG>) was an @ITamoh@it which served as a walkway for the @ITkohanim.@it These two @ITamohs@it are called @hh<HEB>כַּרְכֹּב@ee<ENG>. It is questioned there [in @ITMaseches Zevachim@it] But it states [here]: “under the @hh<HEB>כַּרְכּוֹב@ee<ENG> of the altar downward.” From this we learn that the @hh<HEB>כַּרְכּוֹב@ee<ENG> was in its (i.e., the altar’s) wall [and not on top] while the grate ornament was underneath it! Whereupon someone answers: There were two [@hh<HEB>כַּרְכֹּב@ee<ENG>] — one for ornamentation [on the side] and one for the @ITkohanim@it [at the top] so that they should not slip off [the altar]. The one that was on the side was for ornamentation. And underneath it they covered it with the grate, its width reaching halfway down the altar. Consequently the grate was one @ITamoh@it wide and it served to mark half its (the altar’s) height. This was to delineate between the “upper bloods” and the “lower bloods.” Corresponding to this, they made for the altar of the Eternal House (i.e., the @ITBeis Hamikdosh@it) a red line at its (the altar’s) midpoint. As for the ramp used to ascend [to the top of the altar,] though it is not specifically mentioned here, we have already inferred it from the @ITparsha@it [that begins:] @hh<HEB>מִזְבַּח אֲדָמָה תַּעֲשֶׂה לִי@ee<ENG>, [where it states:] “Do not ascend by means of steps,” [which means:] Do not make steps for its ramp, but, rather, a smooth ramp. From this we learn that it did have a ramp. The above we learned in the @ITMechilta.@it The @hh<HEB>מִזְבַּח אֲדָמָה@ee<ENG> — the earthen altar [mentioned above] is the same as “the copper altar” [here], for its hollow was filled with earth wherever they encamped. This ramp was to the south of the altar separated from the altar by a hair’s breadth. Its bottom reached to within an @ITamoh@it of the curtains of the courtyard on the southern side. [The above is] according to the opinion [that the altar] was ten @ITamohs@it high. According to the opinion that the words mean what they say: “its height was three @ITamohs@it” (v. 1), the ramp was only 10 @ITamohs@it long. This I found in the @ITBeraisa of 49 Midos@it. The fact that [the ramp] was separated from the altar by a thread was derived in @ITMaseches Zevachim@it from a @ITderasha@it from the Torah. @p17@p2Into the rings.@p3 In the four rings made for the grate.<QC>
@p18@p2A hollow structure.@p3 As Onkelos translates it: @hh<HEB>חֲלִיל לוּחִין@ee<ENG> — “hollow” [as a result] of “boards,” boards of acacia wood from every side and the hollow in the middle. It should not be entirely one block of wood, whereby its dimensions would be 5 @ITamohs@it by 5 @ITamohs@it like a solid block. @p19@p2Curtains.@p3 They were made like the sails of a ship with intermittent holes, braided and not woven@n1A@n2 (@ITsee illustration 1 on page 147@it). Onkelos’ translation of it as @hh<HEB>סְרָדִין@ee<ENG> is the same translation he used for @hh<HEB>מִכְבָּר@ee<ENG>, which he translated as @hh<HEB>סְרָדָא@ee<ENG> (v. 4). This, it seems to me, is the reason it is again repeated. @p119@p2All the vessels of the @BImishkon — @p3 which were needed for setting it up and for taking it down, e.g., hammers for driving stakes and posts. @d1Stakes.@d2 A sort of copper nail made for the drapes of the tent and for the curtains of the courtyard which were tied to [these stakes] with ropes wound all the way around their (i.e., the curtains’) bottom edges so that the wind should not lift them. I do not know whether they were put in the ground or whether they were only tied and hung, and their heaviness weighted down the lower edges of the drapes so that they did not move around in the wind. I would, however, suggest that their name (i.e., @hh<HEB>יְתֵדוֹת@ee<ENG> indicates that they were put in the ground, and for this reason they are called @hh<HEB>יְתֵדוֹת@ee<ENG>. And the following verse supports my view: “A tent that shall not be taken down, whose pegs (@hh<HEB>יְתֵדוֹתָיו@ee<ENG>) will never be pulled up.”<QC>
@p120@p2Clear —@p3 without sediment, as we have learned in Tractate Menachos: “He lets it (i.e., the olive) ripen on top of the olive tree, etc.” @d1Crushed.@d2 He would crush the olives in a mortar and not grind them in a mill so that it would not contain sediment. @d1To keep the lamp burning constantly.@d2 He must kindle it until the flame ascends on its own. @d1Constantly.@d2 Each and every night may be referred to as @hh<HEB>תָּמִיד@ee<ENG> as you would say “@hh<HEB>עוֹלַת תָּמִיד@ee<ENG> — the constant @IT korbon olah@it” though it is done only from day to day. @p13@p2To sanctify him to serve Me.@p3 @hh<HEB>לְקַדְּשׁוֹ@ee<ENG> means: to bring him into the service by means of the garments [@hh<HEB>לְכַהֲנוֹ לִי@ee<ENG> means:] so that he shall be a @hh<HEB>כֹּהֵן@ee<ENG> unto Me, the term @hh<HEB>כְּהוּנָה@ee<ENG> denoting “service,” @ITserjentrie@it in Old French.<QC>
@p14@p2A breastplate.@p3 An ornament worn against the heart. @d1An @BIeiphod.@it@d2 I have not heard, nor have I found in the @ITBeraisa,@it any explanation of its form. But my heart tells me that it was something tied behind him, its width being the same as the width of the back of a man, like a sort of apron, called @ITporceint@it in Old French, which the ladies of the nobility tie on when riding horses. It is thus made for the lower [part of the body]. [An indication that the @ITeiphod@it was tied around is that] which is said: “Dovid was tied around with a linen @ITeiphod@it” We learn from this that the @ITeiphod@it is a kind of belt. It cannot be said that it consisted of a belt alone, for it says: “And he put the @ITeiphod@it on it” and afterwards: “And he tied it on him with the @hh<HEB>חֵשֶׁב הָאֵפוֹד@ee<ENG>,” which Onkelos translates: “the belt of the @ITeiphod.@it” We learn from this that the @hh<HEB>חֵשֶׁב@ee<ENG> is the @ITbelt @it and the @ITeiphod@it is the name of an ornament itself. It [also] cannot be said that it is on account of its two shoulder straps that it is called @ITeiphod@it for it says: “the two shoulder-straps of the @ITeiphod.@it” [From this] we learn that the @ITeiphod@it is a separate name and the @hh<HEB>כְּתֵפוֹת@ee<ENG> are a separate name and the @hh<HEB>חֵשֶׁב@ee<ENG> is a separate name. Therefore I say that it is on account of the apron on the lower [part of the body] that it is called @ITeiphod@it (see page 433 for illustration) because it adorns him (@hh<HEB>אוֹפְדוֹ@ee<ENG>) and ornaments him as it is said: “and he adorned (@hh<HEB>וַיֶּאְפֹּד@ee<ENG>) him with it.” The @hh<HEB>חֵשֶׁב@ee<ENG> is a belt which is the upper part of it (i.e., the @ITeiphod@it) and the shoulder-straps were fastened to it (the @hh<HEB>חֵשֶׁב@ee<ENG>). My heart also tells me that there is supporting evidence that it (the @ITeiphod@it) is a kind of garment, for Yonasan translated [the words]: “And David tied on a linen @ITeiphod@it” as “a @hh<HEB>כַּרְדּוּט@ee<ENG> made of linen” while he likewise translated @hh<HEB>מְעִילִים@ee<ENG> (robes) as @hh<HEB>כַּרְדּוּטִין@ee<ENG> in the narrative of Tamar, the sister of Avshalom [where it says]: “for thus were the virgin princesses dressed in @hh<HEB>מְעִילִים@ee<ENG> (robes). @d1A robe.@d2 This was a type of shirt as was the @hh<HEB>כֻּתּוֹנֶת@ee<ENG>, but, [the difference being] that the @hh<HEB>כֻּתֹּנֶת@ee<ENG> was worn on the flesh while @hh<HEB>מְעִיל@ee<ENG> refers to the outer shirt. @d1Checkered.@d2 Made in the form of @hh<HEB>מִשְׁבְּצוֹת@ee<ENG> for beauty. The @hh<HEB>מִשְׁבְּצוֹת@ee<ENG> are a sort of indentations that are made in golden ornaments as settings for precious stones and pearls as it says of the stones of the @ITeiphod:@it “they were surrounded by (@hh<HEB>מִשְׁבְּצוֹת@ee<ENG>) settings of gold.” In Old French they are called @ITchastons.@it @d1A turban.@d2 — a kind of a domed hat called @ITcoife@it in Old French, for elsewhere they are called @hh<HEB>מִגְבָּעוֹת@ee<ENG> which Onkelos translates for us as “hats.” @d1And a sash-belt.@d2 This is a belt worn on the inner-shirt, whereas the @ITeiphod@it is worn over the outer-shirt, as we find at [the relating] of the order of their being put on: “He put the inner shirt on him and tied the sash-belt around him and dressed him with the outer-shirt and then he put the @ITeiphod@it on him.” @d1Sacred garments.@d2 From the @ITterumah@it “that was sanctified for My Name must they be made.” @p15@p2They shall take.@p3 Those “wise at heart” (v.3) who will make the garments shall receive from the donors the gold and the greenish-blue wool from which to make the garments. @p16@p2They shall make the @BIeiphod.@it@p3 If I attempt to explain the making of the @ITeiphod@it and the breastplate in the order of the verses their explanation will be fragmented and the reader will make mistakes in putting the various parts together. Therefore I will [first] write how they were made so that the reader will easily run through it, and afterwards I will explain [it] in detail in the order of the verses. The @ITeiphod@it was made as a kind of apron for women who ride horses.@n1A@n2 (@ITSee illustrations on page 204.@it) He girds himself from behind, level with his heart, beneath his elbows. Its width is the same measurement as the width of the back of a man and somewhat more, reaching to his heels. The @hh<HEB>חֵשֶׁב@ee<ENG> (belt)@n1B@n2 was attached at its (the @ITeiphod@it’s) top along the entire width by the work of a weaver. It is extended [horizontally] beyond the @ITeiphod@it in both directions so as to surround and gird [him] with it. The shoulder-straps@n1C@n2 were attached to the belt — one on the right and one on the left behind the @ITkohein@it at the two ends of the width of the apron. When they were upright they stood (i.e., lay) on his two shoulders. They were like two straps made of the same material as the @ITeiphod,@it long enough to place them near his neck, on both sides. They folded over in front of him slightly below his shoulders. The @ITshoham@it-stones@n1D@n2 were set in them, one on the right shoulder and one on the left shoulder. The settings@n1E@n2 were put in their ends in front of his shoulders. The two golden braided chains@n1F@n2 were inserted into the two rings of the breastplate at the two ends along the upper width,@n1G@n2 one on the right and one on the left. The two ends of the chain on the right are attached to the setting on the right.@n1H@n2 Similarly the two ends of the chain on the left are attached to the setting on the left shoulder-strap. Consequently the breastplate hung from the settings of the @ITeiphod@it over his heart in front. There were two additional rings@n1I@n2 at the two ends of the breastplate at its bottom and opposite them, two rings@n1J@n2 [attached] to the two shoulder-straps of the @ITeiphod@it at the bottom, at its lower end [in the back] where it is attached to the belt. The rings of the breastplate were opposite the rings of the @ITeiphod@it lying on each other. He then joins them (the rings) with a thread of greenish-blue wool that is inserted through the rings of the @ITeiphod@it and the breastplate, so that the bottom of the breastplate should lie against the belt of the @ITeiphod@it and not move about and separate [from it] [thereby] moving to and fro. @d1Gold, greenish-blue wool, dark red wool. crimson wool, and fine twined linen.@d2 These five kinds were twined into every thread. They hammered out the gold into thin trays and cut threads from them (i.e., the trays) and then spun them — one gold thread with 6 threads of greenish-blue wool and one gold thread with 6 threads of dark red wool, and similarly with the crimson wool and similarly with the fine linen. For each kind was braided six-fold and there was a golden thread with each one. Then he would twine them all into one. Consequently the strands were twenty-eight fold. It is thus explained in Tractate Yoma and learned from the following verse: “They hammered out thin trays of gold and cut it into threads to work the golden threads into the greenish-blue wool and into the dark red wool, etc.” From this we derive that a gold thread was twined together with all the other kinds. @d1The work of a craftsman.@d2 I have already explained earlier that this refers to weaving on two sides whereby the images on both sides are not similar to each other. @p17@p2Two shoulder-straps etc.@p3 The apron was underneath [them]. The @hh<HEB>חֵשֶׁב@ee<ENG> of the @ITeiphod@it is the belt, and it was attached to it at the top in the likeness of the apron of the women. On the back of the @ITkohein@it there were attached to the belt two straps similar to two wide straps, one opposite each shoulder which he lifted onto both of his shoulders until they folded over in his front. By attaching them to the rings of the breast plate they were held in front of him over his heart so that they would not fall, as is explained where Scripture discusses them. They continued vertically over his shoulders where two @ITshoham@it-stones were set one in each. @d1At its two corners —@d2 on the width of the @ITeiphod@it for its width was equal only to his back. Its height [reached] till the elbows, called @ITcodes@it in Old French, for it states: @hh<HEB>לֹא יַחְגְּרוּ בַּיָּזַע@ee<ENG> [which means:] “They shall not be tied around in a place where one perspires,” i.e., not above their elbows and not below their loins, but opposite their elbows. @d1They shall be attached —@d2 the @ITeiphod@it with those two shoulder-straps of the @ITeiphod.@it He shall attach [sew] their bottoms to the belt with a needle, and not by weaving them together but, rather, they are woven separately and joined together afterwards. @p18@p2The @BIeiphod@BO’s belt.@p3 [I.e.,] the belt by means of which it (the @ITeiphod@it) enwraps him, and makes it fit the @ITkohein@it properly and adorns him. @d1Which is on it —@d2 on top at the edge of the apron. This refers to the belt. @d1Shall be made in the same manner —@d2 like the weaving of the apron, the work of a craftsman and of five kinds [of material] — so too the weaving of the belt [was] the work of a craftsman and of the five kinds [of material]. @d1It shall be woven with it.@d2 [I.e.] it shall be woven together with it and not woven separately and then attached.<QC>
@p110@p2In the order of their birth.@p3 [Meaning:] in the order that they were born: Reuvein, Shimon, Levi, Yehudah, Dan, Naftali on the one; and on the other one: Gad, Asher, Yissachar, Zevulun, Yoseif, Binyamin — [@hh<HEB>בִּנְיָמִין@ee<ENG> written] “full,” for that is the way it is written in the place where his birth is recorded [making it a total of] 25 letters in each one. @p111@p2The work of a skilled stone engraver.@p3 [Meaning:] the work of one skilled with stones. The word @hh<HEB>חָרַשׁ@ee<ENG> here is in the construct with the word that follows it and is therefore vocalized with a @ITpatach@it at its end (i.e., the letter @hh<HEB>ר@ee<ENG>). Similarly “(@hh<HEB>חָרַשׁ עֵצִים@ee<ENG>) the woodworker stretched a line” meaning “the worker of wood.” Similarly “(@hh<HEB>חָרַשׁ בַּרְזֶל@ee<ENG>) the iron worker makes an axe.” These are [examples] of words in the construct and vocalized with a @ITpatach.@it @d1Like the engraving of a signet.@d2 As Onkelos translates it: “Explicit writing like the engraving of a signet.” The letters were engraved into them as signet-rings are engraved since they are used to seal letters with writing which is obvious and explicit. @d1With the names.@d2 [@hh<HEB>עַל שְׁמֹ֖ת@ee<ENG> is the] same here as @hh<HEB>בִּשְׁמוֹת@ee<ENG>. @d1In gold settings.@d2 The stones were surrounded by gold settings. They should make as a setting of gold for each stone a sort of cavity the size of the stone, and then sink [place] it into the setting. Consequently the setting surrounds the stone. He then joined the settings to the shoulder-straps of the @ITeiphod.@it @p112@p2As remembrance.@p3 So that God will see the tribes’ [names] written before Him and remember their righteousness. @p113@p2Make settings.@p3 The minimum of [the plural] @hh<HEB>מִשְׁבְּצוֹת@ee<ENG> is two. Only part of their purpose is explained in this section. In the section dealing with the breastplate it completes their explanation for you. @p114@p2Two chains.@p3 [@hh<HEB>שַׁרְשְׁרוֹת@ee<ENG> has the same meaning as] @hh<HEB>שַׁלְשְׁלָאוֹת@ee<ENG> — “chains.” @d1The ends.@d2 Make them for the end border of the breastplate. @d1A braided work.@d2 A work of braided threads and not a worked of linked rings like those made for [drawing water from] wells, but, rather, like those made for for incense receptacles called @ITencensiers@it in Old French]. @d1And you will fasten the chains —@d2 of braids; those made of braided work — to these settings. This is not the place of the command for making the chains nor the command to affix them. And the word @hh<HEB>תַּעֲשֶׂה@ee<ENG> that is used here is not an expression of command, nor is word @hh<HEB>וְנָתַתָּה@ee<ENG> that is used here an expression of command but, rather, the future tense, for in the section dealing with the breastplate He returns [to this subject] and commands him regarding their making and their being affixed. It is written here only to let you know some of the need for the settings which He commanded to make with the @ITeiphod.@it He wrote this here to tell you [in effect]: “These settings will be needed by you — when you will make chains for the ends of the breastplate you will put them onto these settings.” @p115@p2A breastplate of judgment.@p3 [so-called] because it atones for the perversion of justice. An alternate explanation: [it is called] @hh<HEB>מִשְׁפָּט@ee<ENG> because it makes its words clear and its promise is true, @ITdereisnement@it in Old French. For @hh<HEB>מִשְׁפָּט@ee<ENG> is used in three senses: [1.] the arguments of the disputants, and [2.] the handing down of the verdict, and [3.] the pronouncing of the sentence — whether punishment of death, whether punishment by flogging, whether a monetary punishment. Here it is used in the sense of making its words clear for it explains and makes its words clear. @d1The same manner as you made the @BIeiphod.@it@d2 [I.e.] the work of a craftsman and [made] of the five kinds [of material]. @p116@p2A span in its length and a span in its width —@p3 doubled. It is placed on him in front over his heart, as it is said: “And they will be over Aharon’s heart.” It is hung from the shoulder-straps of the @ITeiphod@it which come from behind him over his shoulders and fold over and come down slightly in the front and the breastplate hangs from them by means of chains and rings as is explained where they are discussed. @p117@p2Fill it.@p3 Because the stones filled the cavities of the settings which were prepared for them they are referred to by the term @hh<HEB>מִלּוּאִים@ee<ENG> (fillers). @p120@p2Placed in gold settings —@p3 shall the rows shall be @hh<HEB>בְּמִלּוּאֹתָם@ee<ENG> — “in their settings” — surrounded by gold settings to a depth sufficient to be filled by the thickness of the stone. This is the meaning of the term @hh<HEB>בְּמִלּוּאֹתָם@ee<ENG>: the amount needed to be filled by the thickness of the stones shall be the depth of the settings, neither less nor more. @p121@p2Each one’s name.@p3 As was the order of their birth so was the order of the stones — the ruby for Reuvain, topaz for Shimon, and so on for all of them. @p122@p2For the breastplate.@p3 [Meaning:] “@ITfor@it the breastplate” to fasten them by means of its rings as is explained below when it is discussed. @d1@hh<HEB>שַׁרְשֹׁת@ee<ENG>.@d2 From the same root as @hh<HEB>שָׁרְשֵׁי@ee<ENG> (roots) of a tree which hold fast to the tree so that it is held and fixed in the ground. These, too, shall hold the breastplate firm for from them will it hang from the @ITeiphod.@it These are the two (@hh<HEB>שַׁרְשְׁרוֹת@ee<ENG>) chains mentioned above where the subject was the settings. Actually, the word @hh<HEB>שַׁרְשְׁרוֹת@ee<ENG> is also interpreted by Menachem ben Saruk as having a common root with @hh<HEB>שָׁרָשִׁים@ee<ENG> (roots) and he claims that the [second] @hh<HEB>ר'@ee<ENG> is superfluous just as the @hh<HEB>מ'@ee<ENG> of @hh<HEB>שִׁלְשׁוֹם@ee<ENG> (above 21, 36) and the @hh<HEB>מ'@ee<ENG> of @hh<HEB>רֵיקָם@ee<ENG> (Bereishis 31, 42). But I do not see his point-of-view. Rather, @hh<HEB>שַׁרְשֶׁרֶת@ee<ENG>, in Hebrew, is the same as @hh<HEB>שַׁלְשֶׁלֶת@ee<ENG> — “chain,” in Mishnaic Hebrew. @d1End.@d2 This is the @hh<HEB>מִגְבָּלוֹת@ee<ENG> mentioned above, [meaning:] that you shall insert them in the rings that will be at the edge of the breastplate.
