@p131@p2The robe of the @BIeiphod.@BO@d2 [So-called] because the @ITeiphod@it was put on over it as a wrap around. @d1@hh<HEB>כְּלִיל תְּכֵלֶת@ee<ENG>.@d2 [Meaning:] completely of greenish-blue wool, with no other kind [of material] mixed into it. @p132@p2Its opening at the top.@p3 [Meaning:] the opening of the robe at the top. which is the opening for the neck — @d1[shall be] turned inward.@d2 As Onkelos translates it: @hh<HEB>כְּפִיל לְגַוֵהּ@ee<ENG>, [which means:] “folded inward” so that its fold shall form a hem. @p136@p2A diadem.@p3 It was a sort of golden plate, having a width of two finger breadths, going around the forehead from ear to ear.@n1A@n2 (@ITSee illustration on page 205.@it) @p137@p2Set it on a greenish-blue cord.@p3 But elsewhere it states: “Put over it (the @hh<HEB>צִיץ@ee<ENG>) a greenish-blue cord.” Furthermore, here it is written: “So that it will be on the turban” but further on it states: “It shall be on Aharon’s forehead”! [To solve this dilemma we must refer to the @ITgemara:@it] In the Tractate Shechitas Kodoshim we learned: his hair was visible between the diadem and the turban for it was there that he donned his @ITtefillin.@it We learn from this that the turban was high up on the head and it was not deep enough for the entire head to fit into it [all the way down] to the forehead. The threads were [inserted] into holes hanging from it at both ends@n1B@n2 and at its middle.@n1C@n2 Six [threads] at these three places: a thread at the top, one on the outside [of the plate] and one on the inside opposite it. He tied the ends of the thread, all three of them at back of the neck.@n1D@n2 Consequently between the length of the plate and the threads at its ends, his skull was surrounded. The middle thread which was on its top was tied with the ends of the [other] two and passed over the width (i.e., the crown) of his head. The result was that it was made like a hat. It is in reference to the middle thread that it says: “so that it will be on the turban.” He placed the diadem on his head as a sort of hat, over the turban@n1E@n2 with the middle thread holding it so that it should not fall. The plate hung against his forehead. All the verses have now been set right: a thread @ITon@it the diadem and the diadem @ITon@it the thread and a thread on top of the turban. @p138@p2Aharon will carry.@p3 A term meaning “atonement.” Nevertheless, it does not lose its plain meaning: Aharon carried the load of sin thereby removing sin from the holy things. @d1The iniquity of the sacred offerings.@d2 [I.e.] to make acceptable [to God] the blood and the fat [of the sacrifices] that were sacrificed in a state of uncleanliness, as we have learned: For what sin does it (the @hh<HEB>צִיץ@ee<ENG>) atone? The Torah therefore states: “And this is what you shall offer [on the altar],” which follows immediately after this; Just as the burnt-offering [of the next verse] is fit, so, too, [in this verse] only that which is fit [i.e.] that it was fit earlier on and became unfit only after being brought into the courtyard [of the @ITBeis Hamikdosh@it or @ITmishkon@it], for example, [parts to be burnt that were off the altar] overnight, or if they [the parts to be burnt] were taken out [of their designated place], or if they [the parts to be burnt] became ritually unclean, or if it was slaughtered with the thought [of eating it] outside the time allotted for it or outside the place allotted for it and the like. @p140@p2@hh<HEB>וְעִשָׂרוֹן סֹלֶת@ee<ENG>.@p3 A tenth of an @ITeipha@it — [equivalent to] forty three and one-fifth eggs. @d1Pressed [olive] oil.@d2 It is not said here so as to make it mandatory but, rather, only to teach that it is fit [for @ITmenachos@it]. @p112@p2When you take the count.@p3 [@hh<HEB>תִשָּׂא@ee<ENG>] has the meaning of “taking,” as Onkelos translates it [@hh<HEB>תְקַבֵּל@ee<ENG>] — “you will take.” [I.e.] “When you will wish to take the sum total of their numbers, do not take a head count, but, rather, let each give a half-shekel then count the shekalim and you will know [their number].” @d1Thus there will be no plague among them — @d2for things that are counted are subject to the evil eye and as a result a plague befalls them like the one which occurred in the days of David. @p113@p2Must give this.@p3 He (God) showed him the likeness of a coin made of fire whose weight was half a shekel and said to him, “Like this shall they give.” @d1Passing by to be counted.@d2 It is the way of those who take a count to make that which is to be counted pass one after the other. Similarly: “Whoever passes under the rod” and similarly: “the sheep shall pass by the counter.” @d1Half a shekel based on the shekel of the holy [Sanctuary].@d2 I.e., based on the weight of the shekel which I (God) set aside for you with which to weigh the shekels to be used for sacred things. For example, the shekels mentioned in the section [dealing with] evaluating things vowed to the Sanctuary and possessed fields [vowed to the Sanctuary]. @d1Where a shekel is twenty @ITgeirah.@it@d2 It now clarifies for you how much it is. @d1@ITGeirah.@it@d2 [@ITGeirah@it in Hebrew is] the word for @hh<HEB>מָעָה@ee<ENG> [in Aramaic]. The same applies to: “...will come to bow down for an @hh<HEB>אֲגוֹרָה@ee<ENG> of silver and a piece of bread.” @d1Where a shekel is twenty @ITgeirah.@it@d2 I.e., a whole shekel. For a shekel is four @ITzuz@it and a @ITzuz@it at first was five @hh<HEB>מָעָה@ee<ENG>. But then one sixth was added and its value was raised to six silver @hh<HEB>מָעָה@ee<ENG>. “It is half of this [original] shekel, which I described to you, that they shall give as a @ITterumah@it-offering to God.” @p114@p2From [age] twenty years and older.@p3 Here it teaches you that no one below twenty years of age goes into the army nor is he counted among the men. @p115@p2To atone for your souls.@p3 [Meaning:] so that you not be struck by a plague as a result of being counted. An alternate explanation of @hh<HEB>לְכַפֵּר עַל נַפְשׁוֹתֵיכֶם@ee<ENG>: Because it alludes here to three @ITterumah@it-offerings, for @hh<HEB>תְּרוּמַת ה'@ee<ENG> is written three times here. The first represents the terumah-offering for the sockets for he (Moshe) counted them when they began contributing to the @ITmishkon@it and each person gave half a shekel, and it totalled 100 @ITkikar,@it as it states: “And the silver of the counted of the congregation totalled one hundred @ITkikar,@it” and from these the sockets were made as it states: “The one hundred @ITkikar@it of silver, etc.” The second [@hh<HEB>תְּרוּמַת ה'@ee<ENG>], it, too, [was contributed] through counting for they were counted after the @ITmishkon@it was erected. It is the counting that is spoken of at the start of the book of Numbers (Bamidbar): “[God spoke to Moshe] on the first day of the second month, in the second year,” [at which time] each one gave half a shekel. They were for buying the communal @ITkorbonos@it for each year. Both were considered alike regarding them — the poor and the rich. It is regarding this [last] @ITterumah@it-offering that it is said: “to atone for your souls,” because the [purpose of the] @ITkorbonos@it is atonement. And the third [@hh<HEB>תְּרוּמַת ה'@ee<ENG>] is the @ITterumah@it-offering for the @ITmishkon,@it as it is said: “everyone who set aside a @ITterumah@it-offering of silver and copper.” Not everyone participated alike in this case, rather, each person contributed as his heart moved him. @p116@p2And donate it for the work of the Tent of Meeting.@p3 From this you may learn that he (Moshe) was commanded to count them when they began to contribute to the @ITmishkon,@it after the occurrence of the [golden] calf because a plague had come upon them, as it states: “And God plagued the people.” A parable [to which this can be compared]: to a flock of sheep that is dear to its owner that was struck by a plague. Once it (the plague) ceased, he said to the shepherd, “I ask of you, count my sheep so that you know how many of them are left.” [He did this] in order to make it known that it (i.e., the flock) was dear to him. [However,] it is impossible to say that this count is the same one spoken of in the book of Numbers (i.e., Bamidbar), for regarding that one it says: “on the first day of the @ITsecond@it month,” whereas the @ITmishkon@it was erected on the first day of the @ITfirst@it month, as it is said: “On the first day of the first month you shall erect, etc. [the @ITmishkon@it]” and from this count [in this section] the sockets were made from its @ITshekalim,@it as it states: “the one hundred @ITkikar@it [of silver] were used to cast, etc.”: Thus you learn that they were two [separate countings], one at the start of their contribution after Yom Kippur of the first year, and one in the second year in @ITIyar after@it the @ITmishkon@it was already erected. Should you ask: but is it possible that at both [countings] the [number of] Israelites was exactly the same, viz., 603,550?! For in relating the use of the silver [which was collected] at the counting of the congregation it is so stated (i.e., it gives the above number). And in the Book of Numbers (i.e., Bamidbar) there, too, it states: “And the total of all who were counted was 603,550.” Were not [these countings] in two [different] years and it would have been impossible that there would not have been at the first counting some nineteen year olds who were not counted and turned twenty by the second year. The solution to this problem is: As regards to the age of the people [it is considered that] they were counted in the same year, whereas as pertaining to the exodus from Egypt they are [considered] as two years. This is because the exodus from Egypt is calculated from @ITNissan,@it as we have learned in Tractate Rosh Hashanah [and we therefore speak of] the @ITmishkon@it being built in the first year and erected in the second, for the new year begins on the first of @ITNissan,@it whereas the ages of the people are calculated according to the years [since the creation] of the world which begin with [the month of] @ITTishri.@it Consequently both countings [took place, in effect,] in the same year. The first counting was in @ITTishri,@it after Yom Kippur, when God became reconciled with the Israelites to forgive them [for the sin of the golden calf], and they were given the command regarding [the construction] of the @ITmishkon@it while the second [counting] took place on the first of @ITIyar@it. @d1For the work of the Tent of Meeting.@d2 This refers to the sockets which were made of it.<QC>
@p118@p2A wash basin —@p3 like a large pot which had spigots which poured out water through their openings. @d1And its base.@d2 As Onkelos translates it: “and its base,” a stand set up [to hold] the wash basin. @d1For washing.@d2 This refers back to the wash basin. @d1And the altar —@d2 the altar of the burnt-offering, about which it is written that it is “in front of the entrance way of the @ITmishkon@it of the Tent of Meeting.” The wash basin was slightly pulled to the side and stood in the space between the altar and the @ITmishkon@it and did not obstruct at all between them, for it is stated: “And the altar of the burnt-offering he placed at the entrance of the @ITmishkon@it of the Tent of Meeting,” [indicating that] the altar was in front of the Tent of Meeting but the wash basin was not in front of the Tent of Meeting. Then where was it? It was slightly pulled back southward. So it is taught in Tractate Zevachim. @p119@p2Their hands and their feet.@p3 He (the @ITkohein@it) simultaneously washed his hands and feet. Thus did we learn in Tractate Zevachim: How was the washing of the hands and feet done? He placed his right hand on his right foot, and his left hand on his left foot and washed [them]. @p120@p2Before entering the Tent of Meeting —@p3 to burn the incense, morning and afternoon, or to sprinkle from the blood of the bull brought by the anointed @ITkohein@it [@ITgodol@it] and of the goats brought for the sin of idolatry. @d1And they will not die.@d2 But, if they do not wash, they @ITwill@it die, for in the Torah there were said things intended to be understood by inference. Hence from the inference of the negative you may derive the positive.<QC>
@d1The altar.@d2 [I.e.,] the outer [altar] for in this case there is no entering into the Tent of Meeting, but only into the courtyard. @p121@p2And they will not die.@p3 This intends to make liable to the death penalty someone who performs a @ITkorbon@it service on the [outer] altar without having washed his hands and feet; for from the earlier mention of the death penalty we can derive only that it applies to someone who enters the Sanctuary. @p123@p2The finest spices.@p3 [Meaning:] the finest. @d1Fragrant cinnamon.@d2 Since cinnamon is the bark of a tree, there are some that are good and have a good fragrance and flavor, and there are some that are merely like wood, it was therefore necessary to say: @hh<HEB>קִנְּמָן בֶּשֶׂם@ee<ENG> — i.e., of the fragrant one. @d1Half a portion being two hundred fifty.@d2 I.e., half of what is brought shall be two hundred fifty. Consequently the total was five hundred, equal to the amount of pure myrrh. If that is the case, why is it expressed in terms of halves? It is a scriptural decree that it be brought as two halves so that it be increased by two tippings of the scale for things cannot be weighed to their absolute exactness. So this is taught in Tractate Kerisos. @d1Calamus spices.@d2 [Meaning:] cane containing spices. Because there are canes that contain no spices it was necessary to say: @hh<HEB>בֹּשֶׂם@ee<ENG> — “containing spices.” @d1Two hundred fifty —@d2 the amount of the total weight. @p124@p2Cassia.@p3 It is the name of a root of an herb which, in the language of the Sages, is @ITketzia@it @d1@BIHin.@it@d2 The equivalent of twelve @ITlogs.@it The Sages of Yisrael disputed [the oil’s purpose]: Rabbi Meir said that the roots were boiled in it. Whereupon Rabbi Yehuda said to him: [But it is not so] for even to coat the roots this amount [of oil] would be insufficient! But [its purpose was this:] they soaked them (the roots) in water [so that being saturated with water] they would not absorb the oil, after which he poured the oil upon them [and kept it there] until it absorbed the aroma and then skimmed the oil off the roots. @p125@p2A blend made by a [perfume] blender.@p3 @hh<HEB>רֹקַח@ee<ENG> is a noun [and not a verb], and the accent’s being on the first syllable indicates this. It is the same as @hh<HEB>רֶקַח@ee<ENG> — “blended,” or @hh<HEB>רֶגַע@ee<ENG> — “a moment.” It is not the same as: @hh<HEB>רֹגַע הַיָּם@ee<ENG> — “he stirs up the sea;” or the same as: @hh<HEB>רֹקַע הָאָרֶץ@ee<ENG> — “who spreads out the land,” where the accent is on the last syllable. Anything mixed with another thing to the degree that one absorbs from the other either smell or taste is called @hh<HEB>מִרְקַחַת@ee<ENG>. @d1@hh<HEB>רֹקַח מִרְקַחַת@ee@d2<ENG> [therefore means] a blend made through skilled craftsmanship and mixing. @d1The work of a @hh<HEB>רוֹקֵחַ@ee<ENG> — @d2 the name of the craftsman in this field. @p126@p2Anoint with it.@p3 All anointings were done in the form of the Greek @ITchi@it except those of kings which are done in the form of a crown. @p129@p2Sanctify them.@p3 It is this anointing which sanctifies them to become holy of holies. And what is the manifestation of their holiness? It is: “Anything that touches them, etc.” [This means:] anything that is fit for a sacred vessel, once it comes into it (i.e., the vessel) it becomes intrinsically holy, so that it will become unfit though @hh<HEB>יוֹצֵא@ee<ENG>, [i.e., if taken out of the courtyard of the @ITmishkon@it] or @hh<HEB>לִינָה@ee<ENG>, [i.e., being left overnight off the altar], or [by coming in contact with] a @hh<HEB>טְבוּל יוֹם@ee<ENG> and cannot be redeemed so as to become secular. However, anything that is not fit for them they do not sanctify. This is stated as an undisputed Mishnah (i.e., a @ITberaisa@it) concerning the altar. From that which is stated: “Whatever touches the altar becomes sanctified,” I might infer that this applies whether it is fit [for the altar] or whether it is not fit. The Torah, therefore, says [in the next verse]: “sheep” — just as sheep are fit [for the altar], so must everything be fit [to be sanctified by the altar]. Every anointing of the @ITmishkon, kohanim@it and kings is translated by Onkelos as @hh<HEB>רִבּוּי@ee<ENG> — “elevating to a higher status” because the only purpose of anointing is to elevate to a higher status, for so did the King (God) establish that this [shall be the method] of installation to a higher status. However, other anointings, for example, @hh<HEB>רְקִיקִין מְשׁוּחִים@ee<ENG> (anointed wafers), or: “With the important oils shall they anoint (@hh<HEB>יִמְשָׁחוּ@ee<ENG>),” their Aramaic version is the same as the Hebrew. @p131@p2For all your generations.@p3 From this our Sages deduced that one can state that the entire [@hh<HEB>שֶׁמֶן הַמִּשְׁחָה@ee<ENG>] will be preserved for the time yet to come (i.e., after @ITMoshiach@it comes). @d1This.@d2 The numerical value [of @hh<HEB>זֶה@ee<ENG>] [@hh<HEB>ז@ee<ENG>=7, @hh<HEB>ה @ee<ENG>=5] corresponds to the twelve @ITlogs@it [of the @hh<HEB>שֶׁמֶן הַמִּשְׁחָה@ee<ENG>]. @p132@p2@hh<HEB>לֹא יִיסָךְ@ee<ENG> —@p3 spelled with two letters @hh<HEB>י'@ee<ENG> which takes the form: he (or, it) shall not be done, as in: “so that (@hh<HEB>יִיטַב@ee<ENG>) it will be well with you.” @d1It must not be poured on the flesh of any man —@d2 from this oil itself. @d1@hh<HEB>וּבְמַתְכֻּנְתּוֹ לֹא תַעֲשֹוּ כָּמֹהוּ@ee<ENG>.@d2 [Meaning:] with the quantity of each of its spices make no other like it, i.e., with the weight of these spices in proportion to the measure of a @IThin@it of oil. But if he decreased or increased the spices in proportion to the measure of a @IThin@it of oil, then it is permissible. @d1Spices.@d2 [I.e.,] other spices. @d1And pure frankincense.@d2 From here our Sages derived that Moshe was told of eleven spices at Sinai: the minimum amount indicated by @hh<HEB>סַמִּים@ee<ENG> — “spices,” is two (for the least of any plural is two); stacte, onycha, and galbanum are three; so you have five; @hh<HEB>סַמִּים@ee<ENG> [is repeated at this point] so as to add an amount equal to the above; so now you have @ITten;@it and frankincense makes it @ITeleven.@it They are as follows: 1.stacte, 2.onycha, 3.galbanum 4.frankincense, 5.myrrh, 6.cassia, 7.spikenard, 8.saffron; this gives you eight, because @hh<HEB>שִׁבּוֹלֶת@ee<ENG> and @hh<HEB>נֵרְד@ee<ENG> are one, for @hh<HEB>נֵרְד@ee<ENG> is similar to @hh<HEB>שִׁבּוֹלֶת@ee<ENG>; 9.costus, 10.aromatic bark, 11.cinnamon, giving you the total of eleven. @ITBoris karshina@it is not burnt but it is used to smooth the onycha in order to whiten it so as to make it pleasing to the eye. @d1They shall be of equal weight.@d2 The four explicitly mentioned here shall be equal — weight for weight. It is my opinion that similar to this (i.e., @hh<HEB>מְמֻלָּח@ee<ENG>) is: “(@hh<HEB>הַמַּלָחִים@ee<ENG>) the sailors were frightened” [or:] “(@hh<HEB>מַלָחַיִךְ@ee<ENG>) your sailors and your pilots.” [They are so called] because they keep turning the water over (mixing) with oars when they move the ship, similar to a person who stirs beaten eggs with a spoon in order to mix them with water. Anything that a person wants to mix thoroughly he turns over (stirs up) with a finger or a spoon. @d1Thoroughly blended, pure and holy.@d2 Thoroughly blended shall it be, pure shall it be, and holy shall it be. @p136@p2And place it, etc.@p3 This refers to the daily incense burning which [was done] on the inner altar which is inside the Tent of Meeting. @d1Where I will meet with you.@d2 “All appointments that I will set up to speak with you I shall set up in that place.” @p137@p2Its formula.@p3 [Meaning:] according to the quantity of its spices. @d1It shall be sacred to you for Adonoy.@d2 [Meaning:] that you shall do it only for My Name.<QC>

@d1It is sacred to Adonoy.@d2 The observance of its sacredness shall be for My Name and to obey My commandments. @p117@p2And He rested.@p3 As Onkelos translates it: @hh<HEB>וְנָח@ee<ENG> — “and He rested.” All forms of @hh<HEB>נוֹפֶשׁ@ee<ENG> are from @hh<HEB>נֶפֶשׁ@ee<ENG> — “soul,” i.e., he retrieves his soul and breath by his resting from the toil of his work. @p11@p2That Moshe was late.@p3 As Onkelos translates it — a term for lateness. Similarly: “[Why] is the rider (@hh<HEB>בּשֵׁשׁ@ee<ENG>) late?” [or:] “They waited until (@hh<HEB>בּוֹשׁ@ee<ENG>) it was late.” For when Moshe ascended the mountain he had told them: “At the end of forty days I will return within [the first] six hours.” They thought that the day that he ascended is included [in the forty days] but, actually, he had told them “@ITfull [days]@it” [i.e.] forty days, each day with its preceding night. But the day that he ascended, its night was not part of it for he ascended on the seventh of Sivan. Consequently the actual 40th day was the seventeenth of Tammuz. On the sixteenth (of Tammuz) the Satan came and caused disorder in the world by creating the appearance of darkness, deep gloom and confusion [so as to lead people] to say: “Moshe has certainly died and therefore the world has been thrown into disorder.” He said to them: “Moshe @IThas@it died, for six hours have already come and he still has not come, etc.” as is related in Tractate Shabbos. It is impossible to say that their mistake was due only to a cloudy day whether it was before or after noon, for Moshe did not descend until the following day, as it is said: “They arose early the next morning and offered burnt-offerings.” @d1That will lead us.@d2 They yearned to have many gods.<QC>
@d1For this Moshe, the man.@d2 The Satan showed them a likeness of Moshe being carried in the air high in the sky. @d1Who brought us up from the land of Egypt —@d2 and taught us the way to go; now we need gods who will go before us. @p12@p2On the ears of your wives.@p3 Aharon thought to himself: the women and children fancy their jewelry, perhaps, [as a result], the thing will be delayed and in the meantime Moshe will arrive. But they did not wait [for the women and children] and took off [their own jewelry] from themselves. @d1Remove.@d2 @hh<HEB>פָּרְקוּ@ee<ENG> is a plural imperative (a command) derived from @hh<HEB>פָּרֵק@ee<ENG> in the singular as in @hh<HEB>בָּרְכוּ@ee<ENG> derived from @hh<HEB>בָּרֵךְ@ee<ENG>. @p13@p2They removed.@p3 [@hh<HEB>וַיִתְפָּרְקוּ@ee<ENG>] has the meaning of unloading a burden, for when they removed them from their ears they became “unloaded” of their rings, @ITdescharjier@it in Old French. @d1@hh<HEB>אֶת נִזְמֵי@ee<ENG>.@d2 As if it were: @hh<HEB>מִנִּזְמֵי@ee<ENG> — “of the rings,” as in: “As soon as I go out (@hh<HEB>אֶת הָעִיר@ee<ENG>)” [meaning:] (from) of the city. @p14@p2And formed it with an engraving tool.@p3 This can be translated two ways: The first: @hh<HEB>וַיָּצַר@ee<ENG> as a term for tying and @hh<HEB>בַּחֶרֶט@ee<ENG> as a term for a cloth, as in: “the shawls (@hh<HEB>וְהַחֲרִיטִים@ee<ENG>) and the cloths,” [or:] “He tied two @ITkikar@it of silver in two (@hh<HEB>חֲרִיטִים@ee<ENG>) cloths.” The second: @hh<HEB>וַיָּצַר@ee<ENG> as a term for forming images; @hh<HEB>בַּחֶרֶט@ee<ENG>, a tool used by goldsmiths with which they scrape and engrave images into gold, like the stylus of a scribe who engraves letters onto tablets and ledgers, as in: “And write on it with a man’s (@hh<HEB>בַּחֶרֶט@ee<ENG>) stylus.” This was Onkelos’ intent when he translated it: “He engraved it with a @hh<HEB>זִיפָא@ee<ENG>, from the word @hh<HEB>זִיּוּף@ee<ENG> — “forgery.” It is a tool for engraving letters and designs in gold, referred to in Old French as @ITneel,@it and through it seals can be forged. @d1A molten calf.@d2 As soon as he threw it into the fire, in a melting pot, the magicians of the riffraff came — those who went up with them from Egypt — and made it (the Calf) by means of magic. @p15@p2Aharon saw —@p3 that it was alive as it is said [of the Golden Calf]: “in the form of an ox eating grass,” and he thereby saw that the Satan’s act succeeded and therefore had no excuse to put them off completely. @d1He built an altar —@d2 so as to stall them. @d1And he said, tomorrow will be a festival to Adonoy.@d2 But [he did not say] “today,” in case Moshe would return before they worship it. @p113@p2Remember Avraham.@p3 If they transgressed the Ten Commandments, their father Avraham was tested through ten tests, and has not yet received his reward, give it to him [now] and let the “ten” offset the “ten.” @d1Avraham, Yitzchok and Yisrael.@d2 If [their punishment is to be] by burning then remember Avraham who offered himself to be burnt for Your sake in Ur Kasdim. If a chair with three legs cannot stand before You at the time of Your anger [then this is] certainly [true] of a chair with one leg.<QC>
@d1To whom You swore by Your self.@d2 You did not swear to them by something that is finite — neither by the heavens, nor by the earth, neither by the mountains, nor the hills, but only by Your Self. Who is eternal, and Your oath remains forever, as it is said: “‘By My Self I have sworn,’ says God.” To Yitzchok it was said: “I will establish the [same] oath that I swore to Avraham, your father.” And to Yaakov it was said: “I am God Almighty, be fruitful and multiply.” Thus He swore to him by “God Almighty.” @p115@p2On both sides —@p3 were the letters readable, and it was a work of miracles. @p116@p2They were the work of God.@p3 As it implies: He (God) Himself in His Glory made them. An alternate explanation: It is analogous to a person who tells his friend, “All the occupation of so-and-so is with that particular work.” So too is all the preoccupation of God with the Torah. @d1Engraved.@d2 The words @hh<HEB>חָרֹת@ee<ENG> and @hh<HEB>חָרֹט@ee<ENG> are the same, both being terms for engraving, @ITenteillier@it in Old French.<QC>
@p117@p2Rejoicing.@p3 [Meaning:] trumpeting — for they were blowing horns, rejoicing and laughing. @p118@p2It is not the sound of strength.@p3 This sound seems neither the shout of the mighty, who cry out, “Victory”! nor the sound of the weak who cry out, “Woe”! or, “Let us flee”! @d1Beneath the mountain.@d2 [I.e.,] at the foot of the mountain. @p120@p2He scattered it.@p3 A term meaning scattering. Similarly “(@hh<HEB>יְזוֹרֶה@ee<ENG>) there shall be scattered on his dwelling place, brimstone.” And similarly: “For naught (@hh<HEB>מְזוֹרָה@ee<ENG>) is the net spread out” [so called] because grain and beans are scattered in it. @d1And made the B’nei Yisrael drink it.@d2 He intended to test them [in the same manner] as are women suspected of adultery. Three different death penalties were inflicted there: [1.] if there were witnesses and a warning [to the sinner] — by the sword, the same as an idolatrous city, when there are many involved. [2.] If there were witnesses but no warning — by the plague, as it is said: “God struck the people with a plague.” [3.] If there were no witnesses and no warning — by swelling up, for the water tested them and their stomachs swelled up. @p121@p2What did this people do to you.@p3 How much pain did you endure that they tortured you before you brought this sin on them? @p122@p2That [the people] are set on evil.@p3 They are always going on an evil way with temptations before God. @p124@p2I said to them.@p3 I said one thing to them — only: “Who has gold”? and they hurriedly removed it and gave it to me. @d1I threw it into the fire —@d2 and I did not know that this calf would come out and it @ITdid@it come out. @p125@p2Exposed.@p3 [Meaning:] exposed — its shame and disgrace were exposed, as in: “(@hh<HEB>וּפָרַע@ee<ENG>) He shall expose the head of the woman.” @d1Disgraced among their adversaries.@d2 [Meaning:] that this matter shall be a disgrace for them before all who rise up against them. @p126@p2“Whoever is for Adonoy, to me”!@p3 I.e., “Come to me.” @d1All the sons of Leivi.@d2 From here it is derived that the entire Tribe was righteous.<QC>

@p130@p2I will gain atonement for your sin.@p3 [I.e.] “I shall put a @ITcleansing, wiping away@it and a @ITcovering@it in front of your sins, to separate between you and the sin. @p131@p2A god of gold.@p3 “You (God) are the One who caused this to happen to them for You gave them an abundance of gold and anything they desired. A parable comparable to a king, who fed and gave drink to his son and adorned him and hung a bag [full of money] from his neck and made him stand in the doorway of a house of ill-repute; how could the son possibly not sin?<QC>
@p132@p2Now, if You would bear their sin —@p3 then well and good — I will not say to You, “Erase me,” but if not, erase me [from Your Book.] This is an elliptical verse and there are many such cases. @d1From Your Book.@d2 [I.e.] from the entire Torah, so that it should not be said of me that I was not worthy enough to ask for mercy on their behalf. @p134@p2To [the place] of which I have spoken to you.@p3 Here is an instance where @hh<HEB>לָךְ@ee<ENG> is used connected with the verb @hh<HEB>דִבּוּר@ee<ENG> in the place of @hh<HEB>אֵלֶיךָ@ee<ENG>. Onkelos translates it @hh<HEB>וּמִתְמַלֵּל עִם משֶׁה@ee<ENG> which is respect for the Shechinah, as in: “He heard the voice @hh<HEB>מִדַבֵּר@ee<ENG> to him” and it does not read @hh<HEB>מְדַבֵּר אֵלָיו@ee<ENG>. Because when it reads @hh<HEB>מִדַּבֵּר@ee<ENG> its interpretation is: the Voice speaking to itself which the ordinary person happens to overhear, but when it reads @hh<HEB>מְדַבֵּר@ee<ENG> it implies that the king is speaking with the ordinary person. @p110@p2Prostrated themselves —@p3 to the @ITShechinah.@it @p111@p2Adonoy spoke to Moshe face to face.@p3 The correct reading of Onkelos is @hh<HEB>וּמִתְמַלֵּל עִם משֶׁה@ee<ENG>. @d1He returned to the camp —@d2 after [God’s] conversing with him Moshe would return to the encampment and teach the elders what he had learned. Moshe acted in this way from Yom Kippur until the @ITmishkon@it was erected but not longer. For on the 17th of Tammuz the tablets were broken and on the 18th he burned the Calf and judged the sinners and on the nineteenth he went up [the mountain], as it says: “The next day Moshe said to the people, etc.” and spent forty days there and asked for mercy [for the B’nei Yisrael], “and he would return to the encampment,” because it is in the present (ongoing) tense. The same applies to this whole section: @hh<HEB>וְרָאָה כָל הָעָם@ee<ENG> (v. 10) is @hh<HEB>וַחֲזַן@ee<ENG> — “would see,” @hh<HEB>וְנִצָבוּ@ee<ENG> (v. 8) is @hh<HEB>וִקַיְימִין@ee<ENG> — “they would rise,” @hh<HEB>וְהִבִּיטוּ@ee<ENG> (ibid.) is @hh<HEB>וּמִסְתַּכְּלִין@ee<ENG> — “they would gaze,” @hh<HEB>וְהִשְׁתַּחֲווּ@ee<ENG> (v. 10) is @hh<HEB>וְסָגְדִין@ee<ENG> — “they would bow.” But its midrashic explanation is: @d1God spoke to Moshe@d2 that he must return to the encampment; he said to him: “I am angry and you are angry; if so, who will again bring them near to me.” @p112@p2Behold You say to me.@p3 @hh<HEB>רְאֵה@ee<ENG> (lit. “see”) means: Set Your “eyes” and Your “heart” on Your words. You say to me, etc. [“lead this people up”] but You did not let me know, etc. [whom You would send with me]. And this that You said to me: “Behold, I am sending an angel” is not considered letting me know for I do not wish it [to happen]. @d1You [also] said, “I know you by name.”@d2 [Saying to me, in effect:] “I have recognized you from among other people ‘by name’ — i.e., to recognize your importance,” for You said to me [to indicate this importance]: “Behold I come to you in the thickness of the cloud, etc. and also in you will they believe forever.” @p113@p2Now —@p3 if it is so that I have found favor in Your eyes please let me know Your ways — i.e., what reward do You give to those who find favor in Your eyes — @d1so that I will know You that I may find favor in Your eyes.@d2 And through this (i.e., knowing Your ways) the degree to which You give reward — what is this “finding of favor” which I have found in Your eyes. Hence the interpretation of @hh<HEB>לְמַעַן אֶמְצָא חֵן@ee<ENG> is: so that I may appreciate the extent of the reward for “finding favor.”<QC>
@d1And consider that this nation is Your people —@d2 so that You not say: “And I will make you into a great nation,” while You abandon these [the people]; consider that they are Your people of yore, and if You become contemptuous of them I could not be certain that those who descend from me will endure. Our Sages, however, give midrashic interpretations of it in Tractate Berachos, but my purpose is to explain the verses in their context and order. @p114@p2He [God] said, “My Presence will go [with you].@p3 It is as Onkelos translates it: [@hh<HEB>שְׁכִנְתִּי תְהַךְ@ee<ENG> — “My Shechinah will go,” meaning:] “I will no longer send an angel, I, Myself, will go,” as in: “(@hh<HEB>וּפָנֶיךָ@ee<ENG>) You, yourself, will go into battle.” @p115@p2He (Moshe) said to him —@p3 “that is exactly what I wish that You not bring us up from here through an angel.”<QC>
@p116@p2And how will it be known.@p3 The finding of favor will be known only by Your Self going with us. But I ask one more thing of You that You no longer allow Your Shechinah to rest on the nations of the world. @d1So that I and your people will be distinguished.@d2 I.e., through this we will be set apart from all other people, etc., as in: “God (@hh<HEB>הִפְלָה@ee<ENG>) distinguished between the cattle of Israel etc.” @p117@p2Also this thing —@p3 that My @ITShechinah@it will no longer rest upon the nations of the world — I will do. But the [prophetical] words of Bilaam were not by means of the resting of the Shechinah [on him], but, rather, “when fallen (i.e., asleep) but with the eyes uncovered.” For example: “To me a word was stolen” — they (the prophets of the other nations) heard through an intermediary. @p118@p2He (Moshe) then said. “Please grant me a vision of Your Glory.”@p3 Moshe saw that it was a favorable moment and that his words were finding acceptance. So he made an additional request to be shown a vision of His Glory. @p119@p2He (God) said, “I will cause all My goodness to pass, etc.”@p3 I.e., “The time has come for you to see of My Glory, that which I will allow you to see, because I want and need to teach you the order of prayer. For, when you needed to ask for mercy for the Israelites, You reminded Me of the merit of the Patriarchs. You thought that were the merit of the Patriarchs to be exhausted there would no longer be any hope. I will, therefore, cause to pass all the attributes of My goodness before you on the rock while you are concealed in the cave. @d1And will proclaim the Name, Adonoy, in your presence —@d2 so as to teach you the order of asking for mercy even if the merit of the Patriarchs is exhausted. Gracious One” they will be answered, for My mercies are never exhausted. @d1I will be gracious to whom I will be gracious.@d2 [I.e.,] those times that I will wish to be gracious. @d1And I will be compassionate —@d2 those times that I will wish to be compassionate. Up to this point He only promised him that “at times I will respond, at times I will not respond.” But at that time [when He actually proclaimed His attributes,] He said to him: “Behold, I establish a covenant,” thus promising him [that once the Attributes are invoked] they will never return unanswered (lit. empty). @p120@p2And He said, “You cannot,” etc.@p3 Even when I cause all My goodness to pass before you, I shall not permit you to see My Presence. @p121@p2“Behold, there is a place with Me.”@p3 In the mountain, where I constantly speak with you, I have a place prepared for your needs, where I will conceal you so that you will not be hurt, and from there you will see what you may see. That is the plain explanation. However, its midrashic explanation is that [“a place”] refers to the place where the Shechinah is and He, therefore, says: “The place is with Me” and He does not say: “I am in the place,” for God is “the place of the world” but the world is not “His place.” @p122@p2When My Glory passes by.@p3 [Meaning:] When I pass by you.<QC>
@d1In a cleft of the rock.@d2 [The word @hh<HEB>בְּנִקְרַת@ee<ENG> expresses the idea of a hole] as in: “Will you (@hh<HEB>תְּנַקֵּר@ee<ENG>) bore out the eyes of those men”? [or:] “The ravens of the valley (@hh<HEB>יִקְּרוּהָ@ee<ENG>) will bore it out” [or:] “I have dug and drunk water”; They all have a common root. Therefore @hh<HEB>נִקְרַת צוּר@ee<ENG> means “a place dug out in the rock.” @d1And I will cover [you] with My Hand.@d2 From here it may be derived permission is given to destroyers to destroy. Onkelos translates it (i.e., @hh<HEB>וְשַׂכֹּתִי כַפִּי@ee<ENG>) @hh<HEB>וְאָגֵין בְּמֵימְרִי@ee<ENG> — “I will protect you with My word.” This is a euphemism as a way [of explaining] respectfulness towards the One on High, Who, [obviously] does not need to cover him actually, with His Hand. @p123@p2Then I will remove My Hand.@p3 [Onkelos translates it:] @hh<HEB>וְאַעְדֵי יָת דִּבְרַת יְקָרִי@ee<ENG> — “I shall remove the guidance of My Glory,” [Meaning:] “When I remove the guidance of My Glory from before your face” — to go away from there [then —] @d1you will see My Back.@d2 He showed him the @ITtephillin@it-knot [behind the Head]. @p11@p2Carve out for yourself.@p3@d2 He (God) showed him a quarry of precious stone from within his tent and said to him: The [@hh<HEB>פְּסוֹלֶת@ee<ENG> — from @hh<HEB>פְּסָל@ee<ENG>] chips shall be [@hh<HEB>שֶׁלְךָ@ee<ENG> — from @hh<HEB>לְךָ@ee<ENG>] yours. From there Moshe became extremely wealthy. @d1Carve out for yourself.@d2 You broke the First [Tablets], you carve out others for yourself. A parable: [this is comparable] to a king who went off to a faraway land and left his betrothed with the maidservants. As a result of the immoral behavior of the maidservants her (the betrothed’s) reputation was besmirched. Her bridesman arose and tore up her marriage contract, saying: “If the king will decide to kill her I will tell him: ‘She is not yet your wife’.” [Consequently] the king investigated and found that the immoral behavior was only on the part of the maidservants. He was reconciled with her, whereupon the bridesman said to him (the king): “Write her another marriage contract for the first one was torn up.” The king replied to him: “You are the one who tore it up. You buy other paper for yourself and I will write it for her in my handwriting.” Here, too, the king is God, the maidservants are the riffraff, the bridesman is Moshe, God’s betrothed is Yisrael. Therefore it says: “Carve out for yourself.” @p12@p2Be ready.@p3 [Meaning:] prepared.<QC>

@p17@p2He preserves kindness.@p3 [I.e., the kindness] which the person does before Him. @d1For thousands.@d2 [Meaning:] for two thousand generations. @d1[Iniquity, transgression.]@d2 @hh<HEB>עֲוֹנוֹת@ee<ENG> are sins committed knowingly; @hh<HEB>פְּשָׁעִים@ee<ENG> are sins committed for the sake of rebellion, which a person does out of spite. @d1He clears and He does not clear.@d2 According to its plain meaning it conveys that He does not completely waive any sin but exacts retribution for it bit by bit. @p121@p2You must cease plowing and harvesting.@p3 Why are plowing and harvesting [the only ones] mentioned? Some of our Sages say [that this refers] to the plowing of the year before the @ITsheviis@it year which leads into the @ITsheviis@it year, and the harvest of the @ITsheviis@it year which extends into the post-@ITsheviis@it year, so as to teach you that a part of the secular [year] must be added to the holy [year] (i.e., the @ITsheviis@it year). Accordingly this is the meaning of the verse: “Six days you shall work but on the Seventh Day you must cease. But, of the six days’ work which I permitted to you there is a year in which plowing and harvesting are prohibited. There is no need to state [the prohibition of] plowing and harvesting of the Seventh Year for it has already been stated: “Your field you may not sow, etc.” However others [of the Sages] say that this [verse] speaks only of Shabbos, and [the fact that only] plowing and harvesting are mentioned [is] to teach you that just as the [prohibited] plowing is optional plowing so too is the [prohibited] harvesting optional harvesting, which excludes the harvesting of the @ITomer@it which is obligatory and takes precedence over Shabbos.<QC>

@d1That...had become radiant.@d2 @hh<HEB>קָרַן@ee<ENG> is a term having a common root with @hh<HEB>קַרְנַיִם @ee<ENG> (horns) because light shines out and projects as a sort of “horn.” From where was Moshe privileged to these rays of splendor? Thus this entire subject is in the present (ongoing) tense. @p132@p2After that, they came close.@p3 [I.e.], after he taught the elders he would again teach that section or that law to all Yisrael. The Sages have taught: What was the order of the teaching of the Torah? Moshe would learn from the Almighty. Then Aharon would enter and Moshe would teach him his chapter. Aharon moved away and sat on Moshe’s left. Whereupon his (Aharon’s) sons would enter and Moshe would teach them their chapter. They then moved away and Elazar sat on Moshe’s right and Isamar on Aharon’s left. Whereupon the elders would enter and Moshe would teach them their chapter. The elders moved away and sat on the sides. Whereupon the entire people would enter and Moshe taught them their chapter. Consequently the lesson came into the possession of the people once into the possession of the elders, twice, into the possession of Aharon’s sons, thrice, and into the hands of Aharon, four times, etc., as it is all cited in Maseches Eiruvin.<QC>
@p133@p2He placed a cover over his face.@p3 [@hh<HEB>מַסְוֶה@ee<ENG> is] as Onkelos translates it: “a face cover”; it (@hh<HEB>מַסְוֶה@ee<ENG>) is an Aramaic word [meaning “to look”]. It is found in the Gemara: “Her heart (@hh<HEB>סְוִי@ee<ENG>) saw.” Also in @ITKesuvos@it (60a): @hh<HEB>הֲוָה קָא מַסְוֶה לְאַפָּהּ@ee<ENG> where it has the meaning of “looking,” i.e., he gazed at her. Here, too, @hh<HEB>מַסְוֶה@ee<ENG> refers to a cloth placed in front of the face and the area of the eyes. And out of respect to the “rays of splendor” so that they should not all become satiated by the sight, he would place the cover in front of them (his eyes). He would remove it when speaking to the Israelites and when God was speaking with him until he went out. And when he went out [to teach God’s  words] he would go out without the cover — @p134@p2and he would speak to the B’nei Yisrael —@p3 and they would see the “rays of splendor” in his face. And when he would leave them —<QC>
@p135@p2Moshe would replace the cover over his face until he would come to speak to Him.@p3 And when he came to speak with Him he would remove it from his face.<QC>

@p11@p2And Betzalel made.@p3 Because he extended himself for the work more than the other wise men, it (the work) bears his name. @p17@p2Hollow boards.@p3 @hh<HEB>נְבוּב@ee<ENG> means “hollow.” Similarly: “Its thickness was four fingers (@hh<HEB>נְבוּב@ee<ENG>) @IT hollow.”@it @d1Hollow boards.@d2 The boards of acacia wood were on all sides [thereby creating] the hollow in the middle. @p18@p2From the mirrors of the women who had gathered.@p3 The daughters of Israel possessed mirrors into which they would look when adorning themselves. Even those they did not withhold from bringing as a contribution for the @ITmishkon.@it However, Moshe found them repulsive since their purpose is to incite the evil inclination. God said to him: Accept [them], for these are dearer to me than everything else because through them the women raised huge multitudes in Egypt. When their husbands were exhausted from their crushing labor they (the women) would go and bring them food and drink and feed them. They would then take the mirrors and each one would look at herself and her husband in the mirror, and entice him with words, saying, “See! I am more beautiful than you,” thereby awakening their husbands’ desire and they would cohabit with them. They conceived and gave birth there. As is stated: “Under the apple-tree did I arouse you.” That is the intent of what is said: “By the mirrors of the @hh<HEB>צֹבְאוֹת@ee<ENG>.” The wash-basin was made out of them for it serves to bring about peace between a man and his wife, by giving to drink from the waters inside it to anyone whose husband expressed jealousy and she, nevertheless, concealed herself [with another man]. You can know yourself that these were actual mirrors, for it is stated: “The total copper donation was seventy @ITkikar,@it etc. and he made from it, etc.” whereas the wash-basin and its base are not mentioned there. Hence you have learned that the copper of the wash-basin was not from the donated copper. Thus did Rabbi Tanchuma explain it. Onkelos also translates it this way: @hh<HEB>בְמֶחְזְיַת@ee<ENG> of the women [@hh<HEB>בְּמֶחְזְיַת@ee<ENG>] being the Aramaic for “mirrors,” @ITmireors@it in Old French. @p121@p2These are the accounts.@p3 In this @ITparsha@it there are enumerated all the weights of the contributions to the @ITmishkon@it — of the silver, the gold and the copper. Also all its vessels are enumerated each with its function. @d1The @BImishkon,@BO the @BImishkon.@d2 “@hh<HEB>מִשְׁכָּן@ee<ENG>” is mentioned twice. This is an allusion to the @ITBeis Hamikdosh@it (@hh<HEB>שֶׁנִתְמַשְׁכֵּן@ee<ENG>) which was taken as “collateral” by being destroyed twice because of the iniquities of Yisrael. @d1The @ITmishkon@it of Testimony.@d2 So-called because it bears witness for Yisrael that God was indulgent to them for the deed of the [Golden] Calf, for [through the @ITmishkon@it] He rested His @ITShechinah@it among them. @d1The work of the Levites.@d2 Those things that were counted of the @ITmishkon@it and its vessels was the work entrusted to the Levites in the desert  — to carry, to disassemble, and to set it up, each person according to the burden assigned to him, as is related in @ITparshas@it Naso. @d1Under the direction of Isamar.@d2 He was in charge over them, to entrust each paternal family the work for which it is responsible. @p122@p2Betzalel, son of Uri, etc. made all that Adonoy commanded Moshe.@p3 “That which he (Moshe) commanded him (Betzalel) is not written here, but, rather, “all that God commanded Moshe,” [thereby implying that] even things which his teacher (Moshe) had not told him, his own opinion was in agreement with what was said to Moshe at Sinai. For Moshe had instructed Betzalel to first produce the vessels, and afterward the @ITmishkon.@it Whereupon Betzalel said to him: “It is customary in the world to first construct a house and afterwards put furnishings in it.” [Moshe] responded to him: “[You are right] that is indeed what I heard from God.” Moshe [then also] said to him “You must have been (@hh<HEB>בְּצֵל אֵ־ל@ee<ENG>) in the shadow of God, for, certainly, so did God command me.” And so he (Betzalel) did it — first the @ITmishkon@it and afterwards he made the vessels. @p124@p2Kikar.@p3 A @ITkikar@it is the equivalent of 60 @ITmaneh@it and the @ITmaneh@it associated with the Sanctuary was double [the weight of a common @ITmaneh@it] making the @ITkikar@it equal to 120 [common]@it @ITmaneh,@it A @ITmaneh@it is equal to 25 @ITselaim@it (@ITshekalim@it), making the @ITkikar@it of the Sanctuary 3,000 @ITshekalim@it (25 x 120 = 3,000). It is for this reason that it counts as units all the @ITshekalim@it that add up to less than 3,000, because they do not add up to a @ITkikar.@it<QC>
@p126@p2Beka.@p3 This is the name of the weight of one half of a shekel. @d1For six hundred (and three) thousand, etc.@d2 This was the count of the Israelites and this, too, was what their number amounted to after the @ITmishkon@it was erected [when they were counted again] in @ITseifer@it Bamidbar. Now, too, at the time of the contributions to the @ITmishkon@it they were that many. The amount of half-@ITshekalim@it for 600,000 totalled 100 @ITkikar,@it where each @ITkikar@it is 3,000 @ITshekalim.@it How is this calculated? 600,000 half-@ITshekels,@it which equal 300,000 whole @ITshekalim@it giving you 100 @ITkikar@it (100 x 3,000 = 300,000). The remaining 3,550 half-@ITshekalim@it total 1,775 whole @ITshekalim.@it @p127@p2To cast.@p3 [@hh<HEB>לָצֶקֶת@ee<ENG> is] as Onkelos translates it: “to cast.” @d1The sockets for the Sanctuary. —@d2 for the planks of the @ITmishkon,@it which totalled 48 planks for which there were 96 sockets, plus the 4 sockets for the curtain giving you a total of 100 [gold sockets]. @p11@p2From the greenish-blue wool, dark red wool, etc.@p3 Linen is not mentioned here. And it is from here that I derive that these @hh<HEB>בִּגְדֵי שְׂרָד@ee<ENG> are not the priestly garments for the priestly garments @ITdid@it contain linen. But, rather, they are “garments” with which the vessels of the Sanctuary were covered when it was disassembled for travelling, which contained no linen.<QC>
@p13@p2They beat.@p3 As in: “(@hh<HEB>לְרוֹקַע@ee<ENG>) He who spreads the earth,” and as Onkelos translates it: “they hammered out.” They hammered out plates from the gold. @ITEstendre@it in Old French — “to hammer out into thin plates.” Here [the Torah] teaches you how they spun the gold with the threads: they hammered out thin plates from which they would cut threads along the length of the plate, to make threads [which were] combined with each kind [of material thread] separately for the breastplate and the @ITeiphod@it whenever “gold” is stated; one thread of gold with six threads of greenish-blue wool and so, too, with each of the other kinds, for the strand of each of the kinds was spun six-fold, and the gold was the seventh thread with each.<QC>
@p128@p2And the splendor of the fine linen turban-hats.@p3 [@hh<HEB>פַּאֲרֵי הַמִּגְבָּעוֹת@ee<ENG> is the same as] @hh<HEB>תִּפְאֶרֶת הַמִּגְבָּעוֹת@ee<ENG> — “the splendor of the turbans” [meaning:] the splendid turbans. @p131@p2To fasten it to the turban from above —@p3 by means of the threads he set it onto the turban, as a sort of crown. It is impossible to say [however] that the @hh<HEB>צִיץ@ee<ENG> [proper] was on the turban for in Tractate Shechitas Kodoshim (Zevachim) we have learned that his hair was visible between the @hh<HEB>צִיץ@ee<ENG> and the turban which was where he wore his @ITtefillin@it and the @hh<HEB>צִיץ@ee<ENG> was placed on the forehead. Thus you see that the turban was high up and the @hh<HEB>צִיץ@ee<ENG> lower down. Then what is the explanation of: “turban from above”? Another question that I have posed in this matter: Here it states, “They put (fastened) on it a greenish-blue wool cord,” and in the section where the command is given it states: “place @ITit@it on a greenish-blue cord.” [To solve these seeming contradictions] I say: This greenish-blue wool thread is actually strands with which to attach it (the @hh<HEB>צִיץ@ee<ENG>) to the turban. For the @hh<HEB>צִיץ@ee<ENG> only runs from ear to ear and how is it to be fastened to his forehead? There were strands of greenish-blue wool inserted into it at its two ends and at its middle by means of which he ties it and hangs it from the turban when it is on his head. There were two strands at each end (of the @hh<HEB>צִיץ@ee<ENG>) one on the outer side and one underneath towards his forehead and likewise at its middle for this makes it easier to tie and it is not usual to tie something with less than two strands. This is the reason it says: “on [a] greenish-blue thread,” and “on it [the @hh<HEB>צִיץ@ee<ENG>] [was a] greenish-blue thread.” He now ties the two ends of each of all [the threads] together, behind him opposite his neck, and now places it on the turban. Do not wonder that it does not say: “@ITthreads@it of greenish-blue wool” since there were many, for we find [a similar syntax] regarding the breastplate and the @ITeiphod@it: “They shall fasten the breastplate, etc.” and you are certainly forced to admit that there were no less than two [strands] for at the two corners of the breastplate there were the two rings of the breastplate and in the two shoulder-straps of the @ITeiphod@it were the two rings of the @ITeiphod@it opposite them (i.e., the rings of the breastplate). And the way it (the breastplate) was tied there were four strands. But under no circumstances could there have been less than two. @p132@p2B’nei Yisrael did —@p3 the work, according to all that God commanded, etc.<QC>
@p133@p2They brought the @BImishkon,@BO etc. —@p3 for they were unable to erect it. Because Moshe did no work whatsoever in the @ITmishkon@it God left it for him to erect, for no man was able to erect it because of the weight of the planks for no man had the strength to stand them up, but Moshe stood them up. [Actually, Moshe himself could also not do it but...] Moshe said to God: How is it possible for man to erect it? [Whereupon] He answered him: “You busy yourself with your hand”! It appeared as if he (Moshe) had erected it but it had stood upright of its own. This is what is meant by what is said: “The @ITmishkon@it was erected” — it was erected of its own. From the Midrash of Rabbi Tanchuma.<QC>

On that very day Moshe was sanctified with them. @p132@p2And when they approached.@p3 @hh<HEB>וּבְקָרְבָתָם@ee<ENG> is the same as @hh<HEB>וּבְקָרְבָם@ee<ENG> — @ITwhen they would approach.@it @p135@p2Moshe was unable to enter the Tent of Meeting.@p3 Yet, another verse states: “And when Moshe would come into the Tent of Meeting” There comes a third verse [later in our verse] and reconciles them: “for the cloud was upon it (on the Tent of meetings)” Say, therefore: as long as the cloud was upon it he could not come in; once the cloud left he would enter and speak with Him. @p138@p2Before the eyes of the entire Beis Yisrael in all their travels.@p3 At every station that they stopped the cloud would rest at the place where they encamped. Their encampment is also called @hh<HEB>מַסָּע@ee<ENG> Similarly: “He went to his (@hh<HEB>לְמַסָּעָיו@ee<ENG>) resting places.” Similarly: “These were (@hh<HEB>מַסְעֵי@ee<ENG>) the stations.” Because from the place of encampment they again journeyed, therefore, they are called @hh<HEB>מַסָּעוֹת@ee<ENG>.<QC>
